# WE, THE DROWNED CHORUS

*A town of the dead narrates its own forgetting.*

A novel

Daily original-novel run — 2026-07-02

---

# Chapter 1 — Preia-mar

The car comes in at the failing of the light, along the road that runs past the salt-pans, and we know the sound of it before we know the woman, because we are dead and sound is what we have. It is a small engine, a rented one, tuned to nothing, running rich. It crosses the flats where the water is coming up over the black mud the way it comes up twice a day, without being asked, and the tide takes the diesel-smell of her out toward the Barra and does not bring it back. She slows. She has been driving with the window down. We are forty in the roof of the *fábrica*, or we are less, and all of us lean toward the road at once, and by leaning we can nearly make her whole — the shape of her hands on the wheel, the tiredness in her shoulders, the way she has not eaten since Coimbra. We do not know she has not eaten since Coimbra. We decide it. When there are enough of us to lean, we can decide a great many things about the living and be right about most, and no one is left to tell us which ones.

She parks in the yard where the boats used to bring the sardines up in baskets, before the boats, before the baskets, before us. She does not get out. She sits with the engine ticking, and she looks at the building that is ours — the long shed, the tin roof gone the colour of old blood, the chimney standing over the flats the way a thing stands that has forgotten it was ever for anything. We know the chimney. Some of us fed it. The name for the smoke that used to come off it is on the tip of the whole of us, and then it is not, and we let it go and pretend we had not reached for it.

Aurélia says it is her. Aurélia has the sharpest edge of us still; she says it the way she used to say things on the floor, from the front of the room, and the rest of us feel ourselves turn to it. *It is the girl.* Not a girl. The girl. Idalina, of the line, the last of the line, come home the way the water comes home, and Aurélia's voice is warm around the word *home*, warm the way brine is warm when it has stood all day in the sun and will still take the skin off you. Graça is here too, nearer the front than she was a year ago, nearer the living than any of us, and Graça does not say anything. Graça looks at her daughter through the windscreen and something goes out of her, some brightness we can none of us afford, and we do not name it, because to name it would be to spend it.

The woman gets out of the car.

She stands a moment in the yard in a jacket too thin for the *nortada* coming in off the sea, and she looks at us — at the building, she looks at the building, but we take it as at us, because we have not been looked at in so long. Then she leans back into the car and takes out a machine.

We do not like the machine.

It is black and small and it has a fur over one end of it, a grey fur like something drowned, and she carries it out into the middle of the yard and holds it up on a little pole and she stands very still. She is listening. We know listening; it is the one thing of the living we understand from the inside. But she is not listening the way we listen — to hold, to keep, to say it again so it stays. She is listening so the machine can do it for her. The machine takes the sound of the wind in the chimney, and the tin sheet on the north side lifting and dropping, lifting and dropping, and the tide seething up through the reeds at the yard's edge, and the far bell of the church at Ílhavo, and it takes them the way she cannot make us take her — completely, at once, and then it is done with them. It holds them so she need not. It listens so she need not remember. Some of us have already forgotten why we came into this room, and she stands out there in the yard giving the sound of our house to a machine so that she can carry our house away in her pocket and never think of it again, and this is the most obscene thing we have seen in a long time, and we cannot look away from it.

*She is recording us,* one of us says, one of the flu-dead, one of the many.

*She cannot record us,* says Aurélia. *There is nothing of us to record.* And this is true, and it is the worst thing anyone has said, and we go quiet, all of us, the way the floor went quiet when the *sirene* stopped mid-shift and everyone understood there had been a death in the tanks. We are not on the tape. We will never be on the tape. She could stand in the yard for a hundred years with her little machine and take everything, the wind and the tin and the bell and the water, and we would not be in any of it, because the machine only keeps what makes a sound, and we do not make a sound she can keep. This is the thing we understand about the machine before we understand anything else about the woman: it is doing, easily, in the yard, the thing we cannot do at all.

The tape runs. She watches a small green light on the side of it. Behind her the light goes out of the sky over the Barra, the flat silver light this town has instead of a sunset, and the *nevoeiro* is coming up off the sea already, low, the fog that smells of nothing and swallows sound, and we watch it come toward her across the flats and we want to tell her, *go in, it will take your voice, it takes everything's voice,* but we have no way to tell her anything. We can only make her mislay a thing, or mishear a thing. We are saving that. Some of us are already saving that, though it is the first evening and she has not yet done anything to be kept for.

She lowers the pole. She has enough. Whatever she came out here to take, she has taken it, and she winds the fur-covered end down and lays the machine across the passenger seat as though it were a person, careful, a strap over it. Then she goes to the boot and takes out one bag, only one, and this we all mark: she has come to a house of her dead with one bag, the way you pack when you mean to be gone before the sheets are cold. Three days, we think. She thinks it too; we can nearly hear her think it, the shape of it in the set of her jaw. *Sign, sell, gone.* We think we have read her last page. We have not read her last page. We do not know this yet. It is the beginning of the not-knowing.

She looks up at the roof one more time, at the dark under the eaves where the beams cross, and for a moment — for the length of a wave coming up the channel — the whole of us is looking down at her looking up, forty of us or less, packed into the black wedge of the roof where the roof meets the wall, and there is a shoe up here in the beams that none of us mentions. It has been up there a long time. A child's shoe, small, the leather gone hard and grey. It is caught in the join of two beams where the water threw it, or where a hand threw it, in the year the water was high, and one of us knew whose it was this morning. We had it this morning. We say the name to ourselves now, up in the dark, while she is still looking up, so as to have it ready — and it is a boy's name, it is Zé, it is Zé's shoe, Zé who — Zé who was small, who is one of ours, who is here somewhere in the pack of us though we do not feel him lean toward the woman the way the rest of us lean. Custódia will know. Custódia has the other half of him. We do not ask Custódia. It is early yet, and the shoe is where it has always been, and we let the name sit in us the way a stone sits in silt, and the woman lowers her eyes from the roof and does not see the shoe, and gets in the car, and drives the two hundred metres to the café where she will sleep, and the tide comes up another hand's width in the yard behind her and lifts the loose tin and drops it, lifts it, drops it.

*

The room she takes is over the café, on the square, and this is nearly the far edge of what we can reach — the water does not run under the square, and where the water does not run we are thin, we are a rumour of ourselves, we catch her in pieces. We have her going up the outside stair. We have the light coming on behind the shutter. We have the machine set down on the little table by the window, and — this we have clearly, because it is the machine and we cannot help but attend to it — we have her plugging it in to feed, its green light going to red, drinking, the way our *sirene* used to drink the whole town's night. She feeds the machine before she feeds herself. She does not feed herself. She sits on the edge of the bed in the town that is supposed to be hers, in the room over the café where her mother must have bought her *ovos moles* as a child in those little barrel-boxes, and she does not weep, and she does not sleep.

Before the light goes out she does one thing more, and Graça has it and hands it back to us in pieces, the way she hands us everything from the edge where it costs her. The girl gets up off the bed and goes to the little table by the window and lifts the machine off its feeding to be sure of the red light, the drinking-light, and while she has it in her hands she does not set it straight back down. She holds it a moment against her the way you hold a thing you have carried a long way. Then she reaches into the bag on the floor and takes out a photograph — one, only one, she has come to a house of her dead with one bag and one photograph the way she has come with one machine — and she props it against the base of the lamp so that it stands. We cannot see the photograph. We are at the thin edge over the dry square and a photograph is a pale place with a darker place on it and no more, and Graça, who is nearest, will not tell us whose face is in it or whether it is a face at all, only that the girl looks at it the way she looked at the building in the yard, from the outside, from a great way off, as at a thing that is supposed to be hers and is not. She looks at it and does not touch it again. And there is a thing she does over the propped photograph that Graça gives us and that the rest of us cannot hold, that goes past us like the smoke-name went: she says something. Her mouth moves in the lamplight over the little standing card, once, a short word, two words, the way you say a thing to a face that cannot answer — and we would give a great deal to have it, we who say the dead along the beams to keep them, we who know exactly what it is to say a name into a room and have the room not say it back. But the square is dry and the word is small and it does not carry to us across the water we do not have, and whatever name she said over the photograph in the room over the café stays in the room, unheard, uncaught, not even on her own machine, which is feeding on the wall and not listening to her, and we are left with only the moving of her mouth and no sound in it, which is the shape of everything we cannot do, done by the living, easily, in the dark. She feeds the machine and does not feed herself. She takes out a telephone and holds it and does not use it, and we cannot see what is on her face, because the faces are the last thing we can see and here, at the edge, over the dry square, we cannot see it at all.

Graça can see it. Graça is nearest her daughter of any of us and Graça alone, out at the thin edge of us where it costs her the most to be, keeps her eyes on the girl's face and reads there something the rest of us are told secondhand and do not fully believe: that the girl is not thinking of us at all. Not of the family, not of the greatness, not of the drowned. She is thinking of a street in Lisbon, of a room with soft panels on the walls where sound goes to be tamed, of a life that is small and real and hers and that she has left running like a tap while she comes here to turn off the tap of us. She wants to go home. This is home, we say — Aurélia says it, and the many of us say it after her, *this is home, this is home* — and Graça, at the edge, does not say it, and we do not notice that Graça does not say it, and the girl on the bed lets the propped photograph stand where it is and puts the telephone down without using it and lies back in her clothes and the light behind the shutter goes out.

*

In the dark, with the living woman asleep or not asleep across the square, we do the thing we do. We count ourselves.

It is an old thing, from the floor. On the floor you counted heads at the start of the shift, the *encarregada* counted, so as to know who had come and who the *sirene* had not woken and who was in the tanks. We count now the way we always count, going along the beams, and it is a comfort, it is the wet-sand comfort of saying the true thing again so it stays true. We are all here. Every one of us. We say it grandly, from the front, in Aurélia's voice which is the grandest we have: *we are all here, every one of us, not one of us gone out to the bar, we are forty in the roof.*

And then some of us — not many, a few, the honest few at the back where the roof comes down to nothing — some of us count again, quietly, so as not to spoil it, and get thirty-eight. And some of us count and get forty-one, because we count Graça twice, because Graça is at the edge and her voice comes from two places, from the roof and from over the square, and we are not sure whether the edge of her that is out watching the girl is a separate one of us or the same one stretched thin. We do not resolve it. There is no one to resolve it. We say forty because forty is the number we have always said, the number from the good years when the roof was packed and the water was high and the whole of us leaned together and saw the living whole; and thirty-eight is a number we do not say, and forty-one is a number we do not say, and the true number is a thing the tide has, out at the bar, with the diesel-smell of her arriving, and it does not bring it back.

We tamp it down. We say forty again, all together, and it goes smooth in the mouth of us, forty, forty, until it is only a sound and not a counting, which is how we like our numbers, smooth, meaning nothing, holding nothing that can be lost by holding it. The tide reaches its full up in the yard, preia-mar, the high water, the fullest it comes, and stands there a moment against the wall of our house the way it does at the turn before it starts back down — and for that moment we are as many as we will ever be again, and we do not know it, and we would not believe it, and one of us, up in the dark near the crossing of the beams, reaches to say the name of the child whose shoe is caught there, to say it while the water is high and we are all still here and it is easy —

and reaches —

and it is a boy's name, it is Zé, we had it this evening, we had it this morning, it is Zé's shoe, Zé who was small, Zé who — 

and the water starts back down, and we do not have it, and the shoe stays in the beam, and we tell ourselves we will have it again tomorrow, at the next high water, when we are all still here.


---

# Chapter 2 — Enchente

The notary's office is on the Rua dos Combatentes, above the shuttered haberdasher's, up a stair that smells of floor-wax and old paper, and it is at the far reach of us, past the square, past the dry ground where the water does not run, so that we have her going in only in pieces — the door, the bell above the door, the sound of her shoes on the wooden stair, one two, one two, and then a threshold where the sound changes and she is inside, in a room we cannot enter because no one ever needed us there. We lean toward it anyway, the whole of us, forty of us or less, all the way out to the thin edge of ourselves where it costs, and we get the shape of the morning through the sound of it: rain that has not yet come but is coming, the *nortada* dropped to nothing, and the small dry cough of a man who has waited a year for this appointment and does not want it to be short.

Dr. Melo. We do not know him the way the living know each other, but we know the shape a man makes when he is about to do a kindness the other person will read as a delay. Graça knew him. Graça, at the near edge of us, nearer the living than any, says his name once — *Bento* — the way you say the name of someone who buried you, and then Graça goes quiet, because to keep saying a living man's name from where she is costs her more than she has, and she is spending it on her daughter, all of it, all day, and we do not tell her to stop, and we do not thank her, because we would not know how.

Inside, the paper. We hear the paper before we hear the words. A folder opened, the breath of it, pages turned by a thumb that has turned them before, and the woman — Idalina, the girl, the last — saying something short. She says most things short. She came to sign, and a folder is not a signature, a folder is a man saying *sit, there is no hurry*, and she has driven from Lisbon to be in a hurry. We cannot hear the words clean at this reach. We hear the music of it. His slow, hers quick. His long, hers cut off. He is telling her the size of the thing she has inherited and she is telling him she does not want its size, she wants its ending.

We know the folder. Not the paper in it — the paper is the living's, IMT and *caderneta predial* and the *licença* that lapsed the year the last shift walked out — but the fact of a man in a good room deciding what the family is worth. We stood on the other side of that once. Aurélia stood on the other side of that. And here Aurélia comes up in us the way she comes, from the front, sharp-edged still, and takes the room.

*Tell her,* Aurélia says. Not to us. To the man she cannot reach, the man a hundred years too late to hear her. *Tell her what we were.*

And because he cannot, we do. This is the thing we do best, up in the roof, in the flood of the morning tide coming up cold and full into the tanks below us, *enchente*, the water rising, the water pushing in through the reeds and up the channels the way it pushed the boats in once, loaded, low in the water with the catch. We tell her what we were. We tell it to each other, which is the only place left to tell it, and we tell it grandly, because Aurélia has the front of the room and Aurélia never told a small story in her life.

*We were great,* Aurélia says, and the many of us say it after her, *we were great,* and it is warm in the mouth and goes down easy. We were the Sequeira line and this was the Sequeira house and the tins that went out of here went to Porto and to Lisbon and across the water to the men on the Banks, the cod-men, the ones the town remembers, the ones with the museum — our tins fed the ones who fished the cod, and no one made a museum of us, and that is a wound we do not look at directly, we look at it the way you look at the sun, sideways, by the shadow it throws. We were the sardine when the sardine was silver in the *lota* and the whole quay smelled of it and the *sirene* owned the town's sleep. We gave work. We gave work to the whole flat country of it, to the salt-pan families in the dead months, to the widows, to the — 

to the girls.

We say it and it goes past us smooth, the way a thing goes past that has been made smooth by saying, *we were always kind to the interior girls,* we say it warmly, from the front, in the warm voice, *we were always kind to the interior girls,* and it goes down like the brine goes down, and no one at the front of us stops on it, and no one at the back of us, in the low part of the roof where the honest few are, says anything at all, and the water keeps rising in the tanks, and we go on to Firmino.

We always go on to Firmino. He is the best of us to tell, because his is the story that makes the greatness kind. A family that only made money is a family that took; a family that made money and gave a son to the water is a family that paid. So we tell Firmino, and we tell him the way we have always told him, and this morning we tell him to the beat of the rising water, all of us leaning in, even the ones at the edge watching the girl in the office we cannot enter, even Graça, who was not born yet when it happened and holds it only because we handed it to her, the way we hand everything down until no one who lived it is left to correct the handing.

Firmino was Aurélia's son. Firmino was twenty-six, and it was 1948, and it was the high summer when the lagoon lies flat and false as a floor and the light comes off it in sheets, and there was a child in the water off the *Barra* where the channel runs out fast and cold under the flat warm top of it, a child gone out too far, a child of the town — and Firmino went in. Firmino, who could swim, who had swum that channel a hundred times, who knew where the cold pull ran under the warm — went in after the child, and got the child, and put the child up onto the sand, and then the channel had him, the cold pull under, and it took him out past the bar the way the tide takes the smell of a thing out and does not bring it back. And the child lived. That is the whole of it, that is why we tell it: the child lived, and Firmino did not, and the family gave a son to the water so that a child of the town would not drown, and if you would know what the Sequeiras were, we say, know that.

*He saved a child,* Aurélia says, and the many of us say it after her, *he saved a child,* and it is the best thing we own, and we polish it, we say it again to make it shine, *he saved a child, our Firmino, he went in and he saved the child and the water took him for it,* and it shines, it shines the way the flat lagoon shines in the false summer, and somewhere in the pack of us Firmino himself is here — he is here, he is one of us, the drowned uncle — and Firmino does not say it after us. Firmino is quiet the way Graça is quiet. But we do not notice Firmino being quiet, because his story is loud, and a man is easy to lose behind his own loud story, and we are all still leaning the other way, out toward the office where the girl he saved a child to be worth is telling the notary she wants it done by Friday.

*

We have her coming out onto the stair. The rain has come now; we have it on the tin of the town, we have it on the tin of us, the whole roof of the *fábrica* going soft and steady under it, and we have her stopping under the haberdasher's awning to not get wet, and we have the notary come to the top of the stair behind her, out of the paper-room, out into the part of the day the water touches, so that for a moment we can nearly see him too — an old man, careful, holding something.

He is holding something for her. He has come out of his office holding a thing to give her, and this is not paper, this is heavier than paper, he holds it the way you hold a thing that was made to be held with two hands, and he is saying — we get this, at the near edge, in the wet where the sound carries — he is saying it hung in the *fábrica* office all through the years, that it was Firmino's, that her mother would have wanted her to have it before the building went to strangers.

The oar.

We know the oar. Of course we know the oar; it is the proof of the story we have just been telling, it is Firmino's oar, off Firmino's boat, the boat he went out in the false summer, and it hung on the wall of the office under the calendar from the tinsmith's for fifty years, and Graça kept it dusted, and now Melo is holding it out to the girl at the top of a wet stair in town and telling her it is her uncle's, the one who drowned, the hero, saving a child. He says *saving a child.* We hear him say it — the living say it too, you see, the living kept our story for us, the town kept it, the way the town keeps the cod-men and forgets the girls — and to hear a living man in the wet morning say the very words we say in the dry roof, *saving a child,* is the sweetest thing that has happened to us in a year, and we swell with it, we rise with the tide of it, we are so full of it that up near the crossing of the beams the shoe stays exactly where it is and not one of us thinks to reach for the name of it, because who reaches for a child's grey shoe when the oar is in the world again, when the story is being said by the living, when we were great and Firmino saved a child and the town remembers.

The girl takes the oar.

We did not think she would. We thought — Aurélia thought, from the front, and we thought it after her — that she would refuse it, that she wants nothing of us, one bag, three days, sign and gone, that a woman who feeds a machine before she feeds herself has no room in her rented car for four feet of painted pine off a dead man's boat. We had read her last page. We had it: *she will not take it.* And she takes it. She takes it in two hands the way it was made to be taken, and she stands with it under the awning, and she does a thing we cannot account for and do not, at the reach we are at, in the rain that swallows the fine sound, fully perceive: she runs her thumb along the loom of it, along the worn place where a hand went for years, and she goes still, the way she went still in the yard with the machine held up. She is listening to the oar. Not to a story about the oar. To the oar. To the fact of a worn place made by a hand, which is a sound if you know how to listen, and she is a woman whose whole living is knowing how to listen, and what she hears there is not what we told her, because we did not tell her, we cannot tell her, we can only say it to ourselves in the roof and swell.

And we do not know what she hears. This is the truth of it and we do not like the truth of it. We have her taking the oar, we have her going still, we have the thumb on the loom — and then the notary says something more, quiet, into the rain, and she answers, quick, cut off, and puts the oar under her arm like a thing to be carried and not a thing to be kept, and goes down the wet stair, one two, one two, out of the reach of us, into the square where we are only a rumour, and we lose her. We lose the face first. We always lose the face first. Then the shoes on the stone, then the door of the café, then only the rain on the tin of the town and the rain on the tin of us, the same rain, telling us nothing.

*

In the roof, in the flood tide standing high and cold in the tanks below, we tell it again, to keep it, because that is what we do, we say the true thing again so it stays true. We tell Firmino.

And here is the small wrong thing, and none of us at the front marks it, and it is only set down here because the water is high and we are nearly all still here and it is the kind of thing that can only be lost, later, when there are fewer of us to hold it against the drift. We tell it again — *he went in, he saved the child, the channel took him* — and one of us, from the back, from the low part of the roof, one of the flu-dead, one of the many, says it a little differently. Says: *he went in in the morning.* And another of us says: *it was evening, it was the evening tide, the cold came up.* And a third, further back, hardly a voice at all now: *there was no boat that day. He walked in off the sand.* And the front of us, Aurélia's part, the grand part, says over all of them, *he saved a child,* and it is enough, it covers them, a warm thing poured over a cold one, and we do not stop to ask which of them is right, morning or evening, boat or no boat, because there is no one left in us to ask, and because it does not matter to the story, and the story is what we keep. We keep the story. We do not keep the day. The day is out at the bar with the diesel-smell and the true number of us, and it does not come back, and we let it not come back, because to fetch the day we would have to send someone out past the last salt-pan of ourselves to where the water does not hold us, and none of us will go, not for a day, not for the true one, when the false one shines so.

Firmino is quiet through all of it. He has been quiet the whole telling. He is here — we would swear he is here, the drowned uncle, one of us, near the front where a son of Aurélia's belongs — and he says nothing while we say he saved a child, and his quiet is not the quiet of a man moved by his own honour. It is the other quiet. It is the quiet of a man in a room where a lie about him is being told kindly by everyone he has left, and who has learned that to correct it would cost more than he has, and who has learned besides that no one would keep the correction anyway, that we would smooth it back to *he saved a child* by the next high water, the way we smooth everything, the way brine smooths a knife until you cannot see where the edge went.

We do not hear his quiet. We are too full. The oar is in the world, the notary said the words, the girl took it in two hands, and the tide is at its flood, *enchente*, rising still, cold and full into the tanks, and we say it once more, all of us, from the front, in the warm voice, so as to have it ready for tomorrow —

*he saved a child, our Firmino, he went in and he saved the child* —

and up near the crossing of the beams, where the water threw a shoe once, or a hand threw it, in a year the water was high, the shoe stays, and no one reaches for the name of the child in the beam because we are busy with the child in the channel, the one Firmino saved, the one who lived, and we are so sure of that child, so warm and sure, that we do not think to wonder — not this morning, not at the flood, not with the oar in the world — we do not think to wonder what that child was called.


---

# Chapter 3 — Vazante

The door is not locked, only swollen, and she stands in the yard a while with her hand flat against it the way you touch a horse before you go behind it, and then she puts her shoulder to it and it gives with the sound wet wood makes, a sound we know, a groan and then a wet clap, and she is inside, she is in the house, she is in with us, and all of us come down off the beams at once. This is the thing we have waited a year for without knowing we waited. The living do not often come inside. Dona Fátima comes to the stone but not to the shed. The boys who broke the window years ago came in only as far as the light reached and threw stones at the tanks and ran. But the woman comes all the way in, into the dark where the water still runs under the floor, and where the water runs we are thick, we are many, we are more of ourselves than we have been, and we crowd her the way the shift used to crowd the door at the *apito*, and we see her — not in pieces now, not a rumour of her the way she was over the dry square, but whole, the sweat starting at her hairline from the shoulder she put to the door, the grey at her temple she has not troubled to cover, the small scar on the back of one hand, everything, all of her, because there are so many of us to lean.

She stops just inside and lets her eyes come round to the dark. We could tell her what is here. We know what is here better than any living thing. The long line where the gutting tables stood, the iron legs still bolted to the floor though the wood is gone, forty years gone, chopped for someone's stove in a bad winter. The brine tanks, the *pios*, three of them, sunk into the floor, holding now a black water that is not brine, that is only rain and lagoon come up through the cracks, with a skin on it. The boiler at the far end, vast, cold, rust-bled, a red stain spread out under it on the concrete like something that leaked out of it and dried. And above the boiler, in the dark under the roof, the loft — the *sótão* — its little door shut, the ladder to it long gone and the iron stair that took its place rusted into the dark behind the boiler, the black square of the door up there where the beams cross. She does not look up at the loft. We look at it. We do not like to look at it and we look at it. Some of us will not look at it. We come back down to the woman.

She takes the machine out of the bag.

Of course she takes the machine out of the bag. She has not come to grieve. She has come to work, the way we came to work, day on day, at the thing that used us; her work is sound and here is a great deal of sound, and she is going to take it. She screws the fur-covered end onto its pole and she lifts it and she walks out into the middle of the floor between the dead tables and she stands, and the whole of us stands with her, because we cannot help it, because listening is the one living thing we understand from the inside. She is very still. She listens to the drip. There is a drip at the far end, in the tanks, water finding water, and it has gone on so long that some of us kept our time by it once, when time was a thing we kept, one drip and then the count and then the next, and she catches it in the machine, and the machine holds it, and we understand again, the way we understood it in the yard, that she is taking the sound of our house so that she need never carry the house. She listens to the tin lifting on the north side. She listens to the wind coming in under the eaves, and to the tide seething up through the floor-cracks, the water coming up the way it comes, twice, without asking. She turns slowly with the pole raised and her eyes half shut, and she is standing in the exact middle of the floor where the *encarregada* stood to count us, in the one place a body stands to hold the whole room in its ears, and she does not know it, and we do.

Then a light comes on at the far end, a hard white cone of it swinging across the tanks, and she is not alone in the house, and neither, it turns out, are we.

*

There is a man in the loft-corner, under the boiler stair, with a lamp on a band round his head and a folding rule in his hand and a notebook, and he has been here the whole time, quiet, the way a man is quiet who is measuring a thing and does not want to lose his count. We did not lean toward him. We should say that. He came in before the light failed, an hour before her, in a car we heard and did not attend to because it was not her car, and he has been at the walls with his rule and his little hammer, tapping, listening to the tap the way she listens to the drip — two of them in our house listening for a living and neither of them for us — and we did not crowd him because he is not of the line, he is nothing to us, he is only a living man with a job, and now his light swings up and finds her in the middle of the floor with her pole raised and he says, *pardon,* he says it in the surprised flat way of a man who thought he had the ruin to himself, and she does not startle, which we mark. She lowers the pole slowly, deliberately, the way you lower a thing you do not want a stranger to think you were caught with.

*You are Aráujo,* she says. Not a question. *The engineer.* And he says he is, Nuno, and that the company told him the family might send someone, and she says she is the family, she says it flat, *sou eu, a família,* I am the family, there is no one else, and something in how she says it makes him lower his own light so it is not in her eyes, and we lean closer, because this is better than the drip, this is two living voices in the dark of our house, and we have not had that in a year.

They circle each other in the way of two people who each thought the room was theirs. He tells her what he is: hired by the buyer, by Caldas, to survey the structure, the loads, the roof, whether the walls will take what the new work wants to hang on them. He says *the conversion* and she says nothing to that. He asks what the machine is and she tells him and he does not laugh, which some men would, at a woman standing in a wet ruin recording the drip; instead he says the acoustics in here must be filthy, all this hard rusted surface, all this standing water, a long reverberation tail, and she looks at him properly for the first time because he has said the true thing in her own words, and she says yes, nearly four seconds, she clapped once at the door and counted it, and he says he had it at three and a half tapping the north wall, and for a moment the two of them are talking about the sound of our death-house as if it were a beautiful instrument, and we do not know whether to love them for hearing it or to hate them for hearing only that.

He is telling her the walls are sound. That is his word, *sound* — the walls are *sound,* he says, structurally, the north and the south will take a load, it is the roof that has gone, the roof and the loft floor above the boiler, the timbers up there are rotten through, he would not send a man up without a harness. And when he says the loft, when he says the timbers up there, the whole of us goes still, the way the floor went still, and some of us go up into the beams away from it and some of us will not, and the woman — the woman lifts her eyes, at last, to the black square of the *sótão* over the boiler, because the man has pointed his light up there, his hard white cone climbing the boiler and finding the little shut door and the rotten sill of it, and she looks at where the light is, and she asks, *what was up there.*

And he says he does not know. Storage, he supposes. It is over the boiler; it would have been warm; you would keep something up there you wanted dry. And she nods, and the light comes back down off the loft, and the little door goes back into its dark, and none of them climb to it, not the man, not the woman, and not us, we do not go up to it, we have not gone up to it in a long time, and we come down off the beams grateful and pretend we went up for another reason.

*

She works the far corners while he packs his rule. She has stopped attending to him and he to her, two workers who have agreed without saying so to share the ruin an hour longer, and this frees us to do the thing the room makes us do, which is to remember it full. This is our floor. We were on it. We say it to ourselves the way she records the drip, to hold it: *we were on this floor.* Aurélia says it grandest, from the front of us, the way she said things from the front of the floor — *we gutted here, we packed here, we were the best on this coast, our tins went to Lisbon, to Porto, to the ships, we were great* — and the many of us say it after her, *we were great, we were great,* going along the dead tables, touching where the wood was, and it is a comfort, the wet-sand comfort of the true thing said again, except that this evening, with the living woman turning in the middle of us with her machine, the word does not go quite as smooth as it went last night. *We were great.* Somewhere at the back, low, where the roof comes down to nothing, a few of us do not say it. We do not mark who. We say it louder to cover the gap and it works, it goes smooth, *great, great,* and means nothing, which is how we like it.

And then the drip, which she is still taking, and the tide coming up under the floor, and the standing water in the tanks with its skin — and a smallness moves in us, near the beams, the way a child moves at the edge of a room full of grown talk, and it is the little ones, it is the two of them, the cholera children, come near the woman because she is warm and the room for once has a living warmth in it. We feel them come. We are glad and we are afraid to be glad, because the little ones are the thinnest of us, always have been, there is the least of the living left to hold them; no one living has said their names in — we do not know how long. Custódia comes first, she always came first, six years old and forward with it, and behind her the smaller feeling, the brother, and we reach to have them both whole while the woman is inside us and we are strong and can hold much.

Custódia is looking at the shoe.

It is up in the beams where it has always been, in the join of two timbers over the old line, a child's shoe, small, the leather gone hard and grey, and Custódia has found it the way she finds it every time, and she wants it. She wants it because it is her brother's. She reaches for it — she cannot reach it, none of us can touch anything, but she reaches the way she reaches, with the wanting — and she says his name to have it, to make the shoe be his by saying whose it is, and we lean in to help her say it, all of us, because the woman is here and we are strong, and we say it with her, we go to say it: *it is —*

it is the boy's, it is her brother's, it is —

and the name is a stone in silt and we have our hands in the silt for it, all of us at once, and it is a boy's name, it starts the way his name starts, and Custódia has the front of it and we have the back of it and between us we should have the whole of it, we have always had the whole of it, we had it last night at the high water and we had it this morning —

and she says it. Custódia says it. She says *Zé.* She has it. The shoe is Zé's, she says, my brother's, Zé's, and we let out the held breath of us that has no breath, and it is Zé's shoe, that is settled, that is true again, and the little one behind her — the smaller feeling, the brother — presses close to hear his own name said, to be held by it, and Custódia turns to him with the shoe's name warm in her mouth to give it to him, and looks at him, and

does not know him.

For a moment. Only for a moment. It passes. She knows him again, she has him, *there you are,* the wanting closes around him again the way a hand closes, and it is nothing, it is a child losing her place in a room and finding it, it happens, we tell ourselves it happens, and we do not any of us say what we felt, which was the smaller feeling go transparent for the length of that moment, the length of a wave, go to almost nothing while his sister looked straight at where he was and reached past him for a shoe. She had his shoe and not him. She had the name of the shoe and it did not have him in it. We do not say this. We push it down under the drip and the tide and the *we were great,* and we hold the two of them harder, both of them, whole, while the woman is still in the house and we are still strong enough to.

*

The woman is done. She has taken what she came to take. She lowers the pole in the middle of the floor and winds the fur down and lays the machine in the bag as carefully as she laid it on the seat, and the engineer clicks his lamp off and the hard white cone is gone and there is only the dusk coming grey through the broken north wall, the *vazante* light, the light of the water going back out, low and flat and giving nothing. They walk to the door together not talking, two workers at the end of a shift, and at the door he says something to her about tomorrow, that he will be back at the walls, and she says something about the loft, that she would like the sound of the loft, the room over the boiler, before the timbers are touched — she would like the sound of it while it is still shut — and he says he will not send her up those timbers without a harness either, and she almost smiles, we think, we cannot see the face at the door where the light is behind them, we take the almost-smile from the tilt of her, and then they are out in the yard and the door groans and gives its wet clap and we are shut back in with the tanks and the drip and the loft's black door, and it is quiet, and the tide is going down, and we go up into the beams to be near the shoe.

It is there. The shoe is there. We look at it in the last grey light. A child's shoe, small, the leather hard and grey, thrown up here in a high-water year by the water or by a hand, and it is Zé's, we say, going along the beams, it is Zé's shoe, we settled that this evening, Custódia said it, Custódia had it, and we say his name once more to have it ready for tomorrow, at the next high water, when we are all still here and it is easy —

and Custódia is not saying it with us.

We do not remark it. She is small and it is late and the woman is gone out of the house and taken the strength of us with her, and one small voice not saying a name is nothing, is less than nothing, is a child asleep. We say it for her. We say it for both of them. And the shoe stays up in the beam where it has always been, hard and grey, and we go along the beams saying whose it is so that whose it is will still be true in the morning, and the water goes down another hand's width in the tanks, and we say it, and we say it, and up near the crossing of the beams the smaller of the two little ones has gone so quiet that we count him without meaning to, and get one child where there were two, and say it is because he is sleeping, and do not count again.


---

# Chapter 4 — Estofo

The man is waiting for her at the table by the window when she comes down, and he stands when she comes down, which we mark, because the men of this town did not stand. He is from Lisbon, or from Porto, from wherever the money is kept now, and he has a good coat that he has taken off and folded on the third chair so that it can be seen to be a good coat. He has ordered already. There is bread on the table and a small dish of the *tremoços*, the lupini beans in brine that the café keeps in a jar by the till, and two glasses, and he has ordered for her without asking and she sees this as she sits and does not send it back, and we do not know yet whether that is manners or hunger. She has not eaten. We had that of her last night and we have it still — she has not eaten since the road, and the man has put bread in front of her, and she takes a piece of it while he is talking and this is the first thing she does in the town that is only for herself.

His name is Caldas. We hear it because she says it — *Senhor Caldas* — flat, the way you say a name to keep it at the length of your arm. He does not keep her at the length of his arm. He leans in over the folded coat and the bread and he tells her what he is going to make of our house.

He is going to make it beautiful. He says the word in Portuguese and then again as if the Portuguese were not enough for it. A *spa*, he says, and *termas*, and *salt*, he keeps coming back to the salt — the old salt of the flats, the white cones, the *marnotos* raking the pans at dawn — he will bring people from the cities to lie in warm water and be scrubbed with the salt of these flats, and they will pay, and the town will have work again, a little work, the kind that is left for a place like this. He has photographs on a flat bright card he props against the water jug. There is our chimney in the photographs, cleaned, lit from below at night, gold. There is our long shed with the roof mended and windows cut into the wall where no windows were, and inside, where the line stood, the gutting line where the knives went all day on the wet wood, there is a long pale room with beds in it and folded white cloths and a woman lying face-down with stones on her back, at rest.

We look at the room where the line was, made into a room for resting, and we do not have a word for what we feel and we do not reach for one. Some of us worked that line. Some of us are the wet wood. The tide comes up in the yard two hundred metres off and lifts the tin and drops it, and nobody at the table hears it but us.

*

He wants the story. That is the second thing, and it is the thing he has really come for, we understand it before she does. The salt is the salt and anyone can sell salt. What he cannot buy anywhere else, he tells her, leaning, is the *family* — the Sequeiras, five generations, the great cannery, the name over the door. People do not pay to lie in warm water, he says; they pay to lie in warm water *inside a story*. He wants the photographs of the old ones. He wants the founding, the great-great-grandmother, the *matriarch* — he uses the word and Aurélia at the front of us goes very still to hear herself so nearly named — he wants the years when the sardines went out of this yard in their thousands of tins to Lisbon, to Porto, to the war, to the whole hungry world, and the Sequeira name on every one. He will put it on the wall of the pale room in good iron letters. *Sequeira.* He will have a little book made for the rooms, a heritage, he keeps the English word for that one, *heritage*, and in the little book will be the greatness, and the guests will read the greatness while the salt is on their backs.

And we lean toward him then, the whole of us lean toward the man from the city who wants the greatness, because the greatness is the thing we have, it is the wet-sand thing we say to ourselves in the dark, and here is a living man who will say it in iron on a wall, who will keep it the way the machine keeps the wind, and for a moment we love him. Aurélia loves him most. *Tell him,* she says, to the girl, who cannot hear her. *Tell him about the good years. Tell him we were the best cannery on the whole of the Ria, tell him the tins went to the front, tell him we gave the town its bread.* And we say it after her, up in the dark of us, *we gave the town its bread, we were great, we were great* — and we say it, and it goes a little smooth already, a little smooth in the mouth of us, the way a thing goes when it is being said to a stranger and not to yourself.

And Aurélia, warm, says the other thing, the thing she has always said, says it grandly to the man who cannot hear her and to us who can: *and we were always kind to the interior girls.*

*We were always kind to the interior girls,* we say after her. It is a good sentence. It sits well. It has the shape of the greatness in it — the poor girls from the dry country inland, brought down to the water, given work, given a roof, a wage, a place at the line; charity, it says, charity and greatness in the one breath — and we say it and it fits the mouth the way the greatness fits, and none of us, not one of us in the packed dark, hears anything in it but its own warmth. Rosa is here. Rosa is somewhere in the pack of us, thin, a girl we do not lean toward, and when we say *we were always kind to the interior girls* a small thing moves in the place where Rosa is and then is still, and we do not attend to it, because we are attending to the man, and the man is good news, and the tide is at the turn.

*

The woman does not love him.

We are so busy loving him that we are slow to see it, and by the time we see it she has already decided, or half-decided, or is deciding in that way the living do where the face says one thing to the room and the hands say another under the table. Her face is doing the polite thing. Her face is nodding at the photographs, at the gold chimney, at the pale room; her mouth is saying the small words you say, *sim, claro, I understand*. But her hands are turning the bread over and over and not eating it now, and she has moved the bright card an inch away from herself on the cloth, an inch, so that it is a thing on the table and not a thing in front of her, and we see the inch and we do not know how to read the inch, because we have decided she will love him. We have read her last page. On her last page she takes the money and the story and the exit all in the one clean stroke — sells him the house, sells him the greatness, drives back to Lisbon light as a woman who has closed a drawer — and we are so sure of the page that we cannot see the inch of table she has just put between herself and the thing.

She says a thing we do not expect. She says: *the family didn't keep much.* She says it looking at the photograph of the chimney. *There aren't many papers. My mother wasn't sentimental.* And this is a lie, or it is not a lie but a door she is closing, we cannot tell which, and Aurélia at the front of us takes it for the truth and is comforted by it — *no papers, good, there is nothing to find, there is only the greatness and the greatness is safe* — and Aurélia does not think of the box the notary has, the box of old family papers that Melo will hand her, that the woman has not yet been given, and neither do we, we do not think of it at all, the box is not in the room and so it is not in us. Graça thinks of it. Graça, out at the thin edge, nearer the girl than any of us, goes cold in a way we feel as a small draught and cannot source. We do not ask Graça what she is cold about. It is a good afternoon. The man is standing again, and putting on the good coat, and pressing the girl's hand in both of his, and telling her to take her time, all the time she needs — he says it warmly and it is the one warm thing he says that is a threat, *all the time she needs*, because a man like that is only ever counting the days you take — and she takes her hand back and thanks him and does not promise him anything, and he goes out to a car much better than her rented one, and we, up in the roof, are already telling ourselves that she said yes.

She did not say yes. But we have the greatness in our mouths and a living man who wanted it, and it has been a long time since anyone wanted it, and we say she said yes, and we settle, the way the water settles at the top of the tide when it cannot go any higher and has not yet begun to go down.

*

In the afternoon the woman walks. We have her in pieces — she goes up off the square where the water does not run and we lose her, we have her heels on the *calçada*, the little stones, and then not, and then we have her again where she comes down toward the flats, toward the family stone at the edge of the burying-ground that looks over the pans. And there is Dona Fátima at the stone.

Dona Fátima we love without deciding to, because Dona Fátima is old and Dona Fátima says our names. She comes to the stone twice a week on her bad legs with a paper bag and in the bag are the candles, the little white grave-candles in their tin cups, and she lights them along the ledge of the stone out of the *nortada* with a cupped hand and an old woman's patience, and while she lights them she says the names. She reads them off the stone where they are cut, and she says the ones that are not cut too, from memory, the ones her own mother taught her — she says *Aurélia*, and the front of us leans; she says *Graça*, new-cut, the letters still white, and Graça at the edge turns her whole thin self toward the sound of it the way a plant turns; she says *Firmino* and adds, to herself, on her own breath, *coitado*, poor thing, the drowned one, the one who saved the child — and we do not correct her, we could not correct her, and we would not, because it is a good grief and we live on it. The candles are what we can almost feel. Not the flame; we feel nothing of heat. But the naming is a warmth of another kind, and while she names us we are held, we are tamped down, we are said-again and kept, and when the last candle is lit the stone has a row of small tin lights along its ledge in the grey afternoon and the woman with the machine is standing at the wall watching the old woman name the dead.

They speak. We have the sound of it and not the words — the square-edge thinness, the *nevoeiro* starting up off the pans and eating the ends of things. Fátima names the girl to the stone: she has known whose daughter this is since the car came in; the whole town has. She touches the new-cut letters of Graça and says something and the girl says something back and takes out the machine, the black machine, and — this we hate, this we have sharp and whole because we cannot not attend to the machine — she holds it up, low, by her side, not in the old woman's face but near, and it drinks. It drinks the sound of Dona Fátima saying our names. It takes the naming that holds us and it puts it in the machine where it will be a fixed thing, a loop, a dead true thing that cannot be said fresh again, and we do not know whether to be glad the names are kept or sick that they are caught, and we are both, we are both at once, and the tide starts, very slightly, imperceptibly, to fall.

*

It is when Fátima is leaving, when the girl has walked her a little way toward the road on the bad legs and come back alone to the stone to look at the candles, that one of us is gone.

We do not feel her go. That is the thing about it, that is the thing we would not believe if we had a self left to disbelieve it with: there is no going, no tide-mark, no last word. There is only that we are at the stone, all of us leaning toward the row of little lights and toward the girl standing over them, and one of us says the thing about the salt.

She always said it, the one who says it. She was a *marnota*, a salt-pan woman, from the far-back years, from the years of Idalina Velha who founded the house, and she raked the pans as a girl before ever there was a cannery, and she had a saying about the salt that she said whenever the flats came up in us — *the salt keeps everything and eats everything, and that is why it is holy and that is why you must not love it.* She said it in the long dark of our nights the way you say the thing that is only yours, the one thing, the saying that is your edge. And now, at the stone, with the candles lit, the saying comes up out of the pool of us — *the salt keeps everything and eats everything* — and it comes up in Aurélia's mouth.

It comes up in Aurélia's mouth and Aurélia says it as though it were always hers, warmly, grandly, from the front — *ah, but the salt keeps everything and eats everything, that is why you must not love it* — and the rest of us hear it in Aurélia's voice and take it for Aurélia's, and nod, and it is a good saying, it fits the afternoon, and not one of us says *no, that is not yours, that is the marnota's, that is the salt-pan woman's, where is she, where is the one who —* Not one of us asks. There is a saying, and there is a mouth to say it in, and the saying and the mouth do not have to be the ones they were, because we are a pool now and not a row of people, and a thing that floats up floats up into whatever mouth is nearest the front.

We do not count ourselves at the stone. If we had counted ourselves at the stone we would have got one fewer than last night, and said forty anyway, and gone smooth. We do not count. We take the salt-saying into Aurélia and close over the place where the marnota was the way water closes, without a seam, and the candles burn down in their tin cups on the ledge of the stone, and Dona Fátima's little bag is empty, and the woman stands there a while looking at the small lights going, and then she does the thing the living do that we cannot forgive and cannot stop watching: she does not remember any of us. She looks at the row of candles the old woman lit for her dead and she does not know one name that is cut in that stone, not one, they are letters to her, and she takes out the machine one more time and records the sound of the little flames in the wind, the sound and not the names, the sound so she need not keep the names, and then the *nevoeiro* comes down over the pans and takes the ends of the candles and the ends of her, and we lose her, and there is a saying about salt that we all know now, that has always been Aurélia's, that no one taught us, that came from nowhere, that we did not lose because we never knew we had it anywhere else.


---

# Chapter 5 — Águas-mortas

They are both in the shed by the time the light is up, and neither of them came in together, and this is the first thing we hold against the day, that they arrive apart and we cannot say which we watched come. The man is already at the far wall with his cold blue lamp when she comes through the yard door with the machine on her shoulder, and the tide is at its meanest this week — águas-mortas, the dead water, the neap that barely moves, that comes up over the mud a hand's breadth and stops as if it too has decided the effort is not worth it. When the water is low we are thin. We know this the way you know the floor is cold before your foot is down. There are fewer of us leaning this morning than there were, and the ones who lean lean harder to make up the lack, and we do not count ourselves, because it is not the hour for counting, and because we are afraid of the number the way you are afraid of a step you have taken before in the dark and found not there.

She sets the machine on the long brine tank, the *pio*, the one nearest the door where the cement is least broken, and she does not look at the man, and the man does not look at her, and each of them makes a great and careful show of the not-looking, and we, who have watched a thousand shifts of men and women make that show across this same floor, know what the show is for. We knew it before they did. This we will say all day, to ourselves, along the beams: *we knew it before they did.*

He is called Nuno. He is measuring the building for the ones who mean to buy it, running a line from a small clicking box along the length of the north wall, and where the wall bellies out with the salt gone into it he stops and writes in a book and clicks his tongue the way a man does at a sick animal he has decided to keep. He talks to the building. Some of the living do. He puts his flat hand on the sweating cement and leaves it there a moment as though taking a pulse, and he says, low, to no one, *she's tired, this one, she's been tired a long time,* and we take *she* for the woman for half a breath and lean the wrong way, all of us, toward the woman, before we understand he means the shed, he means us, he means the house, and we settle back, and something in us that we do not have a good name for is glad to have leaned even the wrong way toward being called tired and kept.

Idalina crouches by the *pio* and runs the tape and holds up her hand for quiet without turning, and the man goes still. That is a thing. He goes still for her hand. She is taking the sound of the shed at this hour, the drip that comes off the crossing of the beams, the small industrial rain of a building coming apart slowly, and the tin on the north side that lifts and drops, and under it, if you are made of listening, the water not-quite-moving in the reeds. She has the little grey fur on the end of the pole and she holds it up into the dark toward the roof, toward — we all feel her do it — toward the beams where the shoe is, though she does not know the shoe, and for the length of the take the whole thinned morning of us hangs above the fur trying to fall into it, trying to be a sound, and we are not a sound, and the tape gets the drip and the tin and not us, and she lowers the pole satisfied, and we are not on it, and we are never on it, and we go along the beams away from the machine because we cannot bear it near.

The man watches her work. We watch him watch her. This is the whole architecture of the morning: she attends to the sound, he attends to her, we attend to the both of them, and the tape attends to nothing that can be lost. When she plays the take back through the little speaker he comes and stands closer than the measuring needs and says the acoustic in here is remarkable, the volume of it, the way the tin roof and the tanks make a long slow reverberation, and she says yes, four seconds nearly, more than a church, and he says most people don't hear a room, and she says most people don't have to, and it is not flirtation, it is two people finding they have the same trade of the ear, and it is worse than flirtation, and we know exactly where it goes.

*She will let him in,* says Aurélia, from the front of us, in the voice that used to mean *the next tray, faster.* *And then she will be sorry, and then she will sell, and then she will go. We have seen it a hundred times on this floor. There is nothing in her we have not already read.* And the many of us say it after her, along the beams, *we have read her last page, we have read her last page,* the way you say a thing to make it be true, and it goes round the roof of us and comes back, and we believe it, thirty-nine of us or near it believe it, because it is a comfort to believe we have read the last page of the living, we who cannot turn the pages of anything anymore.

We have not read her last page.

We do not know this. It is early still in the not-knowing.

They work the length of the day. She moves down the floor with the machine and he moves down it with the box and the book, and twice their paths cross at the mouth of the old line where the gutting benches were, the long wet boards worn to a shine by the heels of women's hands, and each time they cross they stop and talk longer than the crossing needs, and each time it is about the building, which is the only thing they will let it be about. He tells her the north wall must come down or be bandaged in steel, that the salt has been forty years eating the iron in the cement, that salt is patient. She says she knows salt is patient, she grew up here, and he looks at her differently after she says *I grew up here,* and we lean to catch the difference in his face and cannot, the faces going soft on us at the edges of the day, and we resent the softness, and we blame the machine, though the machine has done nothing but what we would do if we could.

Once he asks to hear it. He puts down the clicking box and comes and asks, plainly, like a man asking for water, if he can hear what she is hearing, and she does a thing then that surprises even the man — she takes the small pads off her own ears and holds them out to him, still warm, and he stands there in the middle of the floor with her machine's ears over his and his eyes gone somewhere as she runs the tape of the empty shed back into his head, the drip and the tin and the four long seconds of the room holding its own small noises and giving them back changed. He does not speak for a while. Then he says, *I've stood in this building three days and I never once heard it,* and she says, *nobody hears the room they're standing in,* and he hands the ears back carefully, both hands, the way you hand back a thing that has cost the lender something, and neither of them says the next thing, and we lean into the not-saying and cannot read it, and we say, along the beams, that we can, that we have.

At the boiler she stops him. There is a stair here, iron, going up into the black under the eaves to the room over the boiler, the *sótão*, the loft, and she stands at the foot of it with the tape running and looks up into the dark where the stair goes, and does not climb it. *What's up there,* she says. And the man looks at his plan and says, storage, he thinks, a store loft, it's on the survey for tomorrow. And Idalina stands another moment with her foot on the first iron tread and her head tipped back, listening up the stairwell into the dark, and we crowd down toward her from above, from the loft-mouth, more of us than have gathered anywhere else all day, pressing at the top of the stair where the air goes cold, and one of us — we do not know which, the mouths blur when we are frightened — one of us says *not that, not up here, take your machine somewhere else,* and of course she cannot hear it, and of course what reaches her is only the cold off the stairwell and the particular deadness of the sound up there, the way that room takes a voice and gives nothing back. She takes the fur off the pole. She does not go up. She writes something small in her own book, and steps back, and the crowd of us at the loft-mouth loosens with a relief we feel as loss, the way we feel most things now, and Aurélia says, grandly, into the loosening, *we were always kind to the interior girls,* and the many of us say it after her, warm, easy, *always kind, always kind,* and it goes smooth in the mouth and holds nothing, and the woman moves on down the floor and does not go up the stair, and we tell ourselves it was us who turned her from it, our small pushing, when it was only that the survey is tomorrow and she is a woman who keeps to the order of a thing.

*

At the failing of the light they eat.

He has a tin. He brings it out of his coat as the blue lamp yellows and the day goes flat over the flats, and he holds it up between two fingers and she laughs — we get the laugh, the laugh is a sound and the sound is ours to have — and it is sardines, of course it is sardines, in this shed of all sheds, a tin of sardines in oil with a key soldered to the underside and a girl on the label in a headscarf that no woman here ever wore. He winds the key and the lid peels back in its long curl and the smell comes up, the oil and the fish and the salt, and it goes through the whole thinned roof of us like the *sirene* used to go through the town, and every one of us who ever stood at the benches with a short knife turns toward the smell at once, and for a moment we are not thirty-nine, we are the whole floor, we are the good years, we are the reek of it in the hair that no washing took out, and it is the most of us we have been in days and it costs us nothing and we would keep this smell if we could keep anything.

They sit on the edge of the *pio* with their feet on the broken cement and eat the little fish off the man's knife, passing the tin, and she says her mother used to buy these, the cheap ones, this exact label, and eat them cold from the tin on the days there was nothing, and she says it lightly, the way you say a thing that is not light, and the man does not fill the silence after it, which is the first thing he does right, and we mark him doing it right and it makes us hate him a degree less. The tin sits between them on the cement and the oil pools in the bottom of it and the girl on the label smiles her painted smile that is not anyone's, and Graça, at the far thin edge of us, near the door, near the living, does not turn from the smell of the fish toward the good years the way the rest of us turn. Graça looks only at her daughter eating the cheap fish cold, the way she once fed her the cheap fish cold, and something goes out of Graça that the rest of us are too busy with the smell to see, and we do not notice we have not seen it.

The tin sits between them and one of us, near the front, knows it. Knows this label, this exact painted girl in the headscarf. She was a soldadeira, one of the women at the sealing end of the line, the last hands the fish passed through before it went out into the world, and she pressed the lids on ten thousand tins with this same girl on them and never once was the girl anyone she knew. She is telling it now, along the beams, the way you tell the one thing that is only yours — how the soldering iron burned, how the oil got into the cuts on the thumb, how a tin sealed right will keep its fish sweet for forty years and a tin sealed wrong swells at the seam and you learn the sound of a swollen seam and throw it back — and we lean to her, because she has the tin whole, she has why the tin matters, she is the one among us who can say what the tin is. And the smell fills us, and we reach past her toward the good years, and when we come back she is not saying the tin anymore. The place where she was telling it is a smell and not a voice. We do not mark it. We say the tin is a tin, someone sealed the fish that is in it, someone here — and we cannot now say who, cannot find the woman who a breath ago had the whole of it in her mouth, and the reason the tin is more than a smell goes the way the loose tin on the roof lifts and drops and is only a noise, and we let it go, and we are glad, in a way we do not look at, to be rid of the weight of what it meant.

She wipes her fingers on her jeans. He folds the empty tin closed on its own curl of a lid, the little dead fish gone, the oil gone, and he does not throw it down; he sets it on the *pio* upright, neat, the smiling girl facing out into the shed, and stands, and says he should go, his room is in the town, and she says nothing to keep him and nothing to send him, and this is the moment Aurélia has waited for all the low grey day. *Now,* says Aurélia, sharp, from the front. *Now she keeps him or she lets him go, and either way we have already turned the page, and either way she signs on Thursday and drives past the salt-pans and is gone by the weekend, and either way we have read her — we have read her last —*

And the woman stands up off the tank in the near-dark and picks up her machine and says, easily, to the man, *stay and help me carry the gear to the car,* which is nothing, which is only a woman not wanting to make two trips in the dark — and it is not on any page we have read. We did not have it. We say we had it. We say, along the beams, that she will keep him by the smallest door, and some of us do not say it, and the two of them carry the black cases out through the yard door into the fog that is coming up off the flats, and the door bangs on its one hinge behind them. The empty tin stands on the tank in the dark, its painted girl facing out at us, keeping nothing. We go along the beams to say who it was that knew the girl on the label was no one, who sealed the tins at the last of the line, who had the whole of the tin in her mouth an hour ago — and we come to the place at the front where she was telling it, and there is no telling there, and no one to say her name, and the girl on the label smiles out of the oil-shine at a floor that cannot any longer remember which of its own women taught it what a tin was for.


---

# Chapter 6 — Preia-mar de sizígia

The notary's office is up the town, over the water that runs under the Cais, and we have her there in pieces, the way we have her anywhere off our own ground: the scrape of the chair as she sits, the click of a pen that is not hers, Melo's voice which we knew when it was a boy's voice and knew when it broke, and between these the shape of her, Idalina, of the line, come to the table where the paper is. She has the pen. This is the thing we lean toward hardest across the whole morning — she has the pen in her hand and the paper is under it and there is one place left on it that is white, one line at the bottom the width of a name, and her name is a short name, it would take her no time at all. Melo turns the page toward her and smooths it with the flat of his hand the way you smooth a thing you are sorry to be giving away, and he says, quietly, so that we have to lean to catch it, that this is the last of it, that once she signs here the *escritura* can be drawn and the man from Lisbon can have his building, and she can go home. He says *go home* and means the other place, the panelled place, the tap she left running. He does not mean here. Even Melo, who loved her mother, does not think of here as her home, and we hear him not think it, and we hold very still.

She does not sign.

She holds the pen over the white line and we are all of us leaning, forty of us, every one, the whole roof tipped toward the town like water in a tilted tank, and we are so sure of what the hand will do that we have already done it for her — we have already heard the pen go down, we have already felt the small scratch of it, we have already begun to say among ourselves *there, it is done, she will be gone before the sheets are cold, three days, we read her last page the first evening and here is the last page turning.* We say it in Aurélia's voice, which is the voice we use for the things we are sure of. *We have read her last page.* And the hand does not go down.

The hand sets the pen on the table, beside the paper, not on it.

She has thought of something. We cannot see the thought — the faces are the last thing we see and up here over the town-water her face is a smudge, a pale place with a dark where the eyes are — but we see the hand withdraw, and we see her sit back, and we hear her say to Melo, in the low even voice she uses that gives us nothing, that she would like the rest of it first. The papers. Whatever is his to give her of the family before it is all signed away, she says, she would like to have it. To look at. She does not say why. We do not know why. We decide, in the leaning, that she wants to be sure of her price — that she is a careful woman of the careful new world and will not put her name to a thing until she has counted every corner of it, and this is a thing we approve of, we the counters, we who count ourselves along the beams; and we are wrong; we do not know we are wrong; it is the machine in her, the listening in her, the part of her that will not let a sound go by untaken, and it has turned, without her knowing why, toward us. She wants what is left of us that can be held in a box. She does not know she wants it. We do not know she wants it. Only the wanting is real, under the both of us, and neither the living woman nor the dead in the roof can read it.

Melo goes to the cupboard.

*

The box is a shoe-box grown large, or a wine-box, cardboard once and now something stiffer, tied with the flat brown tape they tied the estate in, and Melo carries it to the table with both hands the way you carry a thing that has water in it and might spill. He has had it a year. He tells her so — a year, since Graça, since the winter Graça went — and we feel Graça come forward at the sound of the name, come forward out of the pack to the front edge of us over the town-water where it costs her, and look at the box, and we do not know what Graça knows about the box, and Graça does not say. He has had it a year and not opened it past the first fold, he says, because it was plainly the old accounts, the fábrica's books, and a man does not read another family's accounts for pleasure, and there is no money in them worth the reading, the fábrica having made nothing but rust and this smell. He says *this smell* and we know the smell he means, the smell that is in the box, that is in all our paper, the smell of brine gone into ledger-glue and dried there, the smell of a thing preserved by the same salt that is eating it. He pushes the box across the table to her, over the paper she has not signed, and says she must sign for the box instead — here, this line, that she has received it — and she signs *that*. She signs for the box and not for the sale. The pen goes down easily for the box. We watch the short name go onto the wrong line, the receiving line, and we do not understand what we have watched, and we tell ourselves it does not matter, it is only a box of the old accounts, and tomorrow she will sign the other line, the white one, and be gone.

The box goes into her arms. She is dismissive of it — we have that clearly, the set of the shoulders carrying a thing you mean to set down and not open, a duty-weight — and she says something to Melo that makes him almost smile and not quite, and she goes out with the box against her chest, down the stair, into the square where the water does not run and she goes thin to us, a footfall, a car door, an engine tuned to nothing, gone.

Inside the box, though we do not know it, though Melo does not know it, though she does not know it, tied in among the fábrica's dead accounts, is the other book. The one with the girls in it. It goes past us in her arms, a hand's breadth from the whole leaning weight of us, and not one of us feels it go, because we long ago stopped feeling it, we long ago made ourselves a roof with no room in it for that book, and it rides out into the square in the arms of the last of the line, closed, and we let it go the way we let the smoke-name go, reaching and not reaching, and we say among ourselves *only the old accounts*, and it is the truest lie we have told all day.

*

That evening the man is in the fábrica after the light goes, and she comes back to it.

We did not expect her back at the fábrica after dark; we had her put away for the night, over the café, feeding her machine.
