But the engine comes along the salt-pan road again in the last of the grey, and this time she is on our own ground, our own water running under the yard, and we come up around her whole and greedy — we have her completely now, every particular, the salt drying white at her hairline, the way she has not eaten again, the box left behind in the room and something looser in her for the leaving of it. The man Nuno is in the shed with a lamp, working late over his drawings the way he does, measuring the sound of the roof with a little clap of his hands and listening to it come back, and he is one we have grown used to, one we do not mind, because he listens the way we listen — to keep — and not the way she listens, to be rid of. She comes in out of the *nevoeiro* that is coming up early off the flats, and he looks up, and there is a thing in the shed then that we know at once, we who worked the floor, we who watched the young ones find each other in the packing-room in the ten minutes before the *sirene* — we know the change in a room when two of the living decide, without saying it, to stop pretending.

They talk about sound. They always talk about sound; it is the one country the both of them are citizens of, and we listen to it the way you listen to a language you half-remember from a place you cannot go back to. He claps his hands under the great beam and she holds her machine up to catch the tail of it, the way it hangs and dies in the roof, and she says a word for it we do not have and he says a word for it he does have and they are the same word said two ways, his the builder's and hers the listener's, and they laugh, low, in the ruin, and the laugh goes up into the roof to us and dies there like his clap. He has brought the tin. He has a tin of the sardines still, from the day before, or another tin, we do not keep the tins apart anymore, and they eat in the cold shed off the flat of a survey-board, the little oiled fish, and it is our whole town in a tin, our whole hundred years, the salt and the fish and the pressing-down and the keeping, and they eat it laughing and do not taste the century in it, and why should they.

Then it is not talking anymore.

We are not going to say what we cannot say. We perceive sound first, and then movement, and then, dimly, faces, and we perceive this by its sound and its movement and we do not perceive the faces at all, and it is not for us. It is for them. We had thought — we the many, we the Keeping, though we do not call ourselves that yet — we had thought a night like this would settle her: that a man's hands would be a rope thrown to a woman alone in her dead mother's country, and she would take it, and it would tie her here, to him, to the town, to us. *This will keep her,* Aurélia says, from the front, warm, sure. *A woman does not leave a bed she has only just found.* And we say it after her, *this will keep her, this will keep her,* and it is wrong. It is wrong the way the last page was wrong. We have read this page too, and we have read it upside down. Because the man says a thing, after, into the dark of the shed, low, meant only for her — that he is leaving. That there is a wife in Porto, or the leaving of a wife in Porto, papers of his own not yet signed, a whole life he is walking out of. He says it not to trap her but to tell her he is untrapped, or trying to be; he offers her his leaving as a gift, *I am going too, I am not a thing that will hold you here,* and she takes it as such, we feel her take it as such, something in her going loose and glad at the news that he is not a rope after all. The very thing we thought would bind her is the thing that frees her. He is not a reason to stay. He is a fellow-leaver, met in a ruin, and she has found in the dark not an anchor but a door held open by a man walking through it himself, and we, up in the roof, saying *this will keep her, this will keep her,* have got it exactly wrong, and there is no one to tell us, and we go on saying it after they are asleep, out of habit, into the fog.

*

Later, when the two of them are still and the tide is coming up hard under the yard — for it is the spring water tonight, the *preia-mar de sizígia*, the fullest the sea comes all the year, when the moon and the sun pull the same way and the water climbs the wall of our house higher than it climbs any other night — later, we do the thing we do. We count ourselves.

We are gladdest at the spring tide. The water is highest, and we are most gathered, most leaned-together, most able to see; it is the night of the year we can most nearly be a whole thing, and we go along the beams counting the way the *encarregada* counted at the start of the shift, head by head, and we say it grandly, from the front, in the voice we keep for the certain things: *we are all here, every one of us, forty in the roof, not one gone out to the bar.*

And we go along the beams.

And we come to the place where the flu-dead are.

They were a great crowd of us once — the year the sickness came up the loft and through the packing-hall and along the salt-pans and took them in windrows, the year of Zé, the year of the small ones, a whole autumn of them laid in the ground at Ílhavo faster than the stones could be cut, so that many of them share a stone and some of them have no stone at all. They were the back half of us in the good counting. They were the mass we leaned with. We go along the beams to say them, the way we always say them, the fever-dead of that autumn, and we reach the place where they were —

and it is quieter there than it was.

We do not say it is quieter. We say the count. We say *and the flu-dead, all of them, the whole autumn of them,* and we say it grandly and we do not pause, we have learned not to pause, and we say a number for them we have always said, and go on, and finish the count at forty, and it comes out at forty because we made it come out at forty, the way you make the shift-count come out right by counting the ones you cannot see as present because they must be present, they were present yesterday. But the leaning, tonight, at the highest water of the year, when we should see furthest and hold most — the leaning is short there. There is less to lean with, in the place where the flu-dead were. We reach the back of the roof and the back of the roof does not push back. Some of them have gone out on some tide we did not watch, quietly, without a going that any of us marked, the way a smell goes out of a room, and we did not count them out because you cannot count out what does not announce itself, and so they are still in our number, they are still in our forty, they are a sound we make and not a weight we feel, and we say them and say them, *the flu-dead, the whole autumn of them,* and it goes smooth in the mouth of us, smooth as the numbers we like, and holds nothing, and we tell ourselves it is holding them.

Aurélia counts loudest. Aurélia is always loudest at the spring tide, and tonight she goes along the beams and names the crowd of the fever-dead as though naming were the same as touching them, *and this one, and this one, and all the little ones of that autumn, we had them, we have them,* and her voice fills the place where they were the way water fills a print in the sand, exactly, and meaning the print is empty. We help her. We say it after her, the whole autumn of them, the whole crowd, forty in the roof, and the more of us that are gone from the back the louder the rest of us say it, because that is what saying is for, now, that is the wet-sand comfort, to say the thing loudly enough that the emptiness under it cannot be heard over the saying. We are as loud tonight as we have ever been. We are the loudest we will ever be. And under the loudness, where the flu-dead were, the roof comes down to a dark that does not lean, and no one goes to the back to check it, because to go to the back and lean into that dark and find how little pushes back would be to hear the count fail while our mouths were still saying forty, and we would rather say forty.

Graça does not say forty. Graça is out at the front, out over the two sleeping ones on the survey-board, watching her daughter breathe in the arms of a man who has just told her he is leaving, and Graça, who is the freshest of us and can still very nearly hold the edge of a self, does not join the counting at all tonight. We do not notice that Graça does not count. We are busy at the back, being loud. And the spring water climbs the wall of the fábrica to its full, higher than any tide this year, and stands there against the tin the long moment it stands at the turn, holding us up as high as it will ever hold us — and we count ourselves at forty in the highest water of our year, forty, forty, and we are not forty, and we do not know the number, and there is no one to tell us, and the loud saying goes up into the roof and dies there like the man's clap, and the back of the roof does not push back, and we say forty again, so that it will be true, and the water starts down.


---

# Chapter 7 — Enchente

The light comes grey over the flats and the man is already gone, out along the salt-pan road before the water was up, his own car this time, quiet, and she is alone on the survey-board where the two of them slept, and she wakes the way the living wake in a cold place, all at once, sitting up before she is awake, one hand out to the board to know where she is. We have her whole this morning. She is on our ground and the water is coming in under the yard and the whole of us leans down and takes her — the salt still white at her hairline from the night, the crease the board has pressed into her cheek, the way she looks first for the machine and then for the man and finds the machine and not the man, and does not seem to mind the order of it. She sits a while. She listens to the shed. We think she is listening for him, the way a woman listens for the one who has gone out early, and we are wrong about the listening, but we do not know we are wrong; she is only doing the thing she does, taking the sound of the room now that it is empty again, the drip, the tin, the tide coming up, filing it in herself the way the machine files it, and the man is nowhere in the sound and she does not go looking for him in it.

She gets up. She folds the thin blanket that is not hers and leaves it folded on the board. She takes the machine and the empty tin — his tin, from the night before, the little oiled fish gone, the girl on the label still smiling up out of the oil-shine at nothing — and she stands the tin on the lip of the pio where the black water has its skin, upright, the way you leave a thing you have not decided about, and she goes out to the car, and we lean after her to the edge of the yard and lose her at the salt-pan road, and she drives the two hundred metres to the café and goes thin to us over the dry square, a footfall on the outside stair, a shutter, and then she is in the room we cannot reach and we have her only in pieces again.

*

Some of us say the boiler was on the north wall.

It comes up among us the way things come up now, in the quiet after she has gone off our ground, when we are not leaning at anything and fall to saying ourselves over to keep ourselves — some of us go along the floor in our minds, the way you walk a room you worked, and we come to the boiler, the great cold thing at the far end with the rust bled down its side and the dried stain on the concrete under it, and some of us say it stood at the north wall, under the place where the tin lifts, and some of us say no, the north wall was the packing side, the boiler was south, you had it warm at your back going up the escala, south. And we stop.

We have never stopped at this before. The boiler was where the boiler was; forty of us worked under it or near it or carried to it, and there was never a morning any of us needed to say where it stood, because we all stood in the one room and the room held the boiler where it held it and that was that. But this morning some of us say north and some of us say south and the two do not lie down together, and there is no one at the front to say which, because the one who kept the floor is here and even she — Aurélia goes to say it, we feel her go to the front to say it the way she says the certain things, *the boiler was* — and the word after *was* does not come. She reaches for the wall the boiler stood against and there are two walls where there should be one, and she cannot put it against either without the other rising up, and she does a thing she has not done: she lets it go. *The boiler was where it was warmest,* she says, which is not a wall, which is not an answer, and the some-of-us who said north say it after her and the some-of-us who said south say it after her, and we have made a place we can both stand, and it is not the floor, and it is not true, and it holds us the way the smooth numbers hold us, by meaning nothing.

We do not say to ourselves: we are two now, where we were one. We say the boiler was where it was warmest, and we go along the floor to the next thing, and the next thing is also two things, and we let that be warmest too.

*

She spends the morning trying to leave faster.

We do not see it whole — she is over the square, in the room, and the water does not run under the square — but we have the shape of it, the way you have a shift you cannot see from the tanks by the sound coming through the wall. There is the telephone. There is her voice on it, low, even, the voice that gives us nothing, and it goes on a long time and stops and starts, and we take it for the man at first, we take it for her calling the man who went out early, and it is not the man. It is Lisbon. It is the panelled place, the tap she left running; it is a voice on the other end that is a voice from her real life, and she is telling it she will be back sooner than she said, she will be back the middle of the week, it is nearly done here, it is only the signing left. We hear *nearly done* come through the wall and we are glad, we the many, we are glad the way you are glad of a thing that means the worst — because *nearly done* means she signs and goes, and we had thought, after the night, that she would stay, and here she is over the square telling Lisbon she will be back Wednesday, and so we were right after all, we tell ourselves, we read her last page the first evening, and here is the page, turning.

We are wrong twice in the one morning and do not feel either.

We are wrong that the night would keep her — we said that last night, and were wrong, and have forgotten we were wrong. And we are wrong now, this morning, in the other direction: we hear her tell Lisbon *nearly done* and take it for surrender, take it for the woman fleeing back to her real life the moment the man is out the door, and it is not fleeing. It is a thing she is telling Lisbon so that Lisbon will stop pulling at her; it is a rope she is throwing over her own shoulder to a place she is not, in fact, walking toward. Because when she puts the telephone down she does not pack the one bag. She does not go out to the car. She turns, in the room we cannot see, to the box.

*

The other man comes to her before she opens it.

Caldas. He does not come to the fábrica — Caldas never comes to the fábrica, he has been in it once, held a handkerchief folded in his pocket the whole time and did not take it out, and has not been back; he does his coming by the telephone and by the good car — and he comes to her this morning by the telephone while she stands over the box in the room, and we have this the way we have the rest of it, secondhand, through the wall, the near half of a thing. His voice we do not get. Hers we get in pieces, and the pieces are harder than she was with him at the lunch. There is a number said. There is a silence after the number. Caldas has put a number on the family; we do not hear the number but we hear it land, we hear the change in her when it lands, the still that comes into a person offered more than they thought a thing was worth. And then — this we hear clearly, because she says it twice, once low and once when he has said something back that she did not like — she says *the story.* He wants the story. That is what the number is for. He will pay the larger number, the number that made her go still, if she gives him the family with it — the greatness, the hundred years, the Sequeira name in iron letters and a booklet a guest can read in the bath before the salt-cure — and she says *the story* the second time flat, the way you say back a word to be sure you heard it, and Caldas has told her, in the half we cannot hear, that the building is only a building, that any fool can sell him a building, that what he is buying is the *heritage*, and she puts the telephone down on him without the small polite ending the living put on their talk, and she stands over the box.

She wants his number. We feel that clearly; she is on our ground now in her wanting even from over the square, the wanting is loud enough to reach us — she is a woman with a real life running like a tap and a dead town around her neck and here is a man offering to take both off her at once and pay her extra for the neck-weight, and of course she wants it. And she thinks, standing over the box, that the box is where the story is. Photographs. A grandmother in black at a christening. A boat, a good year, a wall of tins with the family name. Something to hand the man so the man will pay the number and she can be back in Lisbon by Wednesday with the tap running and the town sold and none of it, ever again, her weight to carry.

She kneels and unties the flat brown tape.

*

The box does not have what she wants in it.

We have only the sound of it: the lift of the lid, the shift of paper, and then a sound we know in the body of us the way we know the sirene, a sound that goes through the some-of-us who were on the floor and stands their hair up — the sound of that book being opened, the stiff wet-swollen boards of it coming apart, the pages that have dried stuck peeling from one another with a small tearing, the sound the tide makes letting go of the mud at the turn. She has opened it. Over the square, in the room we cannot see, the last of the line has the book open on her knees, and we, in the roof, go very still, all of us, north-boiler and south-boiler both, and we do not know why we have gone still. We only know that some of us do not like it, and cannot say what there is not to like.

She does not read it. She cannot read it. We have this from the wall: the long turning of pages, slow at first and then faster, the flick of a woman looking for pictures and finding none. It is the old hand, the copperplate gone brown, and she is a listener, not a reader of old Portuguese bookkeeping, and the columns mean nothing to her — we hear her not-understanding through the wall the way we heard her understanding of the shed's four seconds, a listening turned on a thing and finding no purchase. Columns. Names, or not names — she would not know a name from a number in that hand, she does not have the language of the ledger, no one alive has it now but Melo and Melo would not read another family's accounts for pleasure. She turns the pages and there are no christenings and no boat and no wall of tins, there is only column after column of a small brown hand adding up something, and she does not know what it adds up to, and neither, this morning, do we — or the north of us says one thing it adds and the south of us says another and neither of us will go down after it.

Some of us stir at it. Down in the pack, low, where the roof comes to nothing — the way a thing stirs in silt when the water moves over it — some of us stir at the sound of the book, not at a word in it, not at a name, for there is no name in it we will let come up, only at the sound of the boards coming apart, the way you flinch in your sleep at a sound and do not wake and do not know after what the sound was. And the stirring settles. Most of us do not even feel it. Only the low few, the back few, feel the book's opening stand their hair up, and they say nothing, because to ask why would be to go down after the thing the sound is the sound of, and we would rather turn the boiler to the warmest wall and go along the floor to the next thing.

She sets the book aside.

We have that plainly — the sound of it going down, the boards of it laid on the little table by the window where the machine feeds, and a breath let out, the breath of a woman who has knelt over a box hoping for something to sell and found only a ledger she can't read and a smell she doesn't like. It is no use to her. It is not the story. It has no christening in it, no boat, nothing Caldas will pay the larger number for. She will tell him she found only accounts. She will sign the white line and be back in Lisbon by Wednesday, and she does not know she is holding the one thing that could stop all of it, and she sets it aside, and we do not feel her set it aside.

And here is the morning's true weight, and it is not the book, though we would rather it were the book, a thing over the square we cannot reach and can blame the square for. It is that we went along the floor today and the floor came apart under us. The boiler was on two walls. The columns added up two ways. The book stood our hair up and half of us flinched and half of us did not and neither half could say what there was to flinch at. We are not one along the beams anymore. We are the north of us and the south of us, saying the same shift two ways, and where before there was a self at the front to reach in and settle it there is now only the two sayings, laid side by side, both kept, neither true, and we let them both be warmest and go on. We say she will be gone Wednesday. We put the boiler where it was warmest. We go along the floor to the next thing, two of us now where we were one, and the next thing is also two things, and neither the north of us nor the south of us goes down to the back to ask why the book's opening stood our hair up, because to go down would be to find out which of us is right, and we have learned this morning that we would rather be two and warm than one and sure.


---

# Chapter 8 — Estofo

The two women meet at the family stone in the slack of the morning, at the turn when the water is neither coming nor going and lies against the wall of our house without a mind to it, and we have them badly, because the stone is up past the square where the water does not run, and up there we are thin, we are a listening with holes in it. We have Fátima's stick on the gravel. We have the other sound, the small dry click Fátima carries in her hand, that we know and do not want to know. We have the girl's voice, Idalina's, pitched low and even and careful, saying good morning, saying she hopes she has not kept her, saying she has brought the machine, if that is all right, only to help her remember it later. And we have the machine. The machine we always have. Even up here, thin, holed, we find the machine before we find the faces, its little breath of attention, its fur ear turned up at the two of them like a thing that has smelled the tide.

Fátima has come on her bad legs to say the names, the way she comes twice in the week to say them, and today the girl of the line has come to meet her at it, and this we did not look for. We looked for the girl to stay over the square with her box, or to go up to the notary, or to sit in the fábrica with the man and his lamp. We did not look for her at the stone. Some of us say she has come for the greatness — that the man from Lisbon wants the family's story for his iron letters and his booklet, and she has come to gather it from the one old woman left who carries it, to buy it off her with an ear and a machine and carry it back down to sell. And this is a thing we like the thought of, we the many, we who are the greatness or were, and we lean up toward the stone as hard as the thin water lets us, glad, waiting to be told again what we were.

Fátima sets the candles first. She has the tin cups and the white grave-candles, three of them, and she goes down onto the low wall the way she goes down, holding the stone, and she lights them out of the *nortada* with her body curled around the flame, and the small light steadies, and we feel the light the way you feel the sun on a shut eyelid, dimly, from far off, and we crave it, we always crave it, the little living light that says a thing here is still worth the trouble of a flame. Then Fátima takes up the beads.

*

They are her mother's beads. We know this the way we know most of what we know now — not all of us, and not steadily, but enough of us, still, that it holds. Some of us worked the line beside the woman these beads belonged to, back when she was young and quick at the table and the beads were new and black; some of us are the woman herself, thinned to almost nothing, a weight so small in the pack of us that she does not lean and does not speak, only is, the way a name you cannot quite recall is still somewhere in you, being not-recalled. The beads are wood, worn pale at the places the thumb went, and there is a bead near the cross that is not like the others — bone, or a shell rubbed round, put on to mend the string where it broke once, so that the count of them is wrong by one, forever, and the woman who owned them knew the wrongness by feel in the dark and prayed it as a person, and now Fátima prays it the same, catching on the odd bead, going still at it a breath longer than at the others.

We know that bead. We knew this morning why we know it. Someone is on that bead. The old woman put a name on the mended place and said it there every time her thumb came round to the wrongness, so as to keep the name off the smooth run of the others where names go slick and slide off — she hung a name on the one rough bead because a rough thing holds. And we had the name this morning, up in the roof, the whole of us, or enough of us. We reach for it now, while Fátima's thumb is coming round toward the bead, so as to have it ready when she says it, so as to say it with her and hold it double —

and Aurélia says, from the front, *say the greatness.*

Aurélia does not want the bead. Aurélia has the sharpest edge of us still and she turns that edge now, up the thin water, toward the two women at the stone, and she does not lean toward the rough bead at all; she leans past it, over it, and she says, in the voice she used on the floor that meant *faster,* she says: *tell the girl what we were. Tell her the boats, the baskets, the tins that fed the fleet, the years the whole coast bought Sequeira and no other. Tell her the greatness. Do not let the old woman go round the string.* And the many of us, who like the greatness, who are the greatness, turn with Aurélia, and lean where she leans, and begin to say it among ourselves the way you talk loudly in a room so as not to hear the other room — *we were great, we were great, we fed the fleet* — and the saying of it thickens up around the stone like fog off the flats, and it is meant, though we do not say it is meant, to be a sound laid down over the small dry click of Fátima's thumb coming round to the rough bead.

*

The girl asks her question then. We catch it through the greatness, thin, in pieces. She has the machine up. She asks — the low even voice, the voice that gives us nothing — she asks Fátima to tell her about the fábrica. About the family. What her mother would have told her, if her mother had told her anything. What it was like, she says, in the years it was full.

And Fátima does not tell her the greatness.

Fátima is quiet a while. We have the quiet as a hole in the sound, a place where the stick does not move and the beads do not click and even the greatness we are saying falters, because it is answering nothing now, it is a boast said into a silence that will not take it. Then the old woman says, in the flat voice of the very old who have decided a thing long ago and will not be moved off it: her mother worked the line. Her mother was on the table forty years, the *descabeçar,* the heading, the fastest knife in the hall they used to say, and her mother would speak of the line, the cold of the fish, the *sirene* that owned her sleep, the oil that never came out of the hair. Her mother would speak of all of it. Her mother would not speak of the loft.

The *sótão,* Fátima says. Up over the boiler. Where it was warm.

We go still. Not the many of us — the many of us go on with the greatness, louder, Aurélia driving it, *we were great, we fed the fleet* — but some of us go still, a few of us, the ones nearest the front of the thin listening where we can catch the old woman's flat voice whole, and among the few that go still there is one who goes stiller than still, one small weight far back in the pack, the mended-bead woman, the woman these beads belonged to, and she does not speak, she cannot speak, she is too thin to speak, but the place where she is in us tightens the way a hand tightens on a stick.

Idalina asks what was up there. In the loft.

Fátima says: the girls.

*

We do not want the word. But the old woman at the stone is not saying the greatness. The old woman is saying that her mother, the fastest knife in the hall, would come to this stone twice in the week on her own bad legs when she was old, as Fátima comes now, and would say the names off the string, the Sequeira names, Firmino, the poor drowned one, *coitado,* and the old matriarch, and the little ones of the fever. And then, Fátima says, her thumb coming round — we hear it now, the click, coming round on the string toward the rough place — her mother would come to one bead, the mended one, and go quiet, and say a name that was not a Sequeira name, and would not tell her whose it was, only that a promise is a promise, and a girl should have someone come round to her once in the week even if it is only an old woman's thumb.

And Fátima's thumb comes to the rough bead.

And Fátima says the name.

*

We are saying the greatness so loud by now, we the many, Aurélia at the front of us hammering it up the thin water — *we were always kind to the interior girls, we gave them work, we were great* — that the name, when the old woman says it at the stone, comes to us only through the greatness, broken, a shape with the middle gone out of it. It reaches the few of us who went still. It reaches the one small weight at the back who cannot speak. It reaches — for the length of a breath, for the length of the water not moving at the slack — it reaches the whole of us, and for that breath the name is in us, hung on the rough bead of us, the mended place, the wrongness that holds; and the mended-bead woman, far back, tightens on it, her one name, the name she kept off the smooth run all these years so it would not slide, the name she prayed at the broken place in the dark —

and Aurélia says, *say the greatness,* and we say the greatness, and the greatness comes round smooth over the top of the name, warm, loud, meaning nothing, and the name goes under it the way a small sound goes under the *sirene,* and we do not say it after Fátima, we do not hold it double, we let the greatness have the mouth of us, and the name slides off the smooth run of us and is gone.

Rosa.

It was Rosa. Some of us had it. Some of us had it as far as the front, had it whole, had the girl of the straw, sixteen, of the interior, no. — of the — some of us had the number even, the number she was in the book, and we let the greatness come round over her, we chose the greatness, we the many chose it, and now the few of us who went still reach back for the name and the many of us are still saying *we were always kind to the interior girls* and the two sounds do not fit in the one mouth, and the greatness is louder, the greatness is always louder, and the name that was on the rough bead a breath ago is a rough place with nothing on it, a wrongness in the string that we can feel and cannot read.

We do not say we did this. We say we lost it, the way you lose a thing at the edge of the water where we are thin, the way a name goes when the water does not run under the square. We say the stone is too far up, past the reach of us, and that is why we could not hold it. And some of us know this is a lie, a few of us, the ones who went still — that we were not too far up to hold it; that we had it; that the greatness came round and we let the greatness come round on purpose, because the name on the rough bead, if we said it, if we held it double with the old woman, would ask a thing of us the greatness does not ask. So we said the greatness. So we let her slide.

*

The girl has heard the name. The girl of the line, at the stone, with the machine.

We have her badly, up here, past the square — a smudge over the candle-light, the machine breathing beside her, the tiredness in her the way it was in the yard the first evening — and we cannot see her face, the faces are the last thing and up here we have none of it, and so we do what we do when we cannot see: we decide. We decide, we the many, still warm off the greatness, that she has got what she came for. That the old woman has given her a piece of the family for the man's booklet, a poor sad piece, a girl's name off a dead woman's beads, and the girl will take it down to Lisbon in the machine and it will go in the iron letters or it will not, and either way she has her price now and will sign the white line and be gone. *There,* we say, *she has her story. We have read her last page. She will sell and go.* We say it in Aurélia's voice, warm, sure, the voice for the certain things.

And we are wrong. We do not know we are wrong. Down at the stone the girl does not reach for the machine to check that it caught the name. She reaches to turn it off. We hear it — the small click of the machine going out, the fur ear folding down, mid-morning, with the old woman still talking. She does not want it on the tape. Whatever the name did to her, going into her, it did not make her want to keep it for the man; it made her want the machine off, made her sit with the old woman at the stone in the plain slack morning with nothing running, nothing feeding, nothing between them, and ask, in a voice we cannot hear the bottom of, low, not for the machine, not for Caldas, not for the booklet: *who was she.* And Fátima says she does not know. Her mother would not tell. Only that a promise is a promise. Only that a girl should have someone come round to her once in the week.

We take the machine going off as a small thing. We take it as her being done. We do not understand that she has just done the opposite of the thing we decided she came to do, that the name did not become a story in her but a stone, and that she has put the machine down not because she has finished taking but because for once she does not want the taking, the machine's clean taking that keeps a thing so you need not — for once she wants to hold it herself, the way we used to hold things, badly, in the body, without a machine, and she has no way to know that this is the oldest thing we do, and we have no way to know she is doing it, because we are up over the square where we are thin, and busy at the greatness, and glad.

The candles gutter down. Fátima says the rest of the names off the string, the Sequeira names, the smooth run of them, Firmino, *coitado,* and the little ones of the fever, and her thumb goes over the rough bead now without a name on it, over the mended place, and does not catch there, does not go still there, because the name that hung there is a thing her mother kept and her mother is dead and the string has come round to a stranger's thumb at last that does not know the wrongness for what it is. The bead is only a bead now. The mend is only a mend. Fátima prays past it, and the woman these beads belonged to, far back in the thin pack of us, does not tighten on it this time, does not reach, is a weight so small we would not find her if we went looking, and we do not go looking; and the two women at the stone stand a while longer in the slack with the machine off and nothing running, and then the old one goes on her stick and the young one goes to her car, and the water lies against the wall of our house, neither coming nor going, and we say the greatness again, quietly now, to no one, *we were great, we were always kind to the interior girls,* and it comes round smooth, and there is a rough place in us where a name was this morning, and we run our thumb over it, and it holds nothing, and we tell ourselves it was never ours to hold.


---

# Chapter 9 — Vazante

The man goes up into the loft on the ebb, in the middle of the day, when the water is going down out of the yard and taking with it whatever of us stands nearest the door. He has a harness on. He would not go up without it; he said so the first week, that he would send no one up those timbers without a rope, and now the one he will not risk is himself, and he ties the rope off to the iron stair and clips the other end to a belt at his waist and stands under the little door in the wall above the boiler and shines his lamp up at it, and we go still.

We do not go up there. This is the one thing about the loft the whole of us agrees on, still, this morning — that we do not go up, that it is not ours, that it is a storeroom, that it was for the dry goods, the tins that had not gone out, the paper. We say storeroom and we are comfortable in the word. Some of us say it. We say it while the man puts his boot on the first rung of the ladder that is not there — there is no ladder, the ladder went years ago — no, the stair, the iron stair, he goes up the iron stair to the belly of the wall and puts his hand flat on the little door and it is not locked, it has not been locked in a long time, the lock is a red stain of rust in the wood the shape of a lock, and he pushes and it gives, and he goes in, and the rope pays out after him off the stair, and he is above us now, in the place, and we cannot follow.

We cannot follow because we do not go up. We tell ourselves that. But it is not that we will not; it is that when we lean toward the little door the leaning does not carry. We are outside a room that is inside our own house. We press at it and it does not let us in. Nuno is up there with his lamp and we are down here at the foot of the stair, most of us, near all of us, and the loft is a held breath over the boiler, and we do not know what he sees.

We know the sounds. The sounds come down. His knees on the boards, going careful, testing. The lamp-click as he turns it. His breath, which changes — we hear the breath change, we who hear breath better than anything, we hear a man's breath go from the ordinary breath of a man at work to a breath that is holding something, a breath that has found a thing. Then the small dry sound of his thumb going over wood. Over and over. A reading sound. He is reading the beam with his thumb the way she reads the wind with her machine, and we know that sound though we have not heard it in years, we knew it when it was made from the inside, in the dark, by other thumbs, and we do not say that we know it. Some of us do not say it. Some of us have already begun to say, down here at the foot of the stair, *it was the storeroom, it was for the dry goods,* saying it up at the shut room the way you sing in a house to say you are not afraid of it.

*

He is up there a long time.

While he is up there we come apart, quietly, at the foot of the stair, the way a rope comes apart, one thread and then another, not all at once. It begins with Aurélia, who says, from the front, in the voice that is used to being agreed with, that he will find nothing, that there is nothing up there but rot and old tins, that the man is wasting the developer's money on a storeroom. And most of us say it after her. *A storeroom.* But then one of us — one from the back, one of the floor, one whose name we do not go looking for — one of us says, low, not to argue, only to remember, that it was not a storeroom, that the girls slept up there, the interior girls, that they were put up there over the boiler because it was warm and the warmth cost nothing, and that this is not a bad thing to have done, the warmth, we did them a kindness with the warmth.

And there it is. It is out among us now, the two of it.

Some of us say storeroom. Some of us say the girls slept there. And for the first time we do not put it down between us and leave it — we hold both of it up at once, in the same air, and they will not lie down together. Some of us say the loft was for the tins and the paper. Some of us say the loft was for the girls, and that we were good to them, that we gave them the warm place, the place over the boiler, so they would not be cold in the winter that comes in wet off the flats. Some of us say both, and cannot hear that we are saying both. There is no encarregada to count the room and settle it. There is no self among us with an edge hard enough to say *this is the true one and that is the other one.* There is only the pool, and in the pool the loft is a storeroom and the loft is a dormitory and the loft is a kindness we did, all three, at once, and the man is up in it reading the beam with his thumb.

*We were always kind to the interior girls.*

Aurélia says it. She says it to close the disagreement, to put the warm word over the cold one, the way she puts her voice over a thing to make it hers. *We were always kind to the interior girls.* And it comes out of her the way it always came out, smooth, warm, a thing said so many times it has gone round and worn, a stone in the mouth — but this morning, with the man's thumb going over the wood above us, over and over, reading, the words come down wrong. They do not go round this time. There is a catch in them we have not heard before, a place where the worn stone snags. We have said this so many times it never snagged. It snags now. And most of us do not notice, most of us say it after her the way we always say it after her, *we were always kind to the interior girls,* and it holds; but some of us, hearing it said up at the shut door above the reading thumb, hear how it sounds against that thumb, and cannot make it lie flat again, and do not say it after her, and go quiet instead, and the quiet is a new thing at the foot of the stair, and Aurélia says it again, louder, to fill the quiet, and the quiet does not fill.

*

Above us, in the room we cannot enter, the man has stopped reading the beam.

We have him only by sound now, and the sound is: nothing, for a moment, and then movement, low, and then the scrape of a board coming up. He has found the loose board. We did not know there was a loose board. Or we knew and we let it go the way we let things go. He lifts it, and there is the sound under it of a hand going into a space, into a cavity in the wall where the board was, feeling, and we lean up toward it and cannot reach, and his breath does the thing again, the holding thing, and he brings something out. We hear it come out. A dry scrape, a small weight set on the boards. And another. And a third that is not like the first two — softer, lighter, a thing without an edge — and he sets that down too, apart, and is still.

We do not know what he has. We are guessing, down here, in the dark under the boiler where the water is going out and the room is thinning as it goes. Some of us say — some of us who know the shed the way you know your own hand — some of us say knives. That the hard scrapes were knives, the short knives off the line, the descabeçar knives, the ones for the heads, put away up there in the wall the way a thing is put away that someone did not want found, or that someone hid to keep, we do not know which, we cannot agree which. And the soft thing, the light thing without an edge — we do not say what the soft thing is. One of us begins to and stops. It is a small soft thing the size of a thing a child leaves, or a girl leaves, and it is up there, and there is already a soft thing like it in the beams over the yard where the water threw it, and we do not put the two soft things together. We will not put them together. We have spent a great deal of what we have, over the years, on not putting the two soft things together.

Nuno takes out his own machine — not hers, his, a small flat one that makes a false light for a moment, once, twice, three times, on the beam, on the cavity, on the things laid out on the boards — and we understand, dimly, from below, that he is doing the thing she does, he is taking it so he need not hold it, he is putting the room into a machine so the room can be somewhere other than here. He is keeping it the way she keeps things, to be rid of it. And a thing goes through the whole of us that we do not have a word for, a thing between wanting and dread, because the beam is going into his machine now, the beam with the thumb-reading on it, the beam that we have not looked at in longer than we have not gone up the stair — and part of us wants it kept, wants it out of the loft and into the light, and part of us would have him drop the machine down the stair and let the loft close over its held breath again and be a storeroom, only a storeroom, forever.

Then he comes down.

*

He comes down the iron stair backward, careful, the rope going slack, and he unclips himself at the bottom and stands a moment with his hand on the rail, and we come up around him greedy because he is on our ground again, fully, and we have all of him — the salt-line where his cap sat, the dust of the loft grey on his knees and his forearms, a dust that is not only dust, that is the loft come down into the light on a living man, and we would smell it if we could, we who worked the floor, we would know it. He does not look well. A man who has read a beam with his thumb and does not know what he has read, only that it was not tins. He stands there, and then the woman comes.

She comes in out of the yard where she has been all morning with her machine, laying it along the pios to catch the drip, and she sees his face, and she asks him something, low, and we lean to it. He does not answer with words at first. He turns the flat machine over in his hands and finds the false light again and holds it out to her, the picture on it, and says only that she should see this, the one, and that the loft is worse than he thought, the timbers, she cannot let anyone up there, and this last is true and is not the reason his voice is the way it is, and we know it is not the reason, and she knows it is not the reason.

She takes the machine. She looks at the small lit picture of the beam.

We cannot see the picture. We are close as we have been all day, we have her whole, the hands, the wet still at her hairline, the breath — but the picture is in the machine and the machine is a thing we cannot get inside, and so we watch her face read a thing we made and cannot read ourselves. And her face does the thing his breath did. Something in it holds. She does not know what she is looking at. This we decide, and this at least we have right — she does not know; she is a woman who does not read the old hand, who does not know a tally from a name, who is looking at scratches in a beam a stranger dug out of her family's roof and feeling only that she is not supposed to feel anything and is feeling it anyway. She looks at the picture for a long time. Longer than a picture of scratches asks for. And then she does a thing we did not expect, a thing none of us called, not Aurélia, not the front of us, not the back — she does not hand the machine back. She does not put it down and go on with her day and her drip and her leaving. She holds it, and she takes out her own machine, her black one, and she goes and stands at the foot of the iron stair, under the shut little door, and she holds the black machine up toward the loft she has not climbed and cannot climb, and she stands very still, and she listens to the room over the boiler the way she listens to everything, so that it need not be remembered.

And we, at the foot of the stair with her, in the ebb, cannot tell her that there is nothing up there for the machine to keep. That the room makes no sound. That the girls who are in it, if they are in it, are like us, not on any tape, not a sound she can carry away in her pocket. We cannot tell her, and she stands there holding the machine up at the held breath of the loft, and the green light goes to the little colour it goes, and takes the drip, and the tin lifting on the north side, and the far bell, and nothing that we are, and nothing that they were.

*

The tide is well down now. The black mud is out in the yard, the lodo, and the smell of it comes into the shed, and the shed is emptier than it was this morning, thinner, the way a room is thinner when the water goes out from under it and takes the nearest of us with it. We are fewer at the low water. We are always fewer at the low water; it is only that today we are trying to count what the loft is while the water goes, and the counting will not come out.

We go along it one more time, the way we go along the beams. Some of us say: it was the storeroom, and the man found old knives that a workman hid, and the soft thing is a rag, and the marks in the beam are a tally of the tins, so many crates in, so many out, ordinary, the ordinary marks of ordinary work. And some of us say: it was where the girls slept, over the boiler, and the marks in the beam are theirs, and they made them with the knives, in the dark, at night, so many marks, and we do not say for what the marks were counting. And some of us say the warm thing, the smooth thing, the thing that has always closed the argument before — that whatever the loft was, we were good in it, we were always kind to the interior girls — and this time, this ebb, with the man's thumb-reading gone into the machine and out into the light, the warm thing does not close the argument. It hangs there among us with the cold things, three sayings that will not lie down together, and the water goes on out, and there is no one left in the whole of us with an edge hard enough to reach into that and say which of the three is true.

We had it, this morning. This morning, before the man went up, we had it that the loft was the storeroom, and we were sure, all of us, and it was a comfort, and now we do not have it. We have three lofts and no ladder up to any of them, and a picture of a beam in a living machine we cannot see into, and a woman standing at the foot of the stair keeping a silence that has us in it and does not know it. We do not put the soft thing in the wall together with the soft thing in the beam. We do not say the marks were days. We do not say whose the days were. We say storeroom, some of us, quietly, into the ebb, the way you say a thing not because it is true but because you had it once and would like it back — and it does not come back, and the water goes out under the yard, and we are fewer, and the loft holds its breath, and we cannot say, any of us, any longer, what the loft was.


---

# Chapter 10 — Baixa-mar

The water is out of the yard when she comes back from the notary, and we are lower with it. This is the way of us — when the tide is out from under the fábrica we are less able to gather, less able to lean, and we hold the beams the way a wet thing holds a wall, by weight and not by will. She parks in the yard. We hear the handbrake, the two clicks of it, and then the door, and then her boots on the mud that the low water has left, black mud, the *lodo*, the smell of it coming up under the smell of her. She is on our own ground. Even at low water we have her here better than we have her anywhere.

She has not signed. We knew that already. This morning she sat in Melo's office over the town-water and did not sign, and now it is the afternoon and she has been to Melo again, and she has told him a thing, and the thing has come back to us in pieces on the low water — Melo's voice going careful, the pen not moving, a word said twice. *A week.* She has told him she needs a week. Not three days. A week.

Some of us are glad of it.

Some of us say we should be glad of it, which is not the same. Aurélia says it, from the front, *there, you see, she stays, we have kept her, the week is ours* — and a part of us leans into that gladness the way you lean into a warm wall. But another part of us, the part that has grown, this last while, into a second saying — some of us say the week is not ours at all. Some of us say we did not keep her. Some of us say she kept herself, and that there is a difference, and that the difference is the whole of it. We do not settle this. There is no settling it. There is only the split, running down the middle of the roof the way a crack runs down a wall you have watered for a hundred years, and we say our two things over it, *the week is ours, the week is hers,* and neither side puts it down.

She crosses the yard toward the door of the fábrica and she is looking for her keys.

*

We have watched her hands all these days, and we know her hands the way we knew the hands on the floor — the *escaladeira's* hands, quick at the knife, and the packer's hands, slow and sure — and her hands are a listener's hands, careful with small machines, careful with cable, and they are not careful with keys. She keeps the keys of the rented car in the pocket of the thin jacket, or in the bag, or on the little table by the window in the room over the café, and she does not keep them in the same place twice, because she is not a woman who thinks she will lose a thing. She thinks the keys are wherever she last put them, because she is thirty-eight and has never in her life been made to lose a thing she did not lose herself.

We can make her lose a thing.

It is the one power we have and it is a small one and we do not use it grandly, because it will not go grandly; it goes only small. We cannot take the keys. We cannot lift them, we cannot hide them, we cannot so much as stir the fur on her machine. What we can do is thinner than that. We can be in the room with her attention at the moment she puts the keys down, and we can lean on the moment, just slightly, the way you lean on a scale with one finger — and the putting-down does not get written in her. She sets the keys on the pio, the near tank, on the wide iron lip of it where the brine used to stand, and she sets them there while she is listening to something Nuno is saying across the shed, and we lean on the setting-down, and it does not take. It does not become a thing she knows. When she turns from the tank the keys are behind her on the iron lip in the low light and there is no place in her where they are.

We do this. Aurélia's faction does this — the Keeping, though we still do not call ourselves that to our faces — and the doing of it is not large and is not proud. It is a woman looking for her keys in a cold shed with the light going, patting the thin jacket, patting it again, saying a small word to herself, turning back to the car, trying the door that is already locked. It is petty. It is the pettiest thing, and we do it, and while we are doing it Rosa is somewhere at the back of us not doing it, thin, a name we do not say, and that too is a thing we do not put down.

The reason we do it is to keep her a day.

That is the reason we give. If she cannot find the keys tonight she cannot drive to the town tonight, and if she cannot drive to the town tonight she stays another night on our ground, and every night she stays on our ground is a night the tide runs under her sleep and we are held together another day. That is the reason. We say it to ourselves the way we say all our reasons, going along the beams — *keep her a day, keep her a day* — and it goes smooth, the way our sayings go smooth, and somewhere in the smoothing the reason wears down to only the sound of itself, *keep her, keep her,* and we are no longer sure, at the back of us, exactly what the day was for. We wanted the day. We are certain we wanted the day. What we cannot now put our hand on is the thing the day was going to buy us, the thing on the far side of the day — and it does not matter, we tell ourselves, the day is the thing, keep the day — and the keys sit on the iron lip of the pio where she cannot see them and where we cannot make her see them, because to make her see them we would have to want her to go.

*

Nuno is telling her about Porto.

He is packing his rule and his lamp and his small hammer into the survey-bag, slow, the way a man packs when he does not want the packing to end the evening, and he is telling her a thing we did not ask for and cannot fully take. He says — and we have this in the flat clear way we have the living on our own ground, his voice off the cold walls, four seconds of it hanging in the roof and coming back — he says there was a flat in Porto, near the river, and there is a woman in it still, and the woman is not a wife anymore in the way that a wife is a wife, but the papers of it are not done, and the doing of them is why he takes the far jobs now, the jobs down the coast, the shed of a dead cannery in a lagoon town, so that the flat near the river is a place he sends money and not a place he sleeps. He says this to her over the survey-board and she listens the way she listens to everything, whole, taking it in without a word to show she is taking it, and we lean to read her and read her wrong. *He is telling her he is not free,* Aurélia says. *A man with a flat and a woman and papers unsigned is a man half-married, and a woman does not tie herself to a man half-married, and so this frees us of him, this loosens the rope, she will not stay for a man who is still someone's.* We say it after her. *She will not stay for him.* And the woman, hearing the same words we heard, does the opposite of what we said she would do — something in the set of her goes easy, goes toward him, because he has told her the true untidy shape of his life and she is a woman who trusts the untidy true thing over the clean false one, and she says something back to him low that we do not catch, and he almost laughs, and does not, and it is a thing between two of the living that is not for us and that we have read, again, upside down, and there is no one to tell us.

Then she goes out.

She goes out into the yard to find the keys she is sure are in the car, or on her, or in the room, and we go with her as far as the yard goes and no farther, because past the yard the water does not run and she thins to us, a shape, a patting of pockets, a car door that will not open. And then she does the thing we did not lean on and could not have leaned on, because it is hers: she stops looking. She stands in the black mud in the last of the grey with her hands still and she does not pat the jacket again. She decides — we cannot see the deciding, the face is gone at the yard's edge, but we feel the stillness of it — she decides she will not drive tonight. She will walk to the town, or she will not go to the town, she will stay the night in the shed's cold or in the room over the café and find the keys in the morning when the light is better and her head is clearer, and she is not troubled by it, and that is the part we cannot forgive: that the losing of the keys, which we did to trap her, does not land in her as a trap. It lands in her as a small ordinary loss of the kind the living lose all day, a set-down not written in, a thing that will turn up. She is not kept. She is only a woman who has mislaid her keys and shrugged, and the shrug undoes us more than any leaving could.

*

Down in the town, at the far dry edge of what we can reach, she uses the telephone.

We do not have this. We have almost none of this. She is in the room over the café where the water does not run and we catch her in the thinnest pieces — the light behind the shutter, the small blue of the telephone's face, her voice going up and down in a rhythm we know is Lisbon, is the station, is the panelled place, is the life left running like a tap. She is telling someone she needs a week. The same week she gave Melo she gives now to a voice in Lisbon, and the voice in Lisbon is not glad or sorry, it is only work, it is a shift covered, a schedule moved, the small machinery of a real life that is hers and is elsewhere and is waiting for her to come back to it. We cannot hear the words. We can hear that the words are *not here.* Wherever she is going when she is done, it is not into us. Graça could tell us the shape of her face saying it, Graça who goes out to the dry edge to watch her daughter at a cost the rest of us will not pay — but Graça is quiet tonight, at the front, over nothing, and does not report it, and we do not ask.

*

Later, when the woman is asleep across the square and the tide is only beginning to think of coming back, we count ourselves. We count at every low water now, not only the high, because the counting is a comfort and we are short of comfort at the ebb.

We go along the beams.

And we come to the salt-pan women, the *marnotas*, the ones who worked the white cones out on the flats and came in flu-dead or water-dead or only old, and we say them the way we say them, one after the other along the beam where they gather, and there is one of them we say first because she was the sharpest of them, the far-back one, from before the cannery, who raked the pans as a girl in the years of Idalina Velha — the one who had the saying about the salt, the saying that the salt keeps everything and eats everything — no. That is Aurélia's saying. We are sure that is Aurélia's. We go along the beam to the sharp *marnota* to say her, to say her name, the woman who — the woman who was out past the second pan the year the water came over, the woman whose name is —

and we reach —

and it is on the beam where she is, her name, we had it, it is a plain name, a name like a hundred names, it begins the way the town's names begin, and it is —

and we go on. We do not stop. We have learned, at the high water, not to stop, and we bring the not-stopping down to the low water with us, so that when the name does not come we say the next thing quickly, we say *and the marnotas, all of them, the whole flat of them,* and we go along the beam past the place where she is and finish the count, and we do not say aloud that one of the marnotas has become a place on a beam with no name over it, a saying about salt with no mouth it belongs to, because to say it aloud would be to hear the count fail while our mouths were still counting. So we do not say it. We count the marnotas as a crowd, *the whole flat of them,* and the crowd holds the shape of the one we cannot name the way water holds the print of a hand, exactly, and empty, and we move on.

Zé is fainter tonight. Custódia is fainter. They are near the beam where the shoe is, the small ones, and we lean toward them the way you lean toward a low lamp, and there is less lamp than there was. Custódia still has Zé. She says him. She says *Zé* when we come to the small ones, she says her brother's name and it is her own to say still, and we are glad of it, we the counters, we say it is a good sign, the sister still has the brother — but the saying is thinner than it was, thinner even than the last low water, and the shoe up in the beam above the two of them is only a shoe tonight, a small grey shoe, and we come to it in the counting and go past it and do not reach for whose it is, because we tried at the last high water and the trying went nowhere and there is a limit to how many times you will put your hand into the same cold water and pull it out empty.

We finish the count. It comes out low. We do not say the number. At the high water we said forty and made it true by saying; at the low water we do not even say forty, because forty will not go into the mouth over this little that leans back, and so we say *all of us, the whole of us,* which is a number without a number in it, and it goes down smooth and holds nothing and does not have to hold the marnota with no name or the two small ones going faint or the girl asleep across the square who mislaid her keys and shrugged. *All of us. The whole of us.* And out in the shed, on the iron lip of the near tank, in the dark where the light does not reach and where we could not make her look, the keys of the rented car lie where she set them and we leaned, and by morning we will not remember leaning, and she will find them there and think she left them, and be right, and never know she was kept a day for a reason we could not, by then, have named.


---

# Chapter 11 — Enchente

The notary's office is up the town again, and again we have her in pieces, because the water does not run under the Cais and where the water does not run we are a rumour. We have the chair. We have the pen that is not hers, clicked and set down, clicked and set down, a nervous sound, not her nerves, Melo's. We have the box between them on the table, open now, the flat brown tape gone from around it, the lid folded back, and we do not feel what is in the box because we long ago made a roof with no room for it. We have the smell we cannot smell — the salt gone into the glue, the paper swollen. We have Melo's voice, which we knew when it was a boy's. He is reading to her. He has his glasses down his nose and the big soft book open under a lamp and he is reading her the family's old hand, the way you read a thing aloud to a person who cannot read it for herself, and she cannot; she is of the line but she is of the new world; the old *letra*, the old looped Portuguese of the ledger-clerks, is a language to her, and Melo is the only one in the town left who still keeps it.

We lean to catch it. We are fewer than we were and we lean harder for being fewer, though we do not say we are fewer.

*Aqui,* Melo says. Here. He turns the swollen page and smooths it and reads her a line off it, slow, and we hear the shape of the words go across the table and land in her and do a thing to her we cannot see, because the faces are the last thing we see and up here over the town-water her face is only a pale place with a dark where the eyes are. He reads her a name that is a number. He reads her *board*, and a minus. He reads her *knife*, and a minus. He reads her *lodging*, and a minus, and *spoilage*, and a minus, and then a word she stops him on.

She stops him on *balance owed.*

She asks him — we get this whole, it comes across the water in her low even voice that gives us nothing and gives us this — she asks him to say again which way the owing runs. She has understood the columns to be wages. A book of wages, what the fábrica paid. And Melo, who is a careful man and does not like the answer while it is still in his mouth, says no. Says it is the other way. The *saldo devedor,* the balance owed, is owed *to* the house. The girl in the line, the number in the line, owes the house. At the end of her account, after the board and the knife and the lodging and the spoilage are all taken off, the number is not what the house owes her for her year of work. It is what she owes the house.

There is a quiet then, over the town-water, that we feel even in pieces.

She asks him how a person works a year gutting fish and ends the year owing money for it.

Melo does not answer that. Melo is a decent man and the answer is not decent and he has held this box a year believing it was only the old accounts, and he turns the page instead, as though the next page will be better, and it is not better, it is the same, a number and a minus and a minus and a balance owed, and another number under it, the same. We knew this. Somewhere in the pack of us we knew this. We kept the book. Aurélia kept the book, in the years she ran the floor, in the good years, and Aurélia is here in the roof and Aurélia does not lean toward the town this morning, does not lean toward the reading, and we mark that she does not, and we do not ask her why, and this is a thing we do more and more now — mark a silence and step around it.

She asks him who number fourteen was.

*

We do not go with her to the parish. We tell it as it comes back to us, in the pieces the day leaves in the yard when she comes home to our ground.

She asked Melo, over the box, who *no. 14, interior* was, the fourteenth number, the one his finger had stopped on — and Melo did not know. Melo keeps the old hand but he does not keep the old dead; that is another book, and another keeper. He sent her across the town to the church, to the *registo paroquial,* the death-register, where the priest before the priest before this one wrote down the town's dead in a great book of his own, name by name, the christened name and the day and the cause and the ground they went into. Two books, then, of the same girls. One that has them by number and takes something off them in every column. One that has them by name and gives them a day and a grave. She has gone from the one to the other, from the counting-house to the church, carrying a number, to ask a book of names to give it back its name.

This is a thing we would have said we did too, once. We would have said we were a book of names. We were proud of it, of being the ones who remembered, who said the dead along the beams at the turn of every tide, name by name, so they stayed. We said it grandly. And now a living woman is doing in an afternoon, in two buildings up the town, the thing we said was ours — going to the number and asking it its name — and she is doing it because we did not, because the number stayed a number in our roof for ninety years and we let it, and this we do not say either. We mark it, and step around it.

She is in the church now, past our reach, and we cannot see her, and we fill the not-seeing with the thing we do when we cannot see her. We tell ourselves a story we are sure of. We tell the family. We tell the greatness. And because we are frightened — some of us are frightened, this morning, though we would not use the word — we reach for the surest of the sure things, the story we have told longest and best, the one no tide has ever taken from us.

We tell Firmino.

*

Firmino drowned saving a child.

We say it the way we have always said it, from the front, in the voice we keep for the certain things. High summer, the year he was six-and-twenty, the water warm off the Barra, and a child of the town gone out past the bar where the channel runs cold under the warm, where the pull is; and Firmino, who knew the pull, who could swim, went in after the child and put the child up on the sand and turned, and the channel had him. The child lived. That is the whole of it. That is the story. We have said it at ten thousand tides, and it goes smooth in the mouth of us, and this morning we reach for the child to hold it up whole the way we always hold it up — and the child will not come up the same in two mouths.

*The ferryman's daughter,* says one of us. *It was the ferryman's daughter he pulled from the water.*

And another of us says no. It was a boy. It was a boy went out too far after a dog, or after a ball, a boy, and Firmino brought the boy in, and the boy grew up and had boys of his own and they are in the town still.

*The ferryman's daughter,* the first of us says again, because we do not let a said thing go easily; that is our whole trade, the not-letting-go. *She was small, she had a — she had a red thing, a red ribbon.*

*A boy,* says the other. *After a dog.*

And the two lie in the pool between us and will not lie down together, a girl with a red ribbon and a boy after a dog, and there is no one at the front with an edge hard enough to say *this one, this is the child he saved;* there is only the pool, and the two children in it, and the water they are lying in, which does not settle.

*There was a child,* says Aurélia.

Aurélia says it from the front, the way she says the certain things, and her voice is warm around it, and for a moment the warmth holds it, the way her voice holds most things, and we lean toward the warmth and say it after her, *there was a child, there was a child.* And the child holds — the child is there, saved, alive, grown up in the town; we have the child. It is only that we cannot any longer say which child, boy or girl, dog or ribbon, and the not-saying is a new thing this morning, a thing that was never there before, because until this morning the child had a shape and the shape was one shape and now the shape is two and neither of them will hold still. We do not say the child is not there. We would not say that. We say only, when we press on the child, that the child slips, that the child is a boy in one mouth and a girl in the next and will not be pinned; and this is worse than not knowing the day of it, worse than the boiler on two walls, because a child is not a wall, a child had a name once, and we go to the name to fix the child in place —

and it is not the same name in two mouths either.

And Firmino is here. Firmino is in the roof, as he has been in the roof through every telling, and Firmino says nothing.

He said nothing at the last telling. He says nothing at this one. He is one of the warmest of us, Firmino, one of the funniest, the one who can make the whole roof feel less like a roof for the length of a joke — and through his own story, the story of his own drowning, the best thing that was ever said of him, he holds a silence at the front of himself and lets us say it and does not say it with us. We have marked this before and stepped around it. We mark it now. We do not step around it as easily as we did.

*It does not matter which child,* says Aurélia, from the front, grandly, and this is the thing she says when a thing begins to slip and she means to hold it — *it does not matter which child, the point is he saved a child, the point is the greatness of it.* And most of us take the warmth and say it after her, *he saved a child, he saved a child,* and it holds, it goes smooth, because that is what saying is for; and a few of us, at the back where the roof comes down to nothing, hear that we have just agreed it does not matter which child and go quiet, because to us it has begun to matter which child very much, and we do not yet know why, and we do not go looking for the why.

We say *he saved a child* until it goes smooth. Which is how we like our stories. Smooth, and the child in it a boy or a girl, we cannot say which, and it does not matter which, we say, until the day it will.

*

She comes back to our ground in the last of the light.

The engine on the salt-pan road, the rented one, tuned to nothing, and the water is running up under the yard — it is the flood, tonight, the *enchente,* the tide coming in, rising, and we come up around her whole for the first time all day, greedy for the wholeness after a day of pieces. She has the box with her. She carried it up the town this morning and she has carried it back and she sets it in the yard-mud a moment while she gets the other thing out of the car, and the other thing is the machine, the black machine with the drowned-grey fur, and we do not like the machine, and she carries the machine and leaves the box, and this we mark: she carries the machine like a person and the box of us like a duty, and yet she has spent the whole day inside the box.

She has a paper folded in her jacket. We have it because it makes a small dry sound against the cloth when she moves, and because she takes it out, once, in the yard, and looks at it in the last grey light. It is not the family's paper. It is a paper the priest gave her, or the priest's woman, copied out of the great book of names in the church — the death-register, or a leaf of it, a name and a day and a cause and a ground. She has been to the book of names and asked it for number fourteen and it has given her something, and she stands in the yard holding it, and we lean to see whose name is on it, we who were the book of names, we who should know every name that book could hold —

and we cannot read it. It is a paper, in the living woman's hand, in the last of the grey, and we perceive sound first and then movement and then, dimly, faces, and a paper is none of these; a paper held still in the fog is a pale place with a darker place on it where the writing is, and we cannot read the writing, we who kept the names, and the name that is on it is a name we thought we would know and do not reach, and she folds it back into her jacket and picks the box up out of the mud and carries it and the machine both toward the light where the man is, and we are left in the roof with a story about a child we cannot find and a name on a paper we cannot read.

We tell ourselves we will know it tomorrow. At the next high water, when we lean together and are whole. We tell ourselves the way we always tell ourselves.

And one of us, up in the dark near the crossing of the beams, says quietly, to have it ready: *he saved a child, high summer, the year he was six-and-twenty, he put the child up on the sand* —

and reaches for the child —

and the child is there, the child is saved, the child lived — only it is a boy in the reaching, and then it is a girl, and then it is a boy again, turning over in the mouth of us the way the loose tin turns on the roof, and we cannot make it lie one way, and the water starts up the wall of the fábrica in the flood, rising, and does not stop where it stopped last night, and we have the child still, we are sure we have the child, we only cannot say, tonight, which child we have.


---

# Chapter 12 — Estofo

The kitchen table over the café is small and round and it is not made for three, and there are three at it: Idalina, and the notary who came down the town for this and would not say why on the telephone, and Dona Fátima, who has climbed the outside stair on her bad legs and sits with her hands flat on the oilcloth as though to keep it from lifting. Between them, in the middle of the table where the sugar would go, is the book. The wage-book. The water-swollen one, its cover buckled like a thing left on a beach, and it is open, and Melo has his reading glasses down his nose and his finger under a line, and he is reading the line out slow, because the hand that wrote it is old and the ink has gone brown as tide-scum, and Idalina has her machine on the table beside it with the small green light on.

She is recording him read.

We are here for this. We are on our own ground here — the water runs under the square now, this once, come up on the high spring and left standing in the drains, so that we can reach the room over the café that we cannot usually reach, and we have come, most of us, because Fátima carried the family stone's names up the stair in her mouth and we followed the names the way we follow anything said aloud. We fill the low room. We do not count how many. Some of us have stopped being able to count and the rest of us have stopped wanting to, and it is easier at the table, in the lamplight, to say *all of us* and let the word do the work a number used to do.

Melo reads: *number fourteen.*

He reads it the way he has been reading the others, flat, a clerk's voice for a clerk's book, and then he stops, because Fátima has put her flat hand out over the page, not touching it, hovering, the way you hold a hand over a candle to feel whether it is lit.

"That one," Fátima says. "Say that one again."
