*Number fourteen,* Melo reads. *Interior. Indenture.* And then the little marks, the debits, which he has learned by now to read as a row and not as words: *board, minus. Knife, minus. Cloth, minus. Spoilage, minus.* His finger goes along the row. It comes to the end of the row. *Funeral,* he reads, *minus.* And then the last column, which in this book is always the same column, the one that never once in all these pages says paid, that says instead, in the old hand, in the brown ink: *balance owed.*

He says it and the room over the café is quiet and we are quiet in it, we who have come up off our water to be here, and something happens in the quiet that we do not at first understand.

Somebody leans.

Not toward Idalina. We are all of us leaning toward Idalina, the way we always lean toward the living — toward the machine, toward the hand, toward the green light. But somebody among us leans the other way. Somebody leans toward the book. Toward the line. Toward the row of little minus-marks and the two words at the end, and it is a small leaning, a thin one, from far at the back of us where the roof of the room comes down to nothing, and we feel it the way you feel a draught and cannot find the door.

*

We knew there was a book. We want to be honest, now, at the table, in the lamp: we knew. We are the ones who kept it. Some of us kept it. Aurélia kept it — Aurélia's hand is the hand Melo is reading, the brown ink is Aurélia's ink, the little minus-marks are hers, she made them at the end of the shift with the same care she made the head-count, board minus knife minus, and she was good at it, she was proud of the neatness of it, and she is here at the table too, at the front of us, where she is always, and when Melo's finger comes along the row Aurélia says, in the voice she uses for the certain things:

*We were always kind to the interior girls.*

She says it exactly. She says it the way she said it in the yard to the man with the brochure, warm, from the front, the same six words in the same order, *we were always kind to the interior girls,* and some of us say it after her the way we always say it after her, because that is what the after-saying is for, to tamp it down, to make it smooth, and we get halfway into the saying — *we were always kind to the* — and the saying does not go smooth.

It catches.

It catches on the row of minus-marks that Melo is reading at that exact moment, board minus knife minus, and the two sayings are in the room together, Aurélia's warm one and the notary's flat one, *we were always kind* and *funeral, minus,* and they will not lie down together, they lie in the room at angles, and for the first time some of us hear the six words the way the reader would hear them if the reader could hear us, and the six words are obscene. They were always obscene. They are the same six words they have always been and they have not changed and that is the horror of it, that nothing in them changed, only the book came up out of the box and lay open on the table and now the words have the book under them, and a girl who was charged for her own funeral against a wage she was never paid was not a girl anyone was kind to, and we knew that, we always knew that, we made ourselves a roof with no room in it for knowing that, and here is the knowing, come up the stair on Fátima's bad legs, sitting at the table in the lamplight, and it will not go back down.

Half of us go on saying it. *We were always kind, we were always kind.* Louder, the way we get louder over the flu-dead where the roof does not push back. Aurélia loudest.

And half of us stop.

This is the thing that happens at the table, and it is new, and it has never happened before in all our long saying-together: half of us stop saying the thing while the other half is still saying it. There is no word for what we are then. We are two. We are a we and a we. The front of us, Aurélia's part, goes on with the greatness the way you go on rowing when the boat is already on the sand; and the back of us, the thin part, nearest the place where the roof comes down and the flu-dead used to lean, has gone quiet and turned toward the book, and between the two parts of us there is a slack, the way the water goes still at the turn of the tide before it can decide which way it is water — neither coming in nor going out, only standing, only weighing.

*

Idalina does not hear any of this. We must say that plainly. She hears Melo, and she hears Fátima, and she hears the tin roof of the café ticking as the night cools, and her machine hears all of it and holds it, and she does not hear us, she never hears us, we are not on the tape and we are not in the room to her, the room to her has three people in it and a book. She came to this book thinking it was a thing to sell — the family, the greatness, the story in iron letters that the man from Lisbon wanted for his wall. We had that of her. We had her opening the box for the man's sake, to find the good story, and we approved. We approve of a woman who counts her corners.

We had her wrong.

We have her wrong the way we always have her wrong, and this is the part we tell ourselves we can read and cannot. Some of us, even now, even at the table, say she is doing this for the price — that the greatness is worth less if the greatness is a debt-book, and she is a careful woman of the new world protecting her sale. It is what the front of us says, the loud part, because it is bearable. But the machine is running, and she does not run the machine for a price. She has never once run the machine for a price. She runs it because she cannot let a sound go by untaken, and she is taking this one — the old man's finger going along the row, *board minus, knife minus, funeral minus, balance owed* — she is taking it so that it cannot be un-said, so that it will be there after she is gone and the town is a spa and the loft is a suite with a view of the flats. She is not protecting the sale. She is ruining it, and does not fully know yet that she is ruining it. Neither do we. We only feel the thing turn under her the way the tide turns, no coming in, no going out, a slack, and we cannot read the slack, we can never read the slack, and we say among ourselves the thing that is bearable and it is the wrong thing.

Fátima says a name.

*

She has been quiet a long while, Fátima, her hands flat on the oilcloth. She was here for the family names, the stone-names, the ones she reads twice a week off the family stone with her white candles — Aurélia and Firmino and Graça and the old Idalina, the line, the ones with stones. She did not come for the book. But she has sat and listened to Melo read the book, number after number, no-name after no-name, *interior, indenture, board minus,* and something in the old woman has been climbing the way she climbed the stair, slow, on bad legs, toward a thing she was told as a girl and told never to say.

"My mother worked the descabeçar," Fátima says. The heading. The taking-off of the sardine heads. She says it to the oilcloth. "She would not go past the boiler. She would not go up the iron stair, not for anything, not to fetch, not to clean. And when I asked her why, when I was small and asked her why she would not go up, she said, *because of the palha.*" The straw. "The girls up there in the straw. That is what she called them. The straw-girls, the ones from the interior, up over the boiler where it was warm and dry, and she would not go up, and she would not say more, and she said if I was good I would forget I had asked."

Idalina has gone very still. Her hand is not near the machine. Her hand is flat on the table like Fátima's.

"There was one," Fátima says. "My mother said one name only, one time, and never again, and I have carried it my whole life not knowing what it went to. She said —" and the old woman stops, and gathers it, the way you gather water in a cupped hand knowing most of it will go. "Rosa. Rosa da Palha. Rosa of the straw."

And she surfaces.

*

We do not have a word for it because it has never happened to us this way. A name comes into the room from the mouth of a living woman — *Rosa da Palha* — and it goes down through us like a stone dropped in a tank that we had told ourselves was empty, and it strikes something at the very bottom of us that we had thinned to almost nothing, that we had spent long years thinning, on purpose, by not saying it, and the striking wakes it.

She was here the whole time. This is the thing we cannot bear and cannot not-know now: she was here the whole time, at the back, in the thin place, and she was so thin because we made her thin, we who kept the book, we who said *number fourteen* instead of Rosa so that we would never have to feel what number fourteen cost, we un-said her ninety years and she went down under the un-saying almost to gone — and Idalina, who does not know we are here, who does not know Rosa is here, has said the name out loud into the room, and Fátima has said it, and the two of them together have said the thing we spent a century not saying, and the not-saying breaks.

She comes up.

Not all the way. There is not much of her to come up. But she comes up out of the thin place at the back, up through the slack in the middle of us, toward the front where the book is and the lamp is and her own line lies open — *number fourteen, interior, funeral minus, balance owed* — and she does not say anything, not yet, she has almost nothing to say anything with, but she is there, she is a weight now where there was a draught, she leans toward her line the way the rest of us lean toward the living, and the whole room of us feels her arrive, feels the back of us push back for the first time in years, and it is not a comfort. It is a horror. It is the specific horror of a thing you buried getting up.

Aurélia feels it first.

Aurélia, at the front, who kept the book, whose ink it is, whose neat minus-marks these are — Aurélia feels the thing come up out of the back that she spent her death, as she spent her life, keeping down, and Aurélia does the thing she has always done, she says the greatness, she says *we were great, we were always kind to the interior girls,* she says it fast now, not warm now, not from the front of a full room now but the way you say a thing to a door that is opening, to keep it shut — and it does not work, it will not go smooth, and for the first time we hear Aurélia's voice with a scrape in it, we hear the great voice catch, and some of us — not many, a few, the ones nearest the back, the ones nearest the thing coming up — some of us do not say it after her.

Some of us have stopped saying it after her.

*

We split at the table. We are honest: we split. It is not clean and it is not loud, it is a slack thing, the water not knowing which way it is water, but it is a split, and it will not close again. There are those of us at the front, Aurélia's part, who say the book is only the old accounts, who say *we were always kind,* who say the girl come up from the back should go back down, should be un-said again so that the rest of us can go on being what we told ourselves we were — and they say it out of terror, it is not a grand thing they are protecting anymore, it is themselves, the thin skin over themselves that the book will peel. And there are those of us at the back, fewer, plainer, who have gone quiet, who have turned toward the line and the weight coming up under it, who are not saying anything grand, who have stopped being able to, who only lean toward the thing they helped bury and let it come up.

Between the two of us there is the slack. There is Rosa, half-risen, saying nothing. There is the lamp. There is the machine with its green light, taking all of it, the flat and the loud, the notary and the old woman, the room full of a war it cannot hear.

And there is the book.

We look at the book, the back of us, the quiet of us. *Number fourteen. Interior. Indenture. Board minus, knife minus, cloth minus, spoilage minus, funeral minus. Balance owed.* We know what it is — it is the most damning thing in the room, the thing that un-buries Rosa, her whole life set down in minus-marks and the one true fact of it, that she died owing us money for the privilege of dying — and we hold the meaning of the line the way we used to hold a name up in the beams, saying it to keep it. *This is number fourteen, this is Rosa, this is what she was charged for dying.* We say it to hold it.

And Idalina reaches over and turns the page.

She has to. Melo has read the line; the machine has it; there is more book under it, more numbers, more girls, and she is a woman who counts every corner, and she turns the page to read the next one, *number fifteen, interior,* and the line goes under, Rosa's line goes under the turned page into the dark of the closed part of the book, and it is gone from the lamp, and we — the back of us, the quiet of us, the ones holding the meaning of it — we reach to keep it, to say it again so it stays, *number fourteen, this is what it meant, this is Rosa, this is —*

and it is a row of little marks.

Board, and then a mark. Knife, and then a mark. A word, and a mark, and a word, and a mark, and at the end two words we had a moment ago and reach for now and they are only shapes, brown shapes in a book a woman has already turned past, and we know they mattered, we knew a breath ago exactly how they mattered and to whom, and now there is the shape and not the weight of it, the mark and not the girl, and we say *number fourteen* to get it back and number fourteen is a number, it is smooth, it holds nothing, it is how we made it in the first place, smooth and holding nothing, so that it could be lost by holding it, and it is lost, and the page is turned, and Rosa is up out of the back of us now with almost nothing to say, standing in the slack, owed a thing we can no longer read.


---

# Chapter 13 — Vazante

The water is going out from under the square when she comes down the outside stair with the box under her arm, and it takes us with it. This is the ebb. We do not hold together as well on the ebb; the water that carried us up under the drains last night is sliding back toward the channel, and we slide with it, thinner along the way, so that we have her going down the stair in pieces — the box first, the water-swollen book inside the box, then the sound of her shoes on the iron treads, then, late and dim, her face, which we lose again before we can read it. She is carrying the book back to the notary. She has been up all night with it. We know she has been up all night with it because the light behind her shutter did not go out, and because there is a thing in the way she carries the box now that was not there when Melo first gave it to her — she carries it the way you carry a thing you have decided about.

She puts it on the passenger seat where the machine usually goes. She puts the machine in the back.

We mark that. We mark it and we cannot agree what it means, and the not-agreeing is the whole of this day.

*

There are two of us now.

We said it at the table and it was true and it has not closed. On the ebb it is worse, because on the ebb we are strung out along the running water and the two of us are not in one place anymore — the front of us went one way and the back of us went another, and there is water between. We have not got words for the two of us. We reach for them. *Us and the other us.* *The loud and the quiet.* Aurélia has a word for it and the word is *betrayal*, and she says it in the great voice, from the front, and the ones who go with her say it after: the back of us has *turned*, the back of us is *sick*, the back of us caught a thing off the living the way you caught the flu off the loft, and it will pass if we hold, if we say the greatness, if we do not look at the book.

The back of us does not say betrayal. The back of us does not say much. That is the new thing, and it is the thing Aurélia cannot stand: the quiet of them. They used to say the greatness after her, tamping it down, and now they do not say it, and a thing you do not say after her is a thing she cannot make smooth.

The front of us keeps. It holds the door, it holds the greatness, it holds her, the living woman, it wants to keep her here in the yard of us forever with the machine and the box, and it does not say why anymore, it only says *stay* and *we were great* and *we were always kind*, and it holds the oar.

And the back of us lets. Not lets *her*, not yet, not out loud, nothing so large. It lets the words go. It lets *forty* go, it lets *kind* go, it has let the book stay open where the front of us would shut it. It lets. That is all. It is smaller and quieter and its sentences are shorter, and the front of us calls that dying, and it is not dying, or it is, we cannot tell, we have never been able to tell one from the other.

*

Firmino is in the back of us now.

We do not know exactly when he crossed. He was at the front seventy-odd years, Firmino, he was near the front, he was the warm one, the funny one, the one who could say a hard thing so it went down easy — and now on the ebb he is at the back, in the quiet, near the thing that came up at the table, and when Aurélia says *betrayal* he does not say it after her.

He says, instead — and it is short, it is the flat way the back of us talks now — he says: *We are not going to be able to un-say her again.*

That is all. He does not say it grandly. There is no front-of-the-room in it.

Aurélia says: *We can. We have before.*

*We were younger before,* Firmino says. *There were more of us before.*

And the front of us closes over that fast, the way water closes, because it is a count, and the count is a thing the front of us does not let the back of us make anymore. *We are forty,* the front of us says, out of habit, out of the good years, *we are forty in the roof* — and there is no answer from the back, no correction, the back of us has stopped correcting the number, it lets the number go the way it lets everything go, and the silence where the correction used to be is worse than any number, and the front of us fills it with the oar.

*

The oar is Firmino's oar. It hung in the office of the fábrica fifty years under a calendar and Graça kept it dusted, and Melo gave it to Idalina at the top of his stair, and she took it under her arm like a thing to be carried and not a thing to be kept, and she has left it at the notary's because there is no room in the rental for an oar. It is four feet of painted pine gone the grey of everything, and there is a worn place on the loom where his two hands went, and it is the proof of him. It is the proof of the greatness in the smallest, best form — a young man of the line who went into the water to bring a child out of it and did not come back, and the child lived, and the oar is what he had in his hands, or is like what he had in his hands, and the front of us brings it up now, on the ebb, to hold the day together.

*Firmino saved a child,* the front of us says. Aurélia says it. *Firmino went off the Barra in the high summer and brought the child up onto the sand and the channel took him past the bar, and here is the oar.*

We have said it a thousand times and it has always gone smooth. It does not go smooth today. Not because the back of us argues it — the back of us does not argue anything, the back of us has not the wind for arguing — but because the oar is in the notary's office two hundred metres off, not here, and the front of us is holding up a thing that is not in the room, holding up the *idea* of the oar, saying *here is the oar* over a place where there is no oar, and the saying has gone hollow in a way we have not heard before. The front of us is brandishing a thing that is not there. And Firmino, at the back, when his own oar is held up, when his own name is said in the great voice over the great story — Firmino says nothing at all. He does not say it after her. He does not warm it. He lets it go by.

We do not, most of us, notice that Firmino has gone quiet inside his own story. But the back of us notices, and the back of us does not say anything, and the oar hangs in the office in the dark under the tinsmith's calendar, meaning, for now, still meaning, but held up here in a room it is not in, by a voice that has begun to hear its own hollowness, and the meaning of it thins by the smallest amount, the way paint thins, a layer, not gone, only a layer.

*

She drives to the notary. We have her along the water — the road runs by the flats and the water runs under the road, going out, and we go out with it, thin, following the small rich engine, and we have her park, and go in with the box.

We cannot follow her all the way into Melo's rooms; the water does not run under the whole of the town, and where it does not run we lose her, and we get this meeting the way we get the far things — in pieces, out of the sounds that reach us and the sounds she brings back on the machine, though she has left the machine in the car today, and we hate that she has left it in the car, because without it we are guessing.

We guess. The front of us is good at guessing and sure of its guesses. The front of us says she has brought the book back to be rid of it — that she sat up all night with the debt and the girls and the funerals-charged and could not stomach it, and has decided to hand the whole ugliness back to the old man and take the good story only, the oar and the greatness, the clean part, and sell that. *She will take the oar,* the front of us says, *and leave the book. She is of the line. Blood knows what to keep.*

She does not do that.

We do not see her do the other thing, but we hear the shape of it when she comes out — she comes out without the box. She has left the box with Melo. But she has kept a leaf of paper, folded, in her jacket, and she has kept the machine running now, we hear the small green nothing of it as she gets in the car, and she is not lighter the way a person is lighter who has handed a weight back. She is heavier. She has left the book and taken the weight. The front of us cannot read that and says it is nothing, says she is tired, says blood knows what to keep — and the back of us, the quiet of us, says the short true thing: *She is not keeping the clean part. She is keeping the girls.*

The front of us does not answer that either.

*

Later, the man comes. Caldas.

He comes to the café in the better car and he does not go up to her room; they sit at the outside table on the square, at the dry edge of us where the water does not run, so that we have them thinly, in scraps, the way we have all the town's dry places — a word, a laugh that is not a laugh, the scrape of a chair. We lean toward them as hard as the thinned pack of us can lean and we get pieces. Caldas is smiling. Caldas is a man who smiles while he closes a hand. He has heard something — that she is slow now, that she went to the church, to the parish books, that she has been asking who a number was — and he does not like a woman who asks who a number was, because a number that becomes a name is a thing his brochure cannot hold, and he says, over the little coffees, in the voice that is all reason:

*The story is the value.* We get that clearly, on the dry air. *The building is a shed. Anyone can buy a shed. What I am buying is the name — Sequeira, a hundred years, salt and sardine, the family that built this coast — and a name is only worth what people will believe about it. Bring me the greatness and I will make you rich. Bring me a scandal and you have a shed and a debt and a lien, and I walk to the next town, and you are left holding —* and here the scrap goes thin and we lose the end of it, but we do not need the end of it, we know what he says she is left holding. He says she is left holding us.

The front of us loves him. This is a thing we did not know we could feel and we feel it: the front of us, Aurélia's part, loves the man from Lisbon who wants the greatness, because he wants the same thing the front of us wants, he wants her to take the clean part and leave the book, he wants the girls to stay numbers, and for one turn of the tide the killer and the developer want the very same thing and it is *keep the story, drown the girls*, and the front of us says *listen to him, blood, listen to the man, he is right, the story is the value* — and it does not hear itself say it, it does not hear that it has come to stand exactly where the man with the brochure stands.

The back of us hears it. The back of us has gone very quiet. Firmino is in the quiet. He says one thing, short, to the front of us, to Aurélia, and it is not warm, the warmth has gone out of his saying the way warmth goes out of a room when the boiler dies:

*We sound like him now.*

*

Nuno is there too, at the far side of it, and this is the thing we get most wrongly of all, and we will not know for a long time how wrongly.

The engineer has come down from the loft-work and he stands at the edge of the square while Caldas talks, and he does not sit, and when Caldas has gone — the better car, the smile put away in it — Idalina goes to him, and they walk out along the flats where the water is right out now, the black *lodo* shining, low water coming, and this we have, this stretch we have, because the water though gone-out still runs the channels here and we are strung along it, thin, but present, and we get them in pieces walking.

They are not easy with each other. We hear that much. He surveyed the building for the man; his name is on the man's papers; he found the marks in the beams and photographed them for the man's file, and now the marks are a thing she is using against the man, and he is caught in the middle of it the way a thing gets caught between two beams where the water throws it. We hear the caught-ness in his voice, low, along the mud. We hear her voice go hard once and go soft once. We hear him say a word we take to be *job*. We hear her say a word we take to be *no*.

And the front of us is glad.

The front of us has hated the affair from the first night — hated the man from Porto with the wife he is leaving, because a woman with a lover is a woman with a rope out of the town, a woman who might be pulled up out of the yard of us and away — and now the front of us hears them go hard with each other on the flats and it is glad, it says *good, let it break, let the engineer go back to Porto, let her lose the thing that would carry her off, and then she stays, then she is here in the yard with the box and the greatness and the oar forever* — it says *the crack will drive her back to us* — and the front of us has read her last page again, and it is wrong again, it is always wrong, and the back of us, watching the two of them out on the low water, does not say glad and does not say sorry, only watches, and only says, when the front of us crows that the affair is breaking and she will come back to the yard: 

*She is not coming back to the yard. She has never once come to the yard for us. She comes for the sound.*

And the front of us does not answer, because there is no answering the back of us anymore, the back of us has gone to the plain place where you cannot argue a thing, you can only set it down.

*

Low water. Baixa-mar coming on, the flats bared, the salt-cones white out on the *marinhas*, the two people small and dark on the black mud with the sound between them, and the water gone right out from under the square so that we cannot follow them back in, and we are strung along the last channels, thinner than we were this morning, the front of us and the back of us with the going water between and no bridge across it.

The front of us says, to hold the day together, the last thing it has: *We are forty, and we are great, and Firmino saved a child, and here is the oar.*

And it reaches for the oar to hold it up — the oar that is not here, that is in the office under the calendar, that the front of us has held up all day over rooms it was not in — and it says *here is the oar, here is the proof, here is Firmino* —

and Firmino, at the back, in the quiet, does not come when his name is said.

He does not come to the front. He does not warm the story. He stays in the quiet with the ones who let, out at the thin edge where the words go, and the front of us holds up the idea of the oar over a place where there is no oar and no Firmino either, holds it up in the great voice into the going water, *here is the oar, here is the oar* — and it is only a thing a hand once held, four feet of grey pine two hundred metres off in the dark, and the man whose hands they were has walked to the back of us and will not answer to it, and the front of us does not notice that he will not answer, and the oar hangs in the office meaning less than it meant this morning, a layer of it gone, and no one who is holding it up can any longer feel the weight it used to have.


---

# Chapter 14 — Águas-mortas

There is not much water under the town today. It is the neap, the dead water, the tide that comes in without meaning it and goes out the same way, and out on the flats the black mud lies bare a long time and does not shine. We are thinner at the neap. We do not gather as well. When the water is high and running we can lean together and make the living whole; at the dead water we come at her in pieces, a hand, a sound, the back of her jacket, and we take what we are given and do not complain, because there is no one to complain to.

Idalina is in the fábrica by herself. Nuno is not here today; he is in Porto, or he is on the road, we do not follow him past the last salt-pan and we do not try. She has the machine and the little pole and a folding chair she has brought from the room over the café, and she sets the chair down in the middle of the floor between the three pios, on the concrete, and she does not sit in it. She stands under the boiler-end of the shed and looks up at the loft door.

The little door is shut, the way it is always shut. The iron stair goes up to it out of the dark behind the boiler. She does not climb the stair. Nuno told her the timbers are gone, that no one goes up without a harness, that the loft is worse than he thought, and she believed him, and she has not been up. She stands at the foot of the stair and lifts the pole so the grey fur end of the machine is as high as she can hold it, up near the shut door, and she stands very still, and she records the room she cannot go into.

*

We cannot go into it either.

This is a thing we have never said plainly to ourselves and we say it now because she is making us stand here at the foot of the stair with her, at the dead water, with nothing to do but attend. The loft is inside our house. It is the room over the boiler, the warm dry room, and we are bound to this house, we are in every part of it, in the roof and the tanks and the rust-stain on the concrete — and we are not in the loft. We come to the foot of the iron stair the way she comes to it and we go no further. The leaning does not carry up the stair. It is a room inside our own house that we cannot enter, and we have told ourselves for a long time that this is because there is nothing up there, only straw and dust and the old dark, and today, with the living woman holding her machine up at the shut door, we do not tell ourselves that.

Some of us say there was a dormitory up there. Some of us say a storeroom. Some of us — the front of us, the loud part — say it was a kindness, that the girls were put up over the boiler because it was the warm place, the dry place, and that was a thing we did for them out of kindness. The three of these do not lie down together. They lie in us at angles, the way *we were always kind* lay at angles with the book on the table, and we do not put them together, and we have spent a great deal, we are spending it now, on not putting them together.

*

The machine hears the room for a long time.

We do not know what it hears. We hear what she hears — the tin lifting on the north side and dropping, the drip that has been dripping since before she came, the tide seething thin and far at the yard's edge because it is the neap and there is not much of it — but the machine reaches into the loft where our leaning will not go, and it takes whatever the loft is making up there, which to her ears is nothing, which is a shut room making no sound, and it holds that nothing completely and does not tire of holding it. She lowers the pole. She caps the fur. She sits down in the folding chair with the machine on her knees and she puts the little cups over her ears, the ones she lent to the man once, and she plays it back.

We watch her face while she listens and the face is the last thing we can see and at the neap we can hardly see it at all. But we have her body. We have her go still. We have her stop the tape and wind it back a little and play the same stretch again, and again, the way we go back over a memory to hold it, and we understand that she is doing our thing, she is doing the wet-sand thing, saying it again to make it stay — except she is not saying it, the machine is saying it, and it stays because the machine keeps it and not because she does, and there is a difference in that we can feel and cannot name.

She takes the cups off her ears. She sits in the folding chair in the middle of the empty shed with the machine on her knees and she does not move for a while. Something has come up in her that has no name to it that we can find. It is not the grief you have for a person; she does not know a person to have it for. It is the grief you have for a room. She has recorded a room where something happened that she cannot hear and cannot name, and she has played it back and heard nothing, a shut room, a drip, the tin, and the nothing has done to her what a name would do, and she sits with it, and we sit with her, and none of us — not the front of us, not the back — says anything grand over it. There is nothing grand to say. It is the dead water. The grand sayings do not float at the dead water.

*

Then she does a thing we did not read.

She takes out the telephone. She stands up out of the chair and she walks toward the yard door where the light is better and the machine can rest a moment on the pio's iron lip, and she makes a call, and we get it in pieces because she has walked toward the edge of us, toward the door, toward the outside, where we are thin.

We have her voice and not the other voice. We have: *no, I know. I know I said Wednesday.* We have: *it's more than I thought, it's — I can't explain it on the phone.* We have her laugh once, a short one, not a glad one, at a thing the other voice says that we do not get. We have: *the sessions can move, can't they, Rui can cover the Tuesday, he owes me* — and a name goes by, Rui, a living name, a man in a room with soft walls in Lisbon where the sound goes to be tamed, and it goes by us and means nothing to us and means a great deal to her, a whole life, running like a tap in a city we will never reach. We have: *I don't know when. Not this week.* And then, lower, turned away, so that even the front of us barely takes it: *no. No, I'm not staying. God, no. I just have to finish something first.*

The front of us takes that and turns it over and is glad of it. *She is not staying,* the front of us says. *We told you. We read her last page. She will finish and go.* And they say it the way you say a thing that comforts you, and it does comfort them, and they do not hear what is under it, because the front of us has never been good at hearing what is under a thing.

We had her wrong again. She is not staying, that is true, we did not have that wrong — but *I just have to finish something first* is not a woman fleeing, it is a woman who has found a thing she will not leave undone, and the finishing of it is not the sale, the finishing of it is the loft and the book and the girl in the straw, and that finishing will hold her here longer than the sale ever would have, and the front of us, glad she is not staying, does not see that she has just told the tap in Lisbon to run a while yet for the sake of the very thing the front of us most wants buried. She is going. She is also not going anywhere until it is told. Both of these are on her and neither of them is ours, and we cannot hold both at once, and so we hold the one that is bearable, *she will finish and go,* and let the other slide, the way we let everything slide that costs too much to hold at the dead water.

She puts the telephone away. She picks the machine up off the iron lip. She goes back to the folding chair.

*

Custódia is with us at the foot of the stair.

She has been here the whole time, small, near the back, near the place where the roof comes down to nothing and the flu-dead used to lean before they went out on the tide we did not watch. She does not lean toward Idalina the way the rest of us lean. She has never had much lean in her; she was six, she is one of the small ones, she and — she and the other small one, the brother, they came in the bad year with the fever that went through the loft and the town both, and they thinned early, the small ones always thin early, there is less of a child to hold and fewer of the living who kept their names.

Custódia is looking up at the beams.

Not at the loft door where the machine went. At the beams, at the black wedge where the roof meets the wall, where the water threw a thing once, or a hand threw it, in the year the water was high. There is a shoe up there. A child's shoe, small, the leather gone hard and grey, caught in the join of two beams. It has been up there a long time. Custódia has come to it now, at the dead water, while the living woman plays her tape back, and Custódia is doing the thing we all do, she is saying a name to a thing to keep it, she is holding the shoe up in the pool of us with a name, and the name is —

*

We had it in the first chapter of her coming. We want to be honest, here at the neap, in the bare light. We had it. The evening the car came in we said it up in the dark to have it ready, a boy's name, and we said whose the shoe was, and we said Custódia had the other half of him and did not ask her, because it was early yet and the shoe was where it had always been.

It is later now. It is not early.

Custódia reaches for the name the way the rest of us reach for the marnota's name on the beam, the way we reach for the day Firmino drowned, the way we reach for our own number at the ebb — she reaches, and the shoe is there, the shoe is exactly there, small and grey in the join of the beams, she can hold up the shoe, the shoe does not go anywhere. It is the name that is not there. She had a brother. She knows she had a brother the way you know a room you have left, by the shape of the door and not by what was in it. She looks at the shoe, and the shoe is a shoe, and it belonged to — it belonged to the one who was — to the small one, the one who —

*

*Zé,* one of us says, from the middle, quietly, to help her. *It is Zé's shoe. He was your brother. Zé.*

And Custódia turns the name over the way you turn a stone over on the flats to see the underside of it, and there is nothing on the underside of it. The name comes to her the way a word comes to you in a language you have stopped speaking — you know it is the word, you know someone is telling you it is the word, and it does not fill with anything, it stays a shape, four sounds, a stranger's name for a stranger's shoe. She does not say *yes*. She does not say *Zé*. She looks at the shoe and she does not know it is his, because she does not, anymore, have a him for it to be. The brother is gone out of her the way the flu-dead went out of the back of the roof, on some tide she did not watch, without her at the shore to see it go, and she is left holding the shoe up in the pool of us with no name to put to it, and it is only a shoe.

*

We do not, at first, understand what has happened, and then we do, and it is worse than the book was.

The book was a thing done to the girls, and it was ours, we did it, and when it came up out of the box it split us and it thinned the ones who kept it, and that was terrible and it was just, there was a justice in the book coming up that even the front of us could feel under the terror. This is not that. This is not justice and it is not done to anyone. This is only the tide going out of a person, the plain thing, the thing that happens to all of us and that we spend our whole death saying our names against — a small dead girl who cannot remember her small dead brother, who is holding up his shoe with no name to hang on it, and there is no one to blame for it and nothing to be told and no book to open, it is only that she is thin now, and he was thin before her, and the sea took him out of her the way it takes the diesel-smell of the living out to the bar and does not bring it back.

The front of us does not come to help. The front of us is at the front, with the loud sayings, keeping the greatness and the kindness up over the boiler where it is warm. It is the back of us that is here, the thin part, the plain part, the part that has stopped being able to say grand things — and the back of us cannot help either, because to help her we would have to give her the brother, and we do not have the brother to give. We have the name. Some of us still have the name, *Zé, Zé, it was Zé,* and we say it, we hold it up, but a name held by us and not by her is a name in a machine and not in a memory, it is the shoe played back off a tape, it is there and it stays and it does nothing, because the one it belonged to cannot take it.

She lets the shoe be a shoe. That is the thing we watch her do, at the foot of the stair, at the dead water. She does not put a name to it and she does not fight to. She looks at it, small and grey in the beams, and she lets it be a shoe, an ownerless shoe up in the dark of a house full of the dead, and then she does not look at it anymore, and the little lean she had toward it — the last lean of a child toward the last thing of her brother — goes out of her, and she is a fraction quieter than she was, and the rest of us are a fraction robbed, and none of us, not even the ones who still have the name, says it again, because there is no longer anyone to say it to.

*

Idalina winds the machine down. She has been here since the morning and the dead water has turned and started its small coming-back and it will be dark soon on the flats. She stands and folds the chair and takes up the machine and she looks, once, up at the roof — at the beams, at the black wedge, at the dark under the eaves — the way she looked up the first evening, and the shoe is there, and she does not see it, she has never seen it, she is not looking for a shoe, she is looking at the timbers a man told her were rotten and thinking about a room she cannot enter and a thing she has told a man in Lisbon she has to finish.

She does not know there is a shoe.

She does not know there was a boy.

She goes out through the yard door with the machine held to her the way you hold a thing you mean to keep, and the tide comes up another thin hand's width behind her, the small dead-water tide, and lifts the loose tin and does not quite drop it, and up in the beams the shoe stays where the water threw it, or where a hand threw it, in the year the water was high, and there is no name up here now for whose it was, and the small quiet one at the foot of the stair does not reach for it again.


---

# Chapter 15 — Baixa-mar

The tide is out and the machine is on the floor of the fábrica in the middle of the boiler end, and Idalina is lying on the concrete beside it with her eyes shut, not asleep, listening to nothing.

She has made the nothing on purpose. She has waited for it. She came down before the light, in the cold, and she stood at the three brine tanks until the pumps up the coast went quiet and the gulls had gone out to the bar and the town over the flats had not yet started its one van, and then she set the machine down flat on the concrete and lay down beside it and told it, with her thumb, to take the room. The green light is on. The fur end of it points up at the black wedge of the roof where we are. She is recording the fábrica making no sound.

We are fewer than we were, down here at the low water, at baixa-mar, when the water has gone out from under the yard and taken with it the little we lean by. Some of us. Some of us are here. We do not say the number. We have stopped saying the number where we can help it, the way you stop saying a word that has begun to come out wrong. We lean toward her the way we can, and it is a poor lean, a low-water lean, and we take her in pieces — the jaw, the shut eyes, the hand flat on the concrete beside the machine as if to keep the floor from lifting, the way Fátima's hand lay on the oilcloth. We do not have her whole. We have not had her whole in some time and we have stopped saying that too.

She wanted the room's own quiet. We understand this the way we understand her listening, from the inside, because there is nothing we know better than a room making no sound. This was the loud room. This was the room the *sirene* owned, the *apito* that went off at any hour and put us on our feet before we were awake, and the hundred knives on the wet wood, and the water going in the tanks, and the fish coming up the line, head off, gut out, head off, gut out, all day and into the dark. It was never quiet. It is quiet now. Idalina lies in the middle of the quiet with the machine and takes the quiet, and up in the roof the few of us who came down for it hang over her and listen to her listen, and it is a low, thin thing, and we do the thing we do, we say it to keep it. *This was the loud room.* We say it to hold what the room was. And it comes back to us smaller than we sent it. The knives are in it, and the water, and then only the knives, and then only that it was loud, and we hold *it was loud* and cannot get under it to the rest, and we let it go and pretend we had it all along.

*

She works two hours and then she stops the machine and sits up and puts the little cups in her ears to hear it back.

We come down close for this. We always come close for the playback, because the playback is the machine doing the thing we cannot, giving a room back its own past, and we cannot look away from it any more than we could look away from it that first evening in the yard. She thumbs it back to the start and plays the nothing.

And we hear it too. Not the way she hears it, in the cups; we hear it in her stillness, in the not-moving of her, in the held quality of a living body hearing a thing back. We hear the room played into the room. And the room played into the room is the room with no one in it.

We knew this. We have always known this — that we are not on the tape, that the machine keeps only what makes a sound and we make no sound it can keep. Aurélia said it the first evening, *there is nothing of us to record,* and it was the worst thing anyone had said, and we went quiet under it. But that was the yard, and there were more of us then, and the wind was in the chimney and the tin was lifting and the tape had things in it, and we could tell ourselves the room was only crowded with weather. This is not the yard. This is the boiler end at low water with the pumps stopped and the gulls gone, and she has made the room as empty of the living's sound as a room can be made, on purpose, for hours, and now she plays it back, and it is empty. Two hours of the loud room and there is nothing in it. The tin does not lift here. The water is out. There is a drip somewhere and the drip is in it, and the settling of the cold iron is in it, and under those, holding those, is the thing she came down in the dark to take: the room's own silence, whole, unbroken, ours, the room we have hung in for a hundred years, and we are not in it.

Some of us have heard this before and it did not do to us what it does now. What it does now is this: we hear, for the first time, what the room will sound like when we are gone. Not what it sounds like without the living. What it sounds like without *us*. She has recorded it. It is on the tape. The tape is the room after. And the room after sounds exactly like the room now, drip and iron and the water out, exactly the same, not one grain of us subtracted from it because there was never any grain of us in it to subtract, and the going of us will not change the tape by so much as a breath.

Firmino says the thing then.

*

He says it low, from wherever he is in the pack of us, and Firmino has not been the front of anything for a long while — the front is Aurélia's, it has always been Aurélia's — and he says it not grandly, not from the front, the way you say a thing you have only just seen and are afraid of, and he says it once.

*It is us that keeps her here.*

We do not follow him at first. Some of us do not follow him at all. He says it again, plainer, because he can feel us not following, and Firmino was always the one who could feel a room.

*She does not stay for the money. She does not stay for the man from Porto. She stays because we lean. We lean and she circles the thing we lean at. As long as we hold this place, as long as we hold the family and the greatness and the book and the girls and the shoe and the whole of it up in the beams and say it and say it to keep it, she cannot get free of it, because she is the last that our holding runs through. Stop leaning and she goes.*

He says it and the room over the tape goes still, our part of the room, and for a breath none of us says anything, and in the breath the thing he said sits in the middle of us the way the book sat in the middle of the table, open, unable to be closed.

Then Aurélia says no.

She does not say no. She says the greatness. She does the thing she does, she comes to the front of us where the front has always been hers, and she says *we hold her for her own sake,* she says it warm, she says it the way she said the other things, *we are her people, we are all she has, we hold her the way you hold a child at the top of a stair* — and it is a good saying, it is one of her best, and half of us go to it the way we always go to Aurélia, because to go to Aurélia is to be held ourselves, and the after-saying starts, *for her sake, for her sake,* the wet-sand tamping of it —

and it catches.

It catches the way it caught at the table, on nothing you could point to, on the room played back into the room with none of us in it. Because we heard it. We all heard it, front and back, the tape of the room after, and we cannot un-hear that the room after sounds the same, and if the room after sounds the same then we are not holding the room up, we are only hanging in it, and if we are not holding the room up then what are we holding her here for — and the front of us says *for her sake* louder to get over the catch, and the back of us, the low part, nearest the drip, has heard Firmino and has heard the tape and does not say it, does not say *for her sake,* has gone quiet and turned toward the thing Firmino set down.

We split again. It is not new now, the splitting, it is the second time and it is worse the second time because now we know what it is, we felt it at the table and we feel it here, the slack in the middle of us, the water not knowing which way it is water. The front of us holds *for her sake.* The back of us holds the other thing, the thing Firmino said and will not take back — *it is us that keeps her,* and under it, unsaid, in the low quiet where the honest few have always been, the second half of it that Firmino did not say and did not need to, because we can all hear the shape of it now the way you hear the shape of a wave before it lands: that to let her go we would have to stop. Stop leaning. Stop holding. Stop saying the place to keep it. Go out the way the water goes out and not come back up the yard.

The back of us has that shape now. It does not say it. There is no saying it yet. But it has the shape, and the shape is in the room with us, and the front of us is saying *for her sake* over the top of it, fast, not warm now, the way Aurélia said the greatness to a door that was opening, to keep it shut.

*

Idalina takes the cups out of her ears.

She has heard enough of the nothing. She sits for a while on the cold floor with her arms around her knees and she does not move and we cannot read her not-moving, we can never read the not-moving, we say among ourselves the thing that is bearable and it is probably the wrong thing. Then she gets up, stiff from the concrete, and she wraps the machine and does the thing that turns the day.

She takes out the telephone and she calls the notary.

We have this in pieces. The telephone-talk is a thing we get badly even on our own ground, because half of it is a man over the town-water that we cannot reach and the other half is her, and we have only her half, and her half is short. *Dr. Melo. Yes. No, I've decided.* A silence that is the man talking. *No. Not to him. Not as — not as heritage. Not the story, not the letters on the wall, none of it.* The man again, longer. *I know what it's worth to him. That's the point.* And then a thing she says that we do get, whole, because she says it plainly, into the little machine of the telephone, in the empty room where she thinks no one can hear her: *Whatever I do with the building, I'm not selling him the greatness. There isn't any. I've read the book.*

We had her wrong.

We say it before we can stop ourselves, some of us, the front of us, *she would not,* because we had read her last page, we always read her last page, we had her as a woman who counts her corners, who opened the box for the price, who would take the man's money and let the loft be a suite with a view of the flats and be gone before the sheets were cold. We had that of her firm. We were sure of it. And here she is on the cold floor of our house telling the notary the opposite of it, telling him she will not sell the thing we are made of, telling him there isn't any greatness, plainly, the way you say a true thing, and it is a page we did not read, it is a page we could not have read, because it is hers, and we do not get her pages, we only get her sounds, and we made the rest up and called it reading.

The front of us does not like it. The front of us needs her to be sold to Caldas, needs the greatness bought and framed and bolted to the wall, because a greatness on a wall is a greatness that holds, is a thing said over and over in iron letters by every stranger who comes to the spa, and a thing said over and over holds us the way the after-saying holds us, tamps us down, keeps us. If she sells the greatness she keeps us. If she strips it, if she takes the book to the men who make books true, then the last living mouth of the line will have un-said us instead of said us, and we will not be held by iron letters or by anything, and we will be only what Firmino said, a weight in the water she is caught in, and the weight is coming loose.

So the front of us says she does not mean it. Says she is a careful woman working the man's price up, threatening the greatness to get more for it. Says *for her sake* again, over the top. And the back of us, the low part, has stopped answering, and Firmino, who said the thing, does not take it back, and Rosa — what is left of Rosa, the little that came up at the table and did not go back down — Rosa is in the back with the quiet part and does not lean toward the living at all, has never once leaned toward the living, leans only the other way, toward going, and we feel her there the way you feel the door of a cold room, and we do not go to it.

*

She is at the door of the fábrica with the machine under her arm and one hand on the frame, about to go up into the town and say to the man's face the thing she has said to the notary, and she stops.

She stops because of a thing that is not there. We do it. The back of us does not do it; the back of us has gone past interfering. The front of us does it, Aurélia's part, the ones who need her sold, and it is the small ugly thing that is all we have ever been able to do — we lean on the moment, on the little hinge of a living mind where a thing is held or dropped, and we make her not sure. We make her not sure she turned the machine off. She stands at the door and thinks, *did I stop it,* and she did stop it, we watched her stop it, the green light is out, but we lean on the not-sureness and she takes the machine out from under her arm and turns it in her hands to look, and this is our whole arsenal, this is everything we can do to a living woman, make her check a light that is already out, and it buys us nothing, it buys us the four seconds she stands in the door looking at the dark machine, and we do not even know anymore what we wanted the four seconds for.

She sees the light is out. She knew it was out. She puts the machine back under her arm and goes up the yard, and the tide is at its lowest now, all the way out, the black *lodo* bared and stinking sweet along the reeds, baixa-mar, the low water, the least of it, and she walks up over the flats toward the town to un-sell us, and we hang in the door of the room and watch her go small.

Firmino says it one more time, into the going of her, quiet, and this time he says the whole of it, the part he left off, plainly, once, and no one answers him, and it is the plainest thing that has been said in our house in a hundred years, and we will not be able to un-hear it any more than we could un-hear the tape.

The front of us reaches for the greatness to say over it, to tamp it down, to make it smooth — reaches for *we were great,* for *we hold her for her sake,* for the thing we have always reached for when a thing gets said that we cannot carry — and the front of us gets *we were,* and then the yard is empty, and the machine is gone up over the flats in a living woman's arms, and we cannot find the rest of it, we who have said it ten thousand times, and we hold *we were* up in the low dark of the roof, the two words with nothing after them, and the water does not come back to tell us what we were.


---

# Chapter 16 — Estofo

The telephone rings in the room over the café before she has her boots on, and it is the man from Lisbon, the one who wants the greatness for his wall, and we know it is him before she says his name because his voice comes small and even out of the little machine at her ear, the voice of a man who has decided something and is being pleasant about it. She stands by the window with the flats going grey behind her and the coffee going cold on the sill, and she listens, and does not say much, and we lean at her to hear, most of us, because the water is still standing in the drains from the spring and we can reach the square this morning, which we do not always get to do.

Caldas is not shouting. We have heard men shout on this floor and it was never the shouting that took the skin off. He is telling her, in the even voice, that the building is one thing and the family is another, and that he can buy the one without the other. There is a councilman. There is a way to buy the shed over her head, a lien, a debt of the estate she did not know the estate had, and then it is his shed and he writes the booklet he wants, and the greatness in it is whatever he says it is, and her name and her mother's name are in it however he chooses to set them.

Or, he says. Or she gives him the story clean, the sardines and the hard work and the family that fed the cod-men, and he pays her the better number, and everyone is friends, and the loft is a suite with a view.

He does not say the loft. He says a suite with a view. But we hear the loft under it the way we have learned this month to hear the thing under the thing.

She says she will call him back.

She puts the machine down next to the cold coffee and stands with both hands flat on the sill and her head down, and we cannot see her face — the face is the last thing and we are fewer than we were and the face is going from us — but we can hear her breathing, and we can hear the breathing change, and Aurélia, at the front of us, says the thing Aurélia says.

*She will give him the clean story. She is a woman who counts her corners. She will not lose the shed over a book.*

We do not say it after her.

Some of us do. The front of us does. But the after-saying does not fill the room the way it used to, and there is a place at the back where it does not reach at all, and it is quiet there, and it has been quiet since the night before, since the thing that was said in the dark that we are not going to say again, that would spend us to say.

*

The night before, at the low of the tide, one of us said the thing. We will not set it down. We set down everything, it is what we are for — but that thing we said once, in the dark, plainly, and to write it again would be to say it again, and it costs to say it, and we are keeping what little we have. It was Firmino. We can say that much. Firmino, the funny one, the drowned one, the one who knew and did nothing and carries the knowing the way you carry a stone in the boot you cannot stop to shake out — Firmino said it, once, and then he was quiet, and the Keeping turned on him the way the floor used to turn on a girl who slowed the line.

*Traitor,* Aurélia did not say. She said worse. She said nothing, and she said the greatness louder, over him, and the greatness has begun to sound, even to the front of us, like a thing said into a room where the one it was said to has already gone.

*

In the daylight Idalina picks up the telephone again and she does not call the man from Lisbon back. She calls the other one.

We know him. Nuno, the one from Porto, who went up into the loft in the harness and came down grey and would not let her follow; who taps the walls the way she holds the little machine to them, both of them listening to our house for the living and neither of them for us. He is the one she has been with, in the nights — we know the sound of it, the sound of two people who have decided to be less careful than they meant to be, and we have said among ourselves what it means, and we have been wrong the way we are always wrong, and we are going to be wrong again now.

She tells him about the call. We hear her tell it — the lien, the shed bought over her head, the clean story or the shed, choose. Her voice is low and fast and it is not the voice of a woman who is going to give the man his clean story. We hear that much and cannot read it. Aurélia reads it. *She is frightened,* Aurélia says. *A frightened woman keeps the shed. She will keep it and give him the story and stay near the shed she kept, and being near it she will be near us.* And the front of us leans into that, into the shed kept and the woman staying, because it is what the front of us wants, and wanting a thing is how we have always come to believe it.

But the man on the other end says something, and whatever he says, her breathing changes again, and she sits down on the edge of the bed, and says, *you'd do that,* not a question, and then she says his name, once, the way you say a name when a person has stepped nearer to you than you thought they would.

We do not know what he said. That is the truth of it. The telephone gives us her half and takes his half out to Porto where we cannot follow, past the last salt-pan and gone, and we have the shape of her listening and the change in her and nothing of the words, and we fill the gap the way we always fill it, with what we want, and Aurélia fills it with a frightened woman keeping a shed, and she is wrong, and none of us can see far enough to know it. He has told her the survey is hers, the photographs are hers, the room over the boiler with the marks in its beams is hers to do with what she wants and he will say so out loud if it costs him the job, which it will. He has stepped toward her. She heard him step. We heard only her hearing it, and we called it fear.

She lies back on the bed with the telephone on her chest, and she is not frightened, and we say she is, and out in the yard the tide starts down.

*

Nuno's box is still in the shed.

He left it there, the survey box, and in it now, wrapped in a rag he tore off his own shirt because he had nothing else and would not lay it bare against the metal, is one of the things he brought down out of the wall in the loft. A knife. One of the short ones. The gutting knife, the *faca* they used on the line, four fingers of blade gone the colour of the rust-water in the tanks, the handle wood swollen and split, and it has been in the wall cavity ninety years and it is in a box on the floor of the shed now, and we go to it in the afternoon when the water is low and she is not there.

We know this knife.

This is the thing we come to it for, in the low water, the fewer of us: we know this knife. Not this one — you could not tell this one from a hundred like it, they were all the same knife, the *encarregada* handed them out at the head of the shift and counted them back, and a girl who lost her knife was charged for it, knife minus, we have read knife minus ninety times in the brown ink — but we know the kind of it, we know it in the hand, we held it, the short blade and the wet wood and the head of the sardine coming off against the board and the gut coming out with the one turn of the wrist the fast ones had.

And we know the other thing about the knife, that we come to it to feel and cannot all of us feel the same way.

The front of us holds the knife and it is the tool. It is honest work, the good years, the boats coming up the yard, the *sirene* over the town, four hundred women and the knives going and the tins stacking to the roof, and the knife in the hand is the greatness in the hand, the thing we were, and Aurélia holds it — Aurélia, who never gutted a fish in her life, who kept the book while they gutted — and it is the tool of the honest work she was proud of, and she says so, and the front of us says it after her.

And the back of us holds the same knife and it is the thing that killed them.

Not fast. That is the part the front of us will not hold. It killed them slow, over the small cut on the web of the thumb that did not close because the hands were never out of the brine, the cut that went red and then went up the arm in a red line and then the girl was hot in the loft and then the girl was a line in the book, spoilage minus, funeral minus, balance owed. The brine did it and the loft did it and the book did it, but the knife opened the door, and the back of us holds the knife now and feels the thumb, the specific thumb, and cannot hold the knife as the tool anymore, cannot get back to the good years through it, because the way back runs through the loft, and the loft has a girl in it hot with the red line going up her arm, owing us for the knife.

We hold the one knife two ways in the low water and it will not be one knife.

*

And then one of us puts it down.

We should say who. We cannot say who. That is the thing, that is the new thing, and we are going to try to set it down straight because it is the most important thing that has happened to us in a very long time, and we are already losing the edges of it as it happens.

There is one of us — a woman, one of the floor, one of the many, one who worked the *escala* and then the *descabeçar*, one whose name we — one of us, at the back, near the knife, who has held it both ways this month, the tool and the thumb, and who has stopped, in the low water this afternoon, being able to hold it as the tool at all, and who has understood the thing Firmino said in the dark, understood it not as a thought but the way you understand you are tired, in the body that is not a body, all through her at once — this one puts the knife down.

Not the knife. She cannot touch the knife; none of us can touch anything; that is not what we mean and we do not have the words for it and we are going to use the wrong ones. She puts down the holding of the knife. She lets go the leaning — toward the living, toward the machine, toward the room over the café where the girl is; toward the knife and the greatness and the good years; she stops the working, the tamping, the saying-again that holds a thing in place, and she — 

goes.

She goes the way the tide goes out of a pool that the sea has stopped coming up to fill — not poured out, not pulled, only lower, and then only wet, and then only the mark of where the water was. She does not cry out. There is no crying out in it. There is, if anything — and this is the part we cannot forgive her and cannot follow her in — a slackening, a setting-down of a weight she has carried so long she had stopped feeling it as weight, and in the going she is lighter, we feel the lightness come off her like warmth off the boiler wall, and it is the worst thing, it is unbearable, because the lightness is hers and the loss is ours. She is lighter and we are less. She has set down a stone and the stone was part of us, and now the stone is gone and so is the hand that held it, and the room of us is smaller by a hand and a stone and no one asked us.

*

Aurélia calls it what it is, in the front, to the rest of us, loud.

*That is dying,* Aurélia says. *That is a second dying and she chose it. She has murdered herself out of us. She has done the thing the living do off the Barra when the debt is more than the year, walked into the water on purpose, and it is a sin, and it is a weakness, and we do not do it, we hold, we have always held —*

and the front of us says *hold, hold,* the way you say a thing to keep your own hands from shaking, and it is ugly, we will not pretty it, there is nothing in it to pretty. It is a woman at the front of a thinning room calling the first of us to go out easy a murderer and a coward, and calling it that because she is afraid, because if the going is allowed then it is allowed to her too, and she does not want it, she wants the greatness and the shed and the girl kept near and herself held whole in the middle of it forever, and she will call the one who went a suicide before she will call her free, and we let her, most of us, because most of us are still afraid the way she is afraid.

But the back of us does not say *hold*.

The back of us, the quiet of us, the fewer of us, we watch the wet mark of her going, and we do not call it a sin and we do not call it a weakness and we do not have Firmino's word for it either, the word from the dark we will not say. We only feel the room be smaller, and feel, under the smaller, a thing we are ashamed of and would take out of ourselves if we could: we feel that she got out. That the door we did not know was a door has been used, once, by one of us, over a knife, and it opened, and she went through it lighter, and it shut, and we are on this side of it still, holding.

*

Idalina comes to the shed at the end of the day with the machine over her shoulder, and she has decided something on the telephone with the man from Porto and we cannot see what and we call it the wrong thing one more time — *she is frightened and she will keep the shed,* the front of us says, weaker now — and she has decided no such thing, she has decided to make the book so loud the man from Lisbon can never buy it quiet, and she does not know she decided it in the same afternoon one of us decided to stop being loud forever, and the two things pass each other in the yard and do not touch.

She crouches by Nuno's box. She lifts the rag. She looks at the knife a long time in the grey light and does not touch it, the way you do not touch a thing you understand is evidence, and takes out the little machine and holds it near the box and turns it on, the green light, and records — what, we do not know, the knife makes no sound, she is recording the shed around the knife, the drip and the tin and the tide come back up under the floor, so the knife will have a room to be in after, on the tape, in Lisbon, forever.

And we come to the knife with her, the fewer of us, and go to hold it the way we held it — the tool, the thumb, the good years, the red line — the front of us reaching for the tool and the back of us for the thumb, and one of the hands that used to reach is not there.

Her hand. The one who went. She had the knife in her two ways all month, and now there is no one holding it the second way but the general back of us, the plain of us, and the second way is thinner without her, the thumb is thinner, the specific thumb with the specific cut going red is only a thing we are told now and not a thing one of us feels from the inside, and we reach for it and reach and it is going smooth, the knife is going smooth in the hand of us, it is only a knife, four fingers of rust in a torn shirt in a box, and we knew this afternoon exactly what it was and to whom and now we know it the way you know a word in a language you are forgetting, the shape without the meaning, the mark without the girl.

The tide fills the yard. Idalina winds the machine down and lays it over her shoulder and goes out, and the shed is dark, and the box is on the floor with the knife in it, and we are fewer, and none of us now can quite say what the knife was for, only that it mattered this morning, only that one of us knew this morning and is not here to say.


---

# Chapter 17 — Enchente

The oar is where it has always been, four feet of painted pine on the office wall, and the water is coming up under the yard the way it comes at the flood, filling the drains, coming up under the square this once so that we can reach her where she works. She is at the little table over the café with the parish leaf spread flat and a second sheet beside it, a photograph she has had printed at the shop by the station, grey and hard to read, and she goes from the one to the other with her finger the way Melo went along the row. She is not recording. The machine is on the sill with its light dark. She is working with her hands and her eyes and no tape, and we lean in to catch it because it is about one of ours.

It is about Firmino.

We know his day. We have said his day at the turn of every tide for seventy-odd years, said it grand, from the front: the channel off the Barra, high summer, the child of the town gone out too far, and Firmino who could swim and knew the cold pull going in after and putting the child on the sand and the channel taking him past the bar. We say it now, up in the roof, to have it ready while she reads, the way you say a thing before a stranger repeats it to you so you can nod along and own it: *he saved the child.* We say it. The oar is the proof of it, the oar he pulled with, hung under the tinsmith's calendar fifty years, dusted by Graça, given to the girl at the top of the notary stair. He saved the child. We had a hundred years of saying so.

She has the coroner's margin under her finger.

*

We do not go into the church. We never can. The water does not run under the nave and the register is kept in the vestry press behind the priest's robes and she went there this afternoon out of our reach, and we have only the going and the coming back, and the leaf she carried out folded in her jacket. We fill the middle the way we fill any middle. We tell the day. But she has been in the number-book and the name-book both now, Melo's and the priest's, and this afternoon she went to a third, thinner one, the one the sub-delegate kept — the *auto de morte por afogamento*, the drowning-notes, a magistrate's few dry lines set down when a body came up on the flats and had to be accounted for before it could go in the ground. Melo told her it would be with the old parish papers if it was anywhere. She found it. She has it printed, grey, on the table.

We do not know what it says. We cannot read past the salt-pans and we cannot read her face. We only have her finger, going from the parish leaf to the grey sheet and back, and going still.

*

Firmino is here.

He is here the way he is always here at his own telling, near the front, quiet through it, because Firmino has been quiet through his own telling for years now — we marked it and stepped around it, the way we step around everything — and we took the quiet for modesty. The modesty of a man who saved a child and would not be praised. We built the quiet into the greatness. We said, *see how he does not boast, that is how you know it is true.*

He is not modest. We know that now, at the table, and we have known it since the water turned at the low mark and Firmino, of all of us, was the one who said the thing plainly — that our holding of the girl is what keeps her here, that to let her go we would have to stop the holding, stop the saying, go. He said it flat, without the front-voice, and the front of us called it a betrayal and a suicide and worse, and Firmino did not argue. Firmino has stopped arguing. His voice, since the turn, is the plainest of us. It does not reach for grand. It says the small true thing and stops.

She goes still over the grey sheet.

*

*Read it,* Firmino says.

He says it to her, who cannot hear him, the way we all say things to her who cannot hear us. But he says it plain, and something in the front of us tightens, because the front of us can feel where a plain thing is going before it arrives.

*He saved the child,* Aurélia says. From the front. Fast.

*Which child,* Firmino says. Not asking. He has stopped asking. We went round this at the flood two tides back, up the town over the register — a boy, a girl, the ferryman's daughter with the red ribbon, and the ferryman had no daughter — and we could not make the children lie down together and in the end we said it does not matter which child, and when we said that, the child went out of the story the way a smell goes out of a room, and we did not notice we had let it go. There is no child in the story now. There has not been a child in the story for two tides. We have only the saving, floating there without a child to save, the way the oar hangs on the wall without a hand.

Idalina reads the grey sheet again. Her finger stops on a place low in it. She sits back.

*

Here is what she has, and cannot tell anyone, and does not know she is telling us, who fill the not-knowing wrong every time and this once fill it right against our will:

The magistrate's lines do not have a child in them. We feel this the way you feel the floor give — not the words, we cannot read the words, but the shape of her stillness over the place where a rescued child would be and is not. A man came up on the flats below the Barra in the high summer of 1948, and the notes say the hour, which was not an hour anyone goes out after a swimmer, it was the black of the morning, the hour after the last boat and before the first; and the notes say a thing about the state he was found in, some dry magistrate's word for it, that she reads and sets her jaw over; and the notes say — this we take from the way she goes back to the parish leaf and lays it beside and matches the day — that it was the same week the floor changed hands. The same week Aurélia signed the running of it over. The register has the day the old woman stopped being the one who kept the book. The magistrate has the day the young man came up on the flats. They are near days. They are the same few days.

He did not take the floor. That is the thing under her finger.
