Firmino, who was to have taken the running of it from his mother, the head-count and the minus-marks and the loft over the boiler and the girls in the straw and the funerals charged against balances owed — Firmino was handed all of it in the high summer of 1948, the whole machine of it, board minus knife minus, and Firmino went out in the black of the morning past the last boat, alone, with whatever was in him, and did not come back, and there was no child, there was never any child, there was a young man who was shown what he was to inherit and went into the water rather than keep the book.

*

We are quiet in the roof.

It is not the quiet of the greatness, the grand held quiet we built around his modesty. It is the other quiet, the one the floor got when the *sirene* stopped mid-shift. Firmino is here in it and Firmino does not fill it. He lets it be what it is.

*There was no child,* says one of us, from the back, the plain part, the part that has been getting plainer since the table.

*There was a child,* Aurélia says.

But she says it thin. She has been saying the certain things thin since the book came up out of the box, since her own ink was read aloud at the table, and this is another of the certain things and it comes out with the scrape in it we first heard over the book, the great voice catching. *There was a child,* she says, and the after-saying does not come. We do not say it after her. The front of us that would have said it after her — that said *we were great* and *we were always kind* and *he saved the child* in one smooth breath for a hundred years — the front of us reaches for the after-saying and finds the plain part of us has already put the thing down.

*No,* Firmino says.

He says it to his mother, plainly, without the front-voice, the way he has said everything since the turn. *No. There was no child. There was the floor. There was the keeping of it, handed to him in the black of the morning. And we would not —* and the saying does not close on a self to have refused it, it stays in the pool, in the plain of us, *and we would not keep it, and we went into the water instead of keeping it.* It is the plainest thing said in the roof in ninety years, and it comes from the place in him that has not thinned, and for a moment Firmino is nearly a man again and not a story, and the roof of us holds still around him the way the water holds still at the flood before it decides.

Aurélia does not answer.

*

We look at the oar.

It is on the office wall, painted pine, the blue gone to the colour of old milk, the worn place on the loom where a hand gripped it. We hung the greatness on that worn place. The hand that wore it smooth was the hand that pulled the child to the sand — we said. The oar was the proof. When Fátima said *coitado* at the stone, the oar was what the word rested on. When the man with the brochure wanted the greatness in iron letters, the oar was the picture he would have put beside it, the drowned hero, four feet of painted pine.

And we look at it now, at the flood, with the plain part of us that has put the child down, and we reach for why it matters.

We had it. We had it a hundred years. *This is Firmino's oar, this is the oar he saved the child with, this is the proof of the greatness.* We say it to hold it, the way we held number fourteen at the table, the way we hold everything, by saying. *This is the oar he —*

And there is no child to have saved with it.

And there was, the notes say, no rescue, no swimmer, no cold pull braved — there was a man in the black of the morning going out past the last boat with the floor handed to him, and a hand that gripped the loom, but not for a child, not for the sand, only for going, and the oar in the boat when the boat came in empty and the man did not, and we cannot make the oar mean the thing we hung on it, because the thing is gone, the child is gone, the saving is gone, and the oar —

the oar is on the wall.

We know it is Firmino's. That we still have. It was in his boat. But we reach for why it is on the wall, why Graça dusted it fifty years, why it was carried up to the notary and given to the girl like a thing to be kept — and the reason is a child that was never there, a greatness the book has peeled, a proof of nothing, and we cannot get the reason back, it is a row of little marks now, it is a shape without the weight of it, painted pine and a worn place, and we know a breath ago it was the most honoured thing in the fábrica and now it is only wood, only four feet of wood on a wall under a calendar no one turns, and the honour has gone out of it the way the child went out of the story, and we let it, and we did not mean to let it, and it is gone.

*

Idalina folds the grey sheet.

She does it slowly, along the fold it came with, and she sets it on top of the parish leaf, and she puts her hand flat on the two of them the way Fátima put her hand flat on the oilcloth over the book. She does not weep. We watch her not weep. She is thinking, we tell ourselves — and here we go wrong again, we cannot help it, we fill her face that we cannot see with the thing that is bearable to us. We tell ourselves she is thinking of the family, of what she has found out about the drowned uncle, of the sale, of what a man from Lisbon will pay for a greatness that turns out to have a boy in the water for no reason anyone would print.

She is not thinking of us. Graça, at the edge, near her daughter, keeps her eyes on the face we cannot see and does not tell us what is there, only goes a little more out of herself, a little more toward the girl, spending what she cannot afford. The girl has a dead uncle who went into the water rather than keep his mother's book, and the girl is the last of the line, and the girl came here to sign and sell and be gone in three days and has been here weeks now over a debt-book and a drowning-note, and whatever is in her face is hers, and it goes past us into the room over the café, and we do not have it.

She picks up the machine off the sill. She does not turn it on. She holds it a moment, the black weight of it, the thing that takes and does not remember — and she sets it back down, dark, and this too we mark and cannot read: that she has found the truest thing yet about her own dead and has not put it on the tape. She wants to hold it in her, we think, the way we used to say we held things. She wants it in the body and not the loop. But we do not know that. We decide it, because deciding is what we have instead of seeing.

*

Up in the roof the water stands at the full, enchente come to its height under the yard, and we say the day.

We say it the way we always say it, from the front, because the saying is old and the mouth of us goes on with the old saying the way a hand goes on with old work: *the channel off the Barra, high summer, the child gone out too far, and Firmino who could swim —*

and it stops there now. The front of us says it and the plain part of us does not carry it and it stops at *Firmino who could swim,* and there is no child at the end of it, there is a young man and the floor handed to him and the black of the morning, and the front of us reaches past *who could swim* for the saving and the sand and the greatness, and reaches, and there is the water and the man and no reason we are allowed to keep, and the saying that we said grand for a hundred years lies in the roof now with its end torn off it, a thing we can begin and cannot finish, and Firmino, quiet, plain, does not help us finish it.

Aurélia does not say *he saved the child.*

She has stopped saying it. We hear her not say it — the great voice, that would have driven it over any doubt the way she drove the greatness over Rosa's name, and it does not come, and where it does not come there is a hole in the front of us the shape of a thing we all said together for ninety years, and none of us fills it. The oar hangs on the wall. It was in his boat. We know that much. We reach for the rest of it, for what it was on the wall for, and the reaching comes back with wood in it and nothing else, and the water turns at the top of the flood and starts down, and we do not have why the oar mattered, and we will not have it at the next high water, and we do not tell ourselves we will.


---

# Chapter 18 — Vazante

The man from Lisbon has come down to the fábrica himself this time, and he has brought the booklet, and he lays it on the lip of the middle pio the way you lay a caught fish on the stones to be admired. It is the ebb outside. The water is going out of the yard, off the black mud, out of the reeds, and it goes out of us too where we are gathered along the beams over the tanks, thinner at the ebb, less able to gather, so that the two of them below — Idalina and the man, Caldas — come to us in pieces, a shoulder, a voice, the flat of a hand on the booklet. He has kept it dry inside his coat against the damp of our house, and he sets it down and turns it so the front faces her, and there is a picture on it, and words over the picture in a thin gold that catches the grey light off the flats.

She does not pick it up.

*

We can read some of it from the beams. Not the small print — we do not read the way the living read, we have not for a long time — but the picture is large and the gold words are large and the summed lean of us takes them. The picture is the chimney. Our chimney, over the flats, the one that has forgotten it was for anything, and in the picture it is clean, the tin roof is not the colour of old blood, the yard is not mud but a pale stone with little trees in pots, and where the boats used to bring the sardines up in baskets there are chairs, white chairs, and a woman in a white robe walking toward a door with steam coming off her the way steam came off nothing in this building but the boiler and the tanks and the girls. Over the picture the gold says a thing with our name in it. Sequeira. The front of us takes the shape of the name and is pleased, the way you are pleased to hear your name said in a room before you have heard what is said after it.

*Sequeira Salt and Sardine,* Caldas says, reading it for her, because she has not picked it up. *Heritage.* And a word after that we do not take, a soft word, for a place you pay to be made clean in.

The front of us leans down toward the name in gold and says the thing it says. *We were great.* Aurélia says it. She is thinner than she was — since the table, since the book came up, there is a scrape in her that was not there in the good years — but she says it still, *we were great, look, they have put us in gold,* and the front of us says it after her, and it goes partway smooth and then it does not, because the back of us has stopped.

The back of us stopped at the table and has not started again. There is Firmino at the back now, which there was not before — Firmino who was at the front all his death with his oar and his drowning and his saved child, and there is no saved child now, we have looked and there is none, the child went out of the story on the flood tide and did not come back, and Firmino came to the back when it left, plainer than he has ever been, and stands in the thin place near the little weight of Rosa that came up at the table and has not gone down. The back of us looks at the gold and says nothing. The saying comes apart in the middle of the room, and we are two, a we and a we, the slack between us wide as the whole ebbing yard.

*

Idalina puts one finger on the booklet and turns it a few degrees, not toward herself, away, so the gold does not face her, and this is a thing we take and cannot read. The front of us reads it as *she is looking closer.* The front of us has been sure since the first evening that she came to sell the greatness, that she would sell it, that we had read her last page — *sign, sell, gone,* three days, one bag — and every day since she has done a thing that was not on the page we read, and the front of us has said each time, *yes, but now she will,* the way you say the fish is coming when the line goes slack. Caldas takes the turned booklet for reluctance, a price, a woman working a better number out of him; we hear him lower his voice into the one men use when they think the thing is nearly done.

"The building I can buy from anyone," he says. "The name I can only buy from you. And the name is the thing, Dona Idalina. People don't come to the coast to sleep in a shed. They come to sleep in a *story.*" He touches the gold with two fingers. "Your family's story. The salt, the sea, the hard clean work. The girls at the tables with their knives, the whole town smelling of the catch. A hundred years of it, and your name over the door."

The girls at the tables with their knives.

He says it lightly, the way you would if you did not know, and he does not know, there is nothing in the man that knows; he has a picture of a woman in a white robe and the word for a clean place, and under the picture is the mud, and under the mud the tank, and over the tank the loft, and in the loft the girls at the tables with their knives, and one of them is standing at the back of us right now with almost nothing left to stand with, listening to a living man price her knife as heritage.

*

"No," Idalina says.

She says it quietly. She is not a loud woman; we have learned that, the way she keeps her voice down even to the machine, even alone. She says *no* and then she says more, and here the ebb takes some of it from us, a clause goes out with the water and we do not have it, we have the shape of her jaw and the flat of her hand come down on the booklet, not hard, but down, covering the gold, covering the chimney and the white chairs and the name.

"—not going to sell you that," she is saying when the water gives her back. "There's a ledger. In the estate. Wages that weren't wages. Girls brought from the interior on a debt they couldn't clear, and worked, and when they died their families were charged for the funerals against what they still owed. Dozens of them. That's what's under your spa."

Caldas is quiet a moment. The two fingers come off the gold.

"Then we don't put that in the booklet," he says.

He says it reasonably. He is a reasonable man. You keep the salt and the sea and the hard clean work, he says, and you leave out the other; every old money in this country has an other, and no one is served by *airing* it, and the town needs the work, the town is dying, she has seen the shops with the paper in the windows — is she going to let a book of old sums keep forty jobs out of a place that has nothing, out of a Lisbon conscience she can afford because she is leaving anyway —

He should not have said *leaving anyway.* We feel it land in her even from the beams — a stillness in the shoulder, the machine-stillness she has when a sound comes she means to keep.

*

The front of us is glad of the man.

We must say this, because it is true and the true things are the ones we are here to say again until they stay: the front of us is *glad* of Caldas. Glad of a living man who wants the greatness so badly he will pay to hang our name over a door, because if the name is worth hanging then we were something, then the book on the table was only the old accounts and the girl at the back should go back down. The front of us leans toward the booklet with the same lean it gives the living. *Keep it. Take his gold. Hang the name.* Aurélia leans hardest, scrape and all — because the greatness said in gold by a stranger is the last place it is still smooth, still holds, still means what it meant in the good years, and if she covers it with her hand and says *no* then the last smooth place is gone and there is only the book, only *funeral, minus,* only the thing we made a roof with no room for.

*She will keep it,* the front of us says. *She has turned the booklet toward her. She will keep it.* And the back of us, looking at her hand come down flat over the gold, does not say she will keep it. The back of us has watched her too long now to say what she will do.

*

Nuno is here. We did not mark him come in — at the ebb we mark things late — but he is off by the cold boiler with his notebook shut in his hand, and he has heard the thing about the ledger and the girls, and he has gone still in a different way than Idalina goes still. He knows the loft. He is the one who went up, in the harness, read the beam with his thumb, brought the hard things and the soft thing out of the wall, and he stands by the boiler now doing a sum of his own, and it is not a sum about sardines or steam.

Caldas turns to him. "Nuno. Tell her. The building's steel and money, it's not—" and the word this time we do have, the water gives it clean, "—it's not a *séance.* Tell her what it costs to walk away from what I'm offering."

And Nuno says, "I'm not going to tell her that."

He says it looking at the boiler, not at Caldas. The survey is done and it is Caldas's, he says, the loads and the roof and the north wall, he was paid for it and it is finished — but the rest of it, and here he looks up at the shut door of the sótão over the boiler, the door he is the only living man to have gone past, the rest of it he is not going to help make into a booklet. He says it the way you say a thing you have just this moment decided and find you decided a while ago. He'll finish out the week. He does not say more, and Caldas understands that a man he was paying has stopped being a man he is paying, and the reasonableness goes out of the developer's face the way the water is going out of the yard, all at once and without any sound, and he picks up his booklet off the lip of the pio and puts it back inside his dry coat.

"Think about the town," Caldas says, at the door. "You get to leave. They don't."

Then he is gone, and his good car starts in the yard, and it goes out along the road past the salt-pans, and we lose it at the last pan the way we lose everything there, the sound of it dropping off the edge of our water into the place we cannot follow.

*

The booklet is gone. It went out in the dry coat.

The front of us reaches for it — the name in gold, the greatness said smooth by a stranger — reaches, and *we were great, we were great in gold, they wanted to hang our name* — and reaches — and it is a picture of a chimney. That is all we can get of it now. A clean chimney and white chairs and a woman with steam on her, and we know it mattered, we knew a breath ago exactly how it mattered and to whom, it mattered more than anything has in years because it was the last smooth place, and now there is the picture and not the weight of it, the chimney and not the reason, and the front of us says *we were great* to get the reason back and it does not come, it is a sound now, *great, great,* holding nothing, and the man has driven the gold past the last salt-pan, and we cannot any longer say why we wanted it.

*

And here is the thing that happens then, at the ebb, that has not happened before and the front of us cannot stop.

The back of us says the greatness back to the front. Not to keep it. To hear it.

The back of us — Firmino, plain now, and the little weight of Rosa, and the quiet ones who stopped at the table — says it flat, the way Melo read the book, a clerk's voice for a clerk's greatness: *we were all here, every one of us, forty in the roof. We were always kind to the interior girls.* It says the old sayings over, plainly, with no warmth in them at all, and hung in the ebbing air of our own house, with the booklet gone and the book still on the table over the café and the loft door shut over the boiler where a living man will not send anyone up — the old sayings are obscene. They were always obscene. *We were all here, forty in the roof* — and we are not forty, we have not been since before she came, we say forty over a hole where the flu-dead used to lean, and now we have heard ourselves say it and heard the hole in it. *We were always kind to the interior girls* — and Rosa is at the back with almost nothing left, thinned by our own not-saying for ninety years, and we have heard ourselves say we were kind to her over the top of her, and we cannot un-hear it. The front of us tries to take the sayings back, to row on with the greatness the way you row on when the boat is already on the sand — and it cannot. Once the back has said the thing flat and the front has heard how it sounds, there is no getting back up the water once it has started down.

We are not one voice anymore. We will not be one voice again. There was a *we* that could say *we were great* and mean it and go smooth and be a comfort — the whole of us, the unanimous us, the forty in the roof leaning together and seeing the living whole — and that *we* is gone, it went out somewhere between the first high water and this ebb, and we did not mark the hour, which is the way of it, we never mark the hour. From here it is the front and the back. The Keeping and the Letting. *Some of us* and *some of us,* and the slack between, and no whole of us over the top to say *forty* and make it true.

*

Idalina stays a while after the cars are gone. She and Nuno.

We watch them from the beams and we get less of it than we would have in the good years, when a crowded roof-full of us took a living scene whole. Now there are gaps. There is her, sitting on the lip of the middle pio where the booklet was, and him, come away from the boiler, sitting near her but not against her, and a thing between them we take in pieces and cannot assemble — she says a thing, he says a thing, she almost laughs and does not, he puts his hand somewhere near her and takes it back. The front of us, what is left of it, says *she will stay for the man. She has lost the sale, but the man is in Porto, so she will not go far, and if she does not go far she does not go, and if she does not go we are held.* It says it because it must say something, because the greatness is gone smooth and empty and it needs a new smooth thing to say, and the man will do.

But the back of us has heard him say *I'll finish out the week,* the same shape as her *sign, sell, gone,* a man who has already packed one bag in his head; and the back of us has watched her not lean into his hand when it came near; and the back of us does not say she will stay for the man. It says nothing about her at all. It has stopped saying her last page. It only watches, in the gaps the ebb leaves, two people in a cold shed who both know one of them is leaving, taking stock of a thing before it is over, the way you fold a net you will not use again.

Then the light goes flat and grey over the flats, the light this town has instead of an evening, and she stands, and he stands, and they go out to the two cars, and the cars go, and we are alone in the fábrica with the tanks and the boiler and the shut loft door, the front and the back of us, the tide all the way out now, the mud bare in the yard where the white chairs were in the gold.

The front of us reaches, one more time, for the greatness, to say it and go smooth and be a comfort, the way we always could.

And the back of us hears it, and it is not a comfort, and the front of us hears the back of us hearing it, and it is not smooth, and there is no one over the top of us anymore to say *forty,* to say *we were all here,* to make the number smooth and meaning nothing and holding nothing that can be lost by holding it — there is no whole of us left to say it — and so the greatness goes unsaid, for the first time in a hundred years unsaid, and the front and the back stand apart in the dark with the ebbing yard between them. Out in the yard the tide is all the way down. The mud is bare where the white chairs were in the gold. The loose north sheet lifts once in the nortada and drops, lifts and drops, over the shut loft door, over the tanks, over the two of us that used to be one.


---

# Chapter 19 — Baixa-mar

She has the book open on the pio again, the middle tank, the one whose black water she covered once with a booklet and once with her flat hand and now with the wage-ledger itself, spread on the iron lip where the wood used to be. It is low water outside. The yard is bare, the mud gone grey and shining a little where the last of the tide left it, and the light comes in flat under the roof and lies on the pages. She has brought a lamp, a small one on a wire, clipped to the tank's rim, and it makes a yellow place on the book and leaves the rest of the shed the colour it always is. Melo is not here. She has the old hand in her own head now, some of it; she has the parish leaf folded in the book at one page and Fátima's names in her, and she reads the way you read a thing you have decided to read all the way to the end.

We are along the beams. Fewer of us than there were. We do not say how many.

*

She reads with a pencil. This is new. Before, she turned the pages and set the book down and left it; now she has a pencil, and she makes a small mark in the margin beside a line, and turns back to the parish leaf, and makes another mark there, and holds the two together with her thumb and her finger the way Nuno held the beam. She is matching. She is putting the number against the name against the day against the cause, and she is doing it slowly, out loud, under her breath, not for the machine — the machine is off, the green light dark, she does not want this on the tape, we have learned that in her by now — but for herself, so the sounds are real, so a thing said is harder to close the book on than a thing only looked at.

*Board,* she says. *Knife. Lodging. Cloth.* And then the word that is not like the others. *Funeral.*

She says it at no. 14 and she says it at no. 7 and she says it at no. 22 and she says it at the two lines that are only *the Conceição girls, interior,* with no first name to tell them apart, and each time she says *funeral* she makes the small mark and her mouth goes to a line.

The front of us leans down to say the greatness over it, the way we always could.

*

Aurélia leads it. She has led it since before this woman was born, since before the woman's mother, since the good years when the roof was full and the water high and she stood at the front of us the way she stood at the front of the floor. She says it now the way she has always said it, and we mean to say it after her, all of us who are left at the front, and hold the sound down over the reading the way you hold a lid on a pot.

*We were great,* Aurélia says. *We gave the interior girls work. We took them off the roads and out of the poorhouses and the failed farms of the interior where there was nothing, no bread, no work, no —*

She stops.

She does not mean to stop. We feel her not mean to. There is a word that comes next, there has always been a word that comes next, she has said the sentence ten thousand times and the word has always been there when she reached the place for it — and she reaches the place for it, *no bread, no work, no —*, and the woman below says, quietly, to the book, *no. 31, interior, taken on at eleven,* and Aurélia's word is not there.

*We gave them work,* she says again, going back to the start of the smooth part, the way you go back over ice. *We gave them work, we fed them, we —*

*Board, minus,* the woman says. *Cloth, minus.*

*— we housed them, we —*

*Lodging, minus.*

And the sentence Aurélia has said all her death, the one she led us in, the one that went smooth as a stone in the mouth of the whole of us, comes apart on her tongue clause by clause as the woman reads the price of each clause off the book. *We fed them* — board, minus. *We clothed them* — cloth, minus. *We gave them a bed over the boiler where it was warm and dry* — lodging, minus; and the boiler is cold now under the woman's lamp and the loft door is shut over it, and the warm dry bed is a room a living man will not send anyone up into, and Aurélia has run out of the smooth part of the sentence and come to the place where the sentence has always turned, before, to *and they were glad of it,* and she reaches for *glad* —

and *glad* is not there either.

*

We should say what happens to a voice when the thing it was made of goes out of it.

There is not a good way to say it. We have watched it happen to the small ones, to the ones at the back where the roof comes down, and it was quiet then, it was a lamp going down, a weight getting lighter until you could not find it. This is not quiet. Aurélia is not a small one. She is the most of us there has ever been in any one of us; she has been the front of us so long that the front of us is mostly her, her edge, her voice, the way she said *faster* on the floor and the way she said *we were great* in the roof, the same voice, we understand it now, the same voice for both. And the woman is reading the book that the voice was built over, the way a floor is built over a tank, and every line she reads takes a board out of the floor.

*No. 14,* the woman reads. *Rosa. Da Palha, the margin says — someone wrote it in later, a different hand.* She looks up at the roof, not at us, at the dark where the beams cross, as if she heard the little weight at the back of us go tight at her own name said in a living mouth. *Board, minus. Knife, minus — they charged her for the knife. Spoilage, minus, three times. Funeral, minus.* She stops. *Balance owed.* She says it and then she says the thing that is the whole of it, the thing the book was built to say without ever having to say it: *She died owing them money for the knife she was cut on and the funeral they buried her in.*

Aurélia does not say *we were great* over that. Aurélia says nothing over that. There is a place at the front of us where the saying comes from and the saying does not come.

*

The woman turns the page. There are more. This is the thing the front of us cannot hold against — that it is not one line, not Rosa, not a girl you could carry as a story the way we carried Firmino and his oar and his drowning; it is line after line, page after page, in the same hand, with the same columns, and the columns are always the same and only the numbers change. Benedita, no. 9, a name because someone said it once and it stuck to the number; then no. 10 and no. 11 with no names at all, only *interior, indenture, board −, spoilage −, balance owed;* then Aline, no. 12, who owes for two knives, which means she broke one, which means there was a day she broke a knife and was charged for it and kept gutting with the next one; then Maria-do-Ó, no. 19, whose funeral line is there and whose *balance owed* is the largest on the page because she was young when she came and lived longer than most, long enough to run up a debt for staying alive that long.

The woman reads them. She reads the numbers where there are no names, the way you would read anything, giving the number the same weight as a name because it is all there is. *No. 10. No. 11. No. 23.* And we, along the beams, who leaned once and saw the living whole and counted ourselves grandly at the high water and said *forty* and meant it, we hear the numbers read up out of the book and we cannot do the thing we have always done with numbers, we cannot make them go smooth. *No. 10* will not go smooth. It has a girl under it. It has a girl under it we sat over for ninety years and did not say, the way we did not say Rosa, the way we thinned them out of the back of us on purpose so the front of us could stay grand — and the front of us was Aurélia, and Aurélia kept the book, this book, in this hand, and wrote *interior* and wrote *minus* and wrote *balance owed* against a dead child of eleven, and led us in the greatness over the top of it, and we said it after her.

We said it after her.

*

The front of us breaks here. Not all at once. It breaks the way the north tin goes in the *nortada*, a sheet at a time, a fastening at a time, a sound and then a wider sound.

Aurélia tries once more. We feel her gather what is left of her edge and go at the sentence from a new place, not the girls, the sardines — *we packed the best fish on the coast, the cod-fleet ate our tins on the Banks, they knew our name in —* and she reaches for the place, the far place, Terra Nova, the Banks, the name of the far cold water her tins went out to, and it was a real thing, it was true, the tins were good, the name was known — and the woman below turns a page and reads *no. 14, funeral, minus,* again, because she has come to Rosa again in a second place, Rosa is in the book twice, once for the debt and once at the back where the deaths are totted up, and the second Rosa lands on Aurélia in the middle of the far cold name and the far cold name goes out.

*We were —* Aurélia says.

*We were great,* she says. *We —*

And it will not come. The *great* comes, the sound of it, but the weight the sound stood on is gone, the floor under it pulled up board by board by the woman with the pencil, and *great* stands there in the mouth of the front of us with nothing under it, a sound over a tank, and Aurélia hears it stand there empty and she does the thing she has never done in all her death, in all her leading of us: she stops leading. She lets the sentence go. She does not say the next word. The front of us, that was mostly her, that turned to her the way the floor turned to her, waits for the next word the way the floor waited for the *sirene*, and it does not come, and in the not-coming we feel her get — we have no other word — *lighter.* Not freed. Not forgiven. The book is still open, the woman is still reading, Rosa is still owed, no. 10 is still a number; nothing is made right, nothing is made clean, there is no white robe, no steam, no door to walk toward. Aurélia is only lighter, the way a thing is lighter when the thing it was carrying is set down not because it was set down but because the arms have stopped being able to hold it.

*

The woman closes the book.

She does it gently, with two hands, the way she laid the machine on the seat that first evening as if it were a person. She has read it to the end. She has said every name there was a name for and every number there was not, out loud, to no one she can see, in the cold shed at low water, and now she sits with her hand flat on the cover the way she covered the gold, and she does not weep, and she does not pray, and she does not say the thing a living person might say into a silence like this. She says, instead, a small practical thing, to herself, the sound-woman's thing, the leaving thing: *Somebody has to keep this.* And then: *Not here.*

We take it and we cannot hold it against the front of us the way we would have. The front of us that would have said *she will keep it here, she will keep us, we are held* — that front of us is standing in the wreck of its own sentence with *great* still empty in its mouth, and does not say what she will do. Only the back of us watches her hand on the cover, and the back of us has stopped saying her last page long ago, and says nothing now, and lets her decide the thing she is deciding, which we cannot see the whole of, which happens partly in her the way the deciding always happens partly in her, in the place we were never able to reach and told ourselves we had read.

*

Along the beams it is very quiet.

We are used to a quiet that is a lid on the loud — the floor after the *sirene* stopped, the roof after we have said the greatness and it has gone smooth and we lie in the smoothness like water gone still. This is not that quiet. This is the quiet of a thing that will not go smooth being said all the way to its end and then set down. The book is closed. The names are out of it now, in the air of the shed, in the woman, said; they will not go back into the numbers the way Rosa went back into *a row of little marks* at the café table, because the woman has said them all, not one, not as a scandal for a stranger, but every one, slowly, to herself, the way you say a thing you mean to carry.

And the front of us cannot say *we were great* over them anymore. It is not that we are forbidden. There is no one to forbid us. It is that the sentence is gone. Aurélia had it and Aurélia has set it down and the front of us was mostly Aurélia and now the front of us reaches for the sentence the way she reached for *glad,* the way she reached for the far cold name, and the sentence is not there, and there is no leader at the front to start it, because the one who started it has gone light and quiet and is not leading, and the rest of the front is only an echo with nothing to echo.

*We were,* the front of us says, into the low water, without her.

*We were,* it says again.

And there is no word after *were,* because the word after *were* was always hers to say, and she is not saying it, and we cannot find it in the pool where it used to be, and the woman below picks up the lamp off the tank's rim and the yellow place goes off the closed book and moves across the floor with her toward the door, and the shed goes back to the colour it always is, and the tide is all the way out, and we do not have the word, and we do not, this time, tell ourselves we will have it again at the next high water.

We do not say there will be a next high water.


---

# Chapter 20 — Enchente

She comes in on the flood, alone, before the light is up, and she has brought a ladder.

We hear the ladder before we understand it. It is aluminium and it knocks on the door-frame going in, a bright short sound, and then it knocks on the concrete, and then she is dragging it across the floor toward the boiler, and the sound of it dragging is the worst sound she has made in this house because we know where she is taking it. The water is coming up in the yard behind her. It is coming up through the reeds and over the black mud, the flood, the enchente, filling the tanks a little as it does, and it fills the room too, the way it fills the room, so that there are more of us gathered along the beams this morning than there have been in days. It does not help. We are more of us and we still cannot go where she is going.

She sets the ladder against the boiler stair, against the place where the iron gives out, and she goes up.

*

Nuno said no one goes up. Nuno said it to her and to Caldas both — the timbers are gone, the floor of the loft is rot, he would not send a man up without a harness and he would not send one up with one either, not now, the survey is done. He went up himself the once, in the harness, on a line, and came down grey. She was there. She heard him say it. She is a woman who keeps her voice down and listens to the room she is standing in, and she heard the man who knows the building say the loft will not hold a person, and she has come at the flood before the light with a ladder of her own, and she is going up anyway.

The front of us does not understand it. The front of us has had her wrong since the first evening, and it has her wrong now: it says she is going up for the man, to see what he saw, so she can tell his gold what to leave out. It says she is going up to price it. The front of us cannot stop pricing.

The back of us says nothing. The back of us has stopped saying her last page. It only listens for the sound of the rot giving under her, because that is a sound we would be able to keep.

*

We lose her at the loft door.

We have said this before and it is still true, truer at the flood when we are most of us gathered and it does us no good: the sótão is a room inside our own house that we cannot enter. We were never used up there. We gutted on the floor, we stood in the tanks, we fed the boiler, we counted heads at the stair — but the loft over the boiler is not ours, the loft is theirs, was theirs, the ones who slept in it locked, and the ones who slept in it locked are here in the pack of us now and cannot go back up into it either. So when she pushes the little door and it gives and she goes through on her hands and knees onto the rotten boards, she goes out of us. We have the sound of her. We have the boards taking her weight and not breaking. We have her breathing, which changes when she is through the door, goes shallow, the machine-stillness gone up into her chest. We do not have her face. We have never had her face out at the edges and we do not have it now, above us, in the room we cannot climb to, and we lean up toward the boards and get pieces — a knee, a wrist, the drag of a coat — and the pieces do not make her whole.

She is a sound over our heads now. That is all we have made of the last of the line. A sound over our heads, in the one room in the house that was never ours to be used in.

*

Above us she stops moving.

She has found the beam. We know it the way you know a thing through a floor — the quality of the stillness changes, the breathing stops and starts. She has found the beam with her hand, in the dark, because she has not brought a lamp up, or she has brought one and not turned it on, we cannot tell, we have only the sound and the sound says she is reading it with her fingers the way the man read it, the way you read a thing you cannot see. The tally-marks. The little cuts. Rows of them, a stroke and a stroke and a stroke and the fifth stroke laid across the four, the way you count days when no one will give you the number, the way you count what you are owed when the book says you owe.

The counter-book. The one the girls kept in the wood because they were not allowed the other one.

She reads it with her hand and then she does a thing we do not expect and the front of us cannot use: she says a name.

She says it out loud, up in the loft, to no one, in the dark, and it comes down to us through the rotten boards small and flat and living — a name off the parish leaf, a name off the mended bead, a name Fátima gave her at the stone. She has carried it up there to lay it against the marks. She reads a row of cuts and says the name that Melo read off the number that the row of cuts stands for, and the number and the name and the marks come together over our heads in a living mouth, and down here under the boards, in the pack of us, a thing tears that we cannot mend.

*

Here is the thing that tears.

The front of us reaches for the girl whose name she said — reaches to know her, to place her, to say *she was ours, she was one of ours, one of the interior girls we were always kind to* — reaches the way we reached in the good years and came up with the whole of a person. And it does not come up with the whole of a person. It comes up with two people, and they will not lie down together, and there is no one left in us to say which is the true one.

Because some of us, reaching, find the girl. Find her at the descabeçar table, sixteen, from the interior, the knife going, the debt going, board and knife and lodging and spoilage all going against a wage that was never paid, the loft locked at night, the flu in the loft, the funeral charged. Some of us find her and know her from the inside, from having been her, from having slept in the locked loft ourselves, and the finding is a wound.

And some of us, reaching for the same name, find the one who kept the book. Find the hand that wrote the number down where the name should have gone, that wrote *balance owed* under a dead girl, that signed the indenture and charged the funeral and called it charity, called it work, called it kindness. Some of us find her and know her from the inside too, from having been her, from having stood at the front of the room in the voice that meant faster.

And these are both us. This is the thing. We are the girl and we are the hand that wrote her down as a debt, and we are pooled now, we have been pooled a long time, the membrane is gone, and there is no edge left in us between the one who was killed in the loft and the one who did the killing with a pen on the floor below it. We reach for a single name and we cannot tell, out of our own pack, which of us was the one it belonged to and which of us was the one who took it away. The seam is gone. We have thinned across it. We cannot find it to hold it.

Aurélia — we say *Aurélia*, we reach for the front of us, for the grand voice, for the one who would sort this, who would say *we were great* and make it smooth — and the front of us is scattered this morning, since the reading, since the man from Lisbon read the whole book out at the table with every number in it and Aurélia's voice came apart in the middle of it and did not come back whole. There is a scrape where the front of us was. It cannot sort anything. It reaches for the name the living woman said, same as the rest of us, and it finds the girl and the hand both, same as the rest of us, and it cannot say which one it was, and that not-being-able is the worst thing that has happened to the front of us, worse than the gold going, worse than the book, because the front of us was the one thing in us that always knew who it was.

*

Above us, in the room we cannot climb to, she takes the shoe down.

We hear it. We hear her hand go into the wall cavity, into the place the man found, where the hard things are, the knives, the short line-knives with ninety years of the loft on them — and there is the soft thing there too, the edgeless thing, and she takes it out. And she takes something down out of the beams as well, out of the high dark join where the water threw it, or where a hand threw it, the small hard grey thing that has been up there the whole time. A child's shoe. She has it now. She brings the two together in her two hands, up there, and we hear her turn them over, and we cannot see them, and we do not need to see them, because we have them already, we have had them for a hundred years, we know them without eyes.

The shoe is Zé's.

We say it the way we have always said it, the wet-sand way, the true thing said again so it stays. It is Zé's shoe, from the beams, caught in the join where the water threw it in the year the water was high, Zé who was small, Zé who is one of ours. We reach to say Zé and lay him against the shoe the way she laid the name against the marks —

and some of us say the shoe is a girl's.

Not Zé's. A loft-girl's. One of the interior girls', small-footed, sixteen and small the way the hungry are small, come down through the little door into the beams — no. Not come down. Left up. A shoe left in the loft, in the wall, with the knives, by a girl who went out across the lagoon one night to leave and did not come to the other side, and the shoe stayed where she stepped out of it. Some of us have that. Some of us have the shoe as hers as surely as some of us have it as Zé's, and both are in the pool, and both come up when we reach, and there is no self left in us that was there to see which foot it came off.

We put it down between us and we cannot pick it up again.

We had it. We had it as Zé's this whole book, we had it as Zé's the first evening while she looked up at the roof and we said his name to have it ready. And now the same reaching brings up the girl, and the girl is as true as the boy, and the boy is as true as the girl, and we cannot hold them both and we cannot choose one, and the shoe goes the way the greatness went — not the shoe, the shoe is in her living hands now, the shoe stays — the *whose* of it goes. A breath ago we knew whose it was and how it mattered and to whom. Now there is a small grey shoe in a living woman's hand in a room over our heads, and we know it mattered, and we cannot any longer say why, or to which of our dead, or whether the dead it belonged to was a child of the workers or a child worked to death, and these were not the same thing, once, and we could once tell them apart, and now they are one weight in the pool with no name on it and no foot to fit it.

Custódia does not reach for it. Custódia let it be a shoe a long while ago. She is fainter than the shoe now.

*

The front of us tries, one more time, to be the front of us.

It reaches for the old sort, the one that always worked: *us and them.* The family and the girls. The greatness and the ones the greatness was got from. It reaches to stand the family on one side and the interior girls on the other, the way the book stood them, names on one side and numbers on the other, so that at least we would know which we were — reaches, and the reaching is the reading again, the same reading, and it comes up the same. Two people to one name. The killed and the one who wrote her down, and both of them us, and no seam.

We are not two kinds of dead in this roof. We thought we were. We said *we were great* on one side and we let Rosa slide on the other and we thought the saying and the sliding were done by different ones of us. They were not. They were done by the same *we*. There is one pack of us in the beams and it did both, is both, cannot now stand itself on two sides because there is only the one thinned pooled us and it holds the pen and the knife in the same missing hand.

The girl she named up there is at the back with almost nothing left. And the one who wrote the girl down as *balance owed* is at the back too, with almost nothing left, and we cannot get between them to say sorry to the one and turn from the other, because we cannot find the between. It is gone. It thinned with everything else. We are owed and we are the debt in the same breath, and the breath is running out.

*

She is coming down.

We hear the ladder take her weight, rung and rung, careful, and then her feet on the concrete, and she stands a moment by the cold boiler with the shoe in her hand and the knife, or the knives, in the other, and she is breathing the loft out of her chest. She sets the machine going — we hear the small feed of it, the room going onto the tape, the loft's nothing and the flood in the tanks and her own breath — and she does not say anything now. She said the names up there, in the dark, where they belonged, and she does not say them down here for the machine. She holds the shoe. She turns it over once more in the grey light that is coming now through the gap in the north tin. She looks at it the way you look at a thing you have decided to carry and not to keep, or to keep and not to carry, we cannot read which, we have never been able to read her, we only thought we could, back when there were enough of us to lean.

Then she puts the shoe in her coat, and the knives in her bag wrapped in something, and she picks the ladder up off the floor, and she carries all of it out into the flood-lit yard, and the tide is high now behind her, preia-mar coming, the fullest it comes, and we are as gathered as we get and we cannot follow her out and we could not follow her up, and up in the beams where the shoe was there is a join of two dark timbers with nothing in it, an empty place in the wood, and we reach for what was there, and we get a shoe, and we cannot say whose, and then we cannot say a shoe, and then we are not sure there was ever anything in the beams at all.


---

# Chapter 21 — Vazante

The knife is on the lip of the middle pio where the man from Lisbon set his booklet down, three days back, in the gold. Nuno has taken it out of his survey box and laid it on the rag he wrapped it in the day it came out of the wall, the strip torn from his own shirt. It is the ebb outside. The water is going off the flats, out of the reeds, out of the yard, and it goes out of us too along the beams, so that the two of them below come to us thin, in pieces, a voice and a hand and the shut face between them we cannot read.

Idalina crouches over the knife. She does not touch it. She has the machine going on the pio beside her, the green light, feeding the room in.

"Ninety years in a wall," Nuno says. "The steel's mostly gone."

It is a short knife. The blade is a rust-coloured tongue, eaten thin, the edge gone to a soft grey line. The handle is wood swelled black. It is one of the line-knives, the short ones for the belly, and there were hundreds of them once on the wet boards, and the sound of them was the sound of the town.

The front of us leans down to it and knows it at once.

*A knife. Only a knife. A tool.* Aurélia says it, or the front of us says it in what is left of Aurélia's saying — there is a scrape in her now, since the book was read out entire, since the greatness came apart in her mouth and did not go back; she does not lead the way she led. But she says it. *A knife off the line. Honest work. We gave them the knife and we gave them the work and that was more than they had in the interior.* The front of us says the work after her, the wet boards, the fast hands, the good clean labour, and it goes partway smooth.

The back of us looks at the knife and does not say the work.

The back of us says what the knife did.

*

We have both of it. This is the trouble now. Since the loft, since the shoe came down and would not settle to one child, since the book was read to the last line — we cannot keep the two of it apart. The front of us has the knife as a tool, ninety years honest in the wall. The back of us has the knife as the thing that opened a girl's thumb on the belly of a fish, and the red line that went up the arm after, slow, three days, and the heat that came with it, and then the loft, and then the funeral, minus. Both are in the pool. There is no one to say which the knife is. It is both. It lies on the rag being both, and we cannot look away from it, the way we could not look away from her machine the first evening.

Nuno lifts it by the rag, not the handle. He turns it to the grey window.

"Sepsis," he says. He is talking to her, low. "You cut yourself gutting, in that heat, no clean water up there — you'd be dead in a week. This isn't a heritage piece. This is evidence."

She does not answer for a moment. When the water gives her back she is saying, "—photograph it in place. The cavity, the beams, the marks. All of it. Before it goes anywhere." Her voice is the flat one, the working one. "Once it's out of the building it's just an old knife in a box. It has to be *of* the place, or he'll say I brought it in."

She has understood something, and we take it late, at the ebb, in pieces: she is not thinking of the knife as a thing that killed. She is thinking of the knife as a thing that *proves.* She wants it to stay where it can convict.

The front of us does not like her voice.

*

The word comes down to us before we have it whole. It comes off the telephone she had against her ear an hour ago, the call we caught only the near half of, the man Caldas at the far dry end of it, and then off Nuno now, saying it back to her plainly so she will hold it. *Lien.* And the other one, the one for a man on the town council who owes the developer a thing, or is owed a thing. And a third, for the debt the estate carries that we did not know the estate carried, that Melo has been quiet about — a charge on the building itself, old, unpaid, that a man with money can buy up and stand on and take the shed out from under her whether she signs or not.

Nuno lays it out for her at the pio, the way he laid out the loads and the roof. If Caldas buys the lien, he does not need her name. He does not need her *no.* He takes the building on the debt, and he cleans the chimney, and he puts the white chairs in the yard, and he prints the booklet without the ledger in it, because the ledger will be a box in a Lisbon flat where no one can be made to read it. The greatness gets hung over the door after all. The loft gets a soft word and a steam-room. The girls go back under.

"He can just — do that," she says. Not a question. Working it.

"He can start it," Nuno says. "It's slow. There's a process — the estate has to be given notice, there's a period to clear the charge or contest it. You have some time. Not much." He is quiet a moment. "And a councilman on the *câmara* who'll move it along for him. Homem I've met. He wants the jobs. He'll say you're a Lisbon woman letting a shed rot out of sentiment while the town closes."

She takes that in. The front of us has the shape of her jaw, nothing else, at the ebb.

"Forty jobs," she says. "That's the number Caldas keeps saying. Forty."

We hear the number. We do not hear it the way she means it. She means the men in the town with paper in the shop windows, the ones who will scrub the chimney and lay the pale stone. We hear *forty,* our forty, the forty in the roof we said over a hole for a hundred years, and the number sits between what she means and what we cannot help hearing, and neither of us can give it to the other.

"So we make it worthless to him," she says. "The story. If the story's already out — properly out, a name on it, a historian, the parish — then he's not buying a heritage, he's buying a scandal with a chimney. The council doesn't want its picture next to that. Nobody hangs a spa on it." She looks at Nuno. "How fast can the survey photos be somewhere that isn't your phone and isn't his box?"

She stands. She walks the length of the middle pio and back, past the boiler, past the shut door of the loft that she has been up into now with her own hands and her own light, and the front of us watches her walk and reaches for the old sure thing.

*She is beaten,* the front of us says. *She has lost the sale, and now she will lose the shed, and there is nothing left to do but leave, and she will leave, and — and she will leave the book. She will not carry the book out into the world. Why would she carry a box of old sums into her small clean life? She will set it down. She will let it go under.* The front of us says it fast, and there is a hunger in the saying we do not look at straight. *Let the man take the shed. Let him hang the name. If the name is hung then we were something, and if we were something then the girl at the back is nothing, is no one, is a row of marks, and can go back down where we put her.*

We have read her last page, the front of us says. She is done. She will leave the book.

She crouches back down at the machine and says, to Nuno, "Then we make it so he can't. We put it somewhere he can't buy."

The front of us has her wrong again. It does not mark that it has her wrong. It never marks it.

*

Here is the ugly thing, and we must say it, because it is happening in us and no one else will say it.

The front of us turns on Rosa.

She is at the back, the little weight of her, thinned near to nothing by ninety years of our not-saying and then dragged half up into the light by the living — by Fátima's mouth at the stone, by the read line, by the girl in the loft with her hand on the beam-marks. Rosa is more here than she has been since 1931, and it is killing what is left of the front of us to have her here, because a girl with a name is a girl the book is true about, and a girl the book is true about is a family that did the thing in the book, and a family that did the thing in the book was not great, and if we were not great then the front of us is nothing but the keeper of a set of sums about dead teenagers, and that is a self no one can bear to be.

So the front of us does the one thing it has ever known how to do to a girl.

It stops saying her.

*Rosa who,* the front of us says. Not asking. Undoing. *There was no Rosa. There was no. 14. There was a number in a book, and numbers are not girls, numbers are only how you keep the count, we kept the count, that was the work, and the girl the living keep saying up out of the stone is a thing the living have made, not a thing that was ours. Say the greatness. Say the good clean work. Say the knife is a tool. Say it over her. Say it until she goes back under, the way she went under before, when we said it the first time.*

It is the same thing we did in 1931. We know it is the same thing. Some of us know it. The back of us knows it and cannot stop the front, because there is no whole of us over the top anymore to stop anything. The front of us leans down over the little weight of Rosa the way it leaned down over the booklet, and it says the greatness flat and hard, not to keep the greatness now but to bury the girl under it, to drown her a second time in the sound of it — *we were great, we gave them work, we were always kind to the interior girls* — and it is trying, right here at the ebb, to un-name her again, to thin her back down to a mark, because a mark cannot cost us anything and a girl can cost us everything, can cost us the last of what we thought we were.

Rosa does not answer. She is too thin to answer. She only stays. She stays the way the shoe stayed in the beam, the way a thing stays that will not be got rid of by wanting it gone.

*

Below, the living do not hear any of it. They cannot hear us. It is the one mercy on the wrong side.

But the machine is going. And this is the thing the front of us cannot forgive: the machine takes the room while the front of us is trying to bury the girl in it, and the machine does not take the burying, because the burying makes no sound the machine can keep, and the machine does not take Rosa, because Rosa makes no sound either — but the machine takes the *room,* the drip and the tin and the tide going out under the floor, the room the girl died in, and it holds it, and it will carry it out in the girl's pocket to a Lisbon flat and set it down on soft panels and play it for a historian, and the historian will hear the room and read the book and say the girl's name into the record where it cannot be got at again.

The front of us is trying to un-name Rosa in the same hour the living are making it so she can never be un-named. We feel the two things cross in the cold shed like the two tides that will not agree at the turn, and there is no smooth to be had, none, the front pushing her down and the living pushing her name up and the machine drinking it all with its green light and keeping none of us at all.

Idalina picks the knife up.

She does it carefully, in the rag, the way Nuno held it, and she holds it a moment in the grey window-light, turning it, and the front of us leans in fast and greedy — *she sees the work in it, she sees the honest line, she will keep it, she will keep the greatness after all* — and it is wrong again, and it takes us the rest of the ebb to hear how wrong. She is not looking at the work. She is reading the rust the way Nuno read the beam, with her thumb near but not on it, and she says, low, not to Nuno, almost to the knife, "Somebody bled on you."

Then she sets it in the box, on the rag, beside the folded parish leaf and the grey coroner's sheet and the copied ledger-line she carries now everywhere she goes. "This goes to the archive with the ledger," she says. "Not to him. Not to a display case." She latches the box. "It's not a thing anyone gets to make pretty."

The front of us wanted her to keep the knife as a tool. The back of us watched her name it as a wound. Between the two of them the knife stops being either. It is in the box. We cannot get at it to say what it was.

*

They do not stay long after. The affair is a strained thing now, we take that much even in the gaps — there is a distance she keeps that she did not keep before, a hand not taken, a Porto that sits between them like a third person at the pio. The front of us tries to make it the old sure thing — *she will stay for the man, the man is near, near is held* — and cannot even finish the saying, because the back of us has heard him say *I'll finish out the week,* and has watched her not close the distance, and the two of them below are folding the net they will not use again, and the front of us has said *she will stay* so many times over so many things that the words have gone smooth and empty and hold nothing, and it lets them go.

She takes the box. She takes the machine. The green light goes off. She goes out to the car, and Nuno goes to his, and they say a thing to each other in the yard we do not get, and the two cars go, out along the road past the salt-pans, and we lose them at the last pan the way we lose everything there.

*

The front of us goes back to the burying.

It is all it has left. It leans down over the thin place where the girl is and says the greatness over her, hard, flat, *we were great, we gave them work, we were always kind* — and it reaches, while it says it, for the knife, to have the knife back the way it wants the knife, a tool, honest work, a thing off the line — and reaches — and the knife is gone in the girl's box out past the salt-pans, and the front of us cannot get it back to say what it was, and the back of us has it as the other thing, the thumb, the red line, the three days, and there is no whole of us to say which, and so the knife is not a tool and it is not the thing that killed her either, now, it is neither, it is a word we are both saying over the same rust and cannot make agree —

and the front of us says *Rosa who,* one more time, to be rid of her —

and it comes out wrong. It comes out as a name. The front of us reaches to un-say her and says her instead, *Rosa,* the way you reach to put out a small flame and cup your hand around it by mistake, and the name is out, in the mouth of the front of us that meant to drown it, and we cannot take it back, and she is still there, thinner, and named, and owed, and we did not manage it, we did not manage to lose her.

The knife is gone in the box past the last salt-pan. The rag it lay on is on the lip of the pio, the torn strip of a living man's shirt, empty now, going grey in the grey light. The water is all the way out. The mud lies bare in the yard, and the salt-cones stand white on the marinhas, and none of it holds a name, and none of it lets one go, and it does not care which we do.


---

# Chapter 22 — Águas-mortas

The neap tide does not fill the yard. It is the small water, the dead water, the tide that hardly comes and hardly goes, and so the flats lie bare all morning and the reeds stand in mud and there is no seething up through them to gather us. We are thin at the neap. We are thinner than the tide. We hold along the beams over the tanks and we do not reach far, and out on the road, where the salt-pans start, the woman is walking toward the stone with the old one beside her, and we get them the way you get a thing through fog. A shoulder. The old one's stick on the stones. A word, then not a word.

Fátima is slow this morning. Her legs are bad. She comes on the woman's arm, and they cross the last of the yard and go out to where the family stone stands among the low ones, and we go with them as far as we can go, which is not far now, and there we stop. The water does not run under the stone. It never did. We are a rumour of ourselves out here. We catch what the summed lean of us can catch, and there is less of us to lean than there was, and we do not say how much less. We have stopped saying it.

*

Idalina has a paper. We see the pale of it in her hand, a folded leaf, the one she has carried since the church, and she opens it in the flat neap light and she reads.

She reads the names.

She reads them the way you read a list you have checked twice, low, even, not for anyone to hear but for the saying of it — the mother first, *Graça,* and then the others, the ones off the beams and the ones off the stone and the ones she has matched, name to number, board to knife to spoilage to the last minus, and Fátima stands with her tin cups and her white candles and does not light them yet, only listens, and says *sim* after some of them, *sim,* yes, that one, she is here.

And then Idalina says a name we have not let ourselves have in ninety years, and the living mouth makes it whole.

*Rosa da Palha.*

*

She comes up.

There is no other way to say it and we have no room left for a better one. The living mouth says the name over the stone and the name goes down into the thin place where we put her, where we have twice put her, once for ninety years and once again three days ago when the front of us could not bear the book and tried to lay her back under the marks — and the name finds her there, and she comes up.

Not far. Not whole. She comes up the way a thing comes up off the bottom when the water is taken away from over it — not risen, uncovered. The neap has bared the flats and the name has bared her, and for the space of the woman's breath there is a shape at the back of us that has an edge to it, that has not had an edge since before any of us can hold, and the edge is Rosa, sixteen, of the straw, no. 14 of the interior, and she is looking at the living woman reading her name off a paper, and she is not grateful.

We must say this because it is the true thing and the true things are the last we have. She is not grateful. There is no warmth come up with her. Whatever we thought would rise if the name was ever said — some ease, some thanks, some end to the owing — it does not rise. She comes up owed and she comes up cold, and the front of us, what is left of the front, recoils from the edge of her the way you recoil from a knife you had forgotten was in the drawer.

*

Idalina folds the paper. She has said the names. She stands there in the small water with the old woman, and now Fátima lights the candles, one for each cup, her hands not steady, the flames small and flat in the day, and the woman watches her do it and does not help and does not stop her. The front of us reads this. The front of us always reads. *She is staying,* it says, thin as it is now. *She has read the names at the stone, she has lit no candle but she let the old one light them, she is one of us at the stone, she will not go now.* It says it because it must say something and the greatness is gone and the name in gold is gone and there is only the woman at the stone to make a smooth thing of.

It is wrong. It has been wrong every time and it is wrong now. She is not at the stone to stay. We know this and cannot hold why we know it — that she reads the names to set them down, not to take them up; that a person who records the sound of a room so she need not remember it does not come to a stone to be kept by it. She lets Fátima light the candles because Fátima needs to light them. Then she will fold the paper into her jacket and drive back to the town and do the thing she has decided, which we cannot see, out past the edge of us, in the offices and the church and the wire she will send, the thing that will put the book somewhere it cannot be buried again. She is not staying. But the front of us cannot see her whole anymore, only the shoulder and the stick and the flat flames, and out of the pieces it makes the thing it wants, the way it always has, and calls it reading.

*

Rosa speaks.

She has never spoken. In ninety years she has been a stir in the low pack, a name off a bead, a row of little marks on a table, a weight so small we lost it twice. Now the name is over her and she has an edge and she speaks, and it comes plain. It comes the plainest of anything in this house. There is no floor-voice in it, no front-of-the-room in it, none of the warmth that used to make the greatness go smooth. It is flat the way the book is flat.

She says she was kept.

She says it once. Not *we were kept* — she reaches past that, past the pool, toward the harder word, the one that will not come to any of us, the one that finishes a person to say — and it does not come, it collapses back the way it always collapses, and she does not push it, and what stays is the plain of it: *there was a keeping, and it was her that was kept, and it was us that did the keeping, and now there is this one at the stone who would keep her too, with candles, with a paper, with a name read soft, and it is the same keeping, it wears a kinder coat and it is the same.*

The front of us says *no.* The front of us, Aurélia in it still, thinner than the candle-flame, the scrape all through her now — the front says *we kept nothing, we gave them work, we were always* — and cannot finish it, cannot get the old saying out over the edge of Rosa, because the edge of Rosa is right there and the saying breaks on it. The front of us has said that saying for ninety years and it will not go over her now that she has an edge. It stops. It has to stop.

And Rosa says the way out.

She says it without softness in it, which is how we know it is true and not a comfort. She does not say it to save us. She says it because she of all of us knows the one thing, the thing she paid for and we did not: that the loft over the boiler was warm too, that they called the keeping of the girls up there a kindness — *board, so much, off the wage; a roof over you, off the wage* — and the wage never cleared, and the warm room was a room you were charged for and worked in and died in, and being needed and being wanted in it are two different columns in the same book. She says: *stop leaning.* She says it the way you would say a plain fact to someone slow. *You lean on the woman and the leaning holds her here and the holding is the last of the keeping. Stop. Let the tide have her. Let the tide have all of us. There is no greatness at the bottom of it and there is no forgiving at the bottom of it and there is only the salt-pan gone dry and the mud where the water was — and that is not the worst thing, we have made the worst thing ourselves and hung it in gold, and none of you will go down to the dry mud and none of you will let her.*

She does not say the word for the thing she is offering. There is no word for it that would not be a comfort, and she is not here to comfort. She says *stop leaning,* and *let the tide have her,* and then she is done. She has said her one thing. She had a name for one morning and she used the whole of it to say the one plain thing and now there is nothing she wants to keep it for.

*

She goes.

We would like to make it more than it is. We cannot. She has an edge for the length of the woman's saying of her name and then the edge softens, not into peace, not into light — into the small water, into the neap that does not come and does not go. The name held her up for a breath. She sets it down. She had it given to her at last and she does not keep it, because keeping is the thing she will not do, not even her own name, not even the one true thing she was owed. She lets it go the way you let go a stone you have carried so long the hand has closed around the shape of it, and the hand does not want the stone and does not want the shape either.

And she is lighter. We feel it the way the rule of this house makes us feel it — she goes lighter and we are less. The one who is going is eased and the ones who stay are robbed, and the front of us reads the easing as a dying and calls it a dying, and it is not a dying, it is a thing longer overdue than any of us, and we cannot say which it is anymore, easing or dying, because to say we would have to have a self out at the edge to say it from, and the self out at the edge is going.

She goes cold and she goes owed. She does not forgive us on the way. There is no turn of her toward the family, no hand put out, no light come over the mud. She is owed a thing we can no longer read and she takes the owing with her into the nothing, and the last of her is not a face and not a name — the name is already down on the stone, given and set down — the last of her is only a place at the back of us where an edge was, closing, the way water closes over the place a stone went in.

*

And in the same water, in the same small dead tide, another goes.

We cannot keep them apart. This is the thing we cannot do now and it is worse than any single going. One of the front goes with her. Not Aurélia — Aurélia holds, thin as she is, holds to the last of the greatness the way you hold a rail in the dark — but one of the front, one of the ones who kept the book with her, or kept the loft, or kept the funerals in the minus column and called it the sums; one of the ones who did it; a perpetrator; one of us that killed one of us.

He goes too. Or she. We had the name of them this morning and we do not have it now, and it does not matter, and that it does not matter is the whole horror of it. One who did the thing and one it was done to go out on the same small water, in the same breath, eased the same way, lighter the same way, and we reach to keep them separate — *that one was owed, that one owed; that one was kept, that one kept; that one is Rosa and that one is the one who* — and the reaching does not hold. They go out together. They pool as they go. The killer and the killed run into one water at the last and we cannot get a hand between them, we who should most of all be able to, we who did it and had it done to us in the one roof, we who are the both of them.

The front says *suicide.* The front says it small and it does not mean it, it says it out of habit, the way you say the word for a thing you are too tired to fight. There is no fight in it now. Two go, and one of them was the injury and one of them was the wound, and by the time they are out past the reeds where we cannot follow we can no longer say which was which, and we let them go because we could not hold them if we tried, and we do not try.

*

Fátima's candles gutter in the flat air. Idalina stands a while longer. We get her in pieces now — the pieces are all we get, at the neap, out past the water, with two fewer of us to lean — the flat of her back, the stillness she has when a sound comes she means to keep, though there is no machine in her hand, she left the machine, she is keeping this one in herself the way we used to keep things, in the body, without a tape.

Then she puts her hand under the old one's arm and turns her from the stone, and they go back across the bare flats toward the town, slow, the stick and the two of them, and we lose the sound of them at the salt-pans the way we lose everything there.

*

The front of us reaches, after, for the name.

Not to keep her. There is no her to keep, she is gone, she went while the candles were being lit. It reaches for the name itself, *Rosa,* the way it reached for the greatness in the gold and the way it reached for the child Firmino saved — it reaches to have the thing said again so it stays, the wet-sand comfort, the old work of this house. *Rosa. Rosa da Palha. No. 14. The one at the back we lost twice and the living woman gave back this morning at the stone.* It reaches.

And the name is a row of little marks. That is all it can get of it now. Four sounds and a paper folded into a jacket that has driven off past the last salt-pan, and a stone with a candle guttering on it, and we know it mattered, we knew a breath ago how it mattered and to whom, it mattered more than the greatness ever did because it was the one true owed thing and we had it back for the length of a woman's breath — and now there is the shape of the sounds and not the weight of them, the marks and not the girl, and the front of us says *Rosa* to get the weight back and it does not come. It is a sound. *Rosa, Rosa,* holding nothing. She gave it away herself, set it down on the stone and would not keep it, and now we cannot keep it either, and the neap goes out no further than it came in, the small water, the dead water, and does not bring it back, and we do not have it, and we are two fewer, and we do not count.


---

# Chapter 23 — Estofo

The tide is at the slack in the yard, neither coming nor going, the black mud half under and half bare, and Idalina carries the box out to the rental car in the flat grey light and sets it on the back seat and belts it in.

We watch her from the beams, fewer of us than watched her come, and we do not say how few.

*
