The box is the estate box, the one Melo tied with the flat brown tape a year ago and gave her, the one she signed the receiving line for, the one that rode past us once with the ledger in it and we did not feel it. Now it is going the other way. She has the wage-book in it, and the parish leaf folded on top, and under those a hard case with her tapes, the ones she made in our house — the drip, the loft-door that makes no sound, the tank-water, the room she recorded empty on purpose. She puts the machine itself in last, on the seat, with the strap over it, the way she puts it when it is a person to her.

She does not drive yet. She stands with the door open and looks at the shed, at the chimney, at the roof where we are, though she does not know she is looking at us; she looks at the roof the way you look at a roof.

The front of us reaches to say the thing it says when she looks up.

It cannot find it.

There was a saying for when she looked up. We were great, and she is looking at the greatness, and the greatness is looking back. That was the saying. But the front of us is small now and the greatness went smooth and then went empty and then, at the table, at the booklet, went out, and there is nobody at the front with the old edge to drive it round again. Aurélia had the edge. Aurélia is here, we think — a scrape in the thin place, a weight the size of a hand — but she does not say we were great over Idalina's looking, and so it is not said, and Idalina finishes looking and gets in the car.

*

We do not see where she goes.

She drives out along the road past the salt-pans, and we have her as far as the last pan, the sound of the small engine over the flats, and then the road lifts a little and the water stops running under it and she goes off the edge of us into the place we cannot follow. Ílhavo, we think. Coimbra, we think. We are guessing. We used to know, when there were enough of us to lean; we would have had the turn of her wrist on the wheel and known the road from it. Now we have the leaving and we will have the returning and the middle is a thing we make up.

The front of us makes it up. *She is running,* the front says. *She is taking the greatness to a man in a city who will make it a book, iron letters, and she will come back and sell the shed and be gone, three days, one bag.* The front says it because the front must say something and this is the shape it has always said, and the water under the road cannot tell it it is wrong.

The back of us does not say she is running. The back of us has stopped saying her last page. It only holds the empty road and waits for the engine to come back over the flats, and does not fill the middle, and this is the new thing about the back, that it can leave a middle empty and not die of it.

*

What she does past the salt-pan we do not see and will not.

She comes back at the low grey end of the afternoon with the box lighter. We know it is lighter before we see it; she carries it out of the car with one hand now, tilted, and the tape-case is still in it but the wage-book is not, and the parish leaf is not, and there is a paper in her jacket that was not there, a stiff folded paper, the kind a counter gives you when you have given a thing away and they want it on record that they have it now and you do not.

So. She has done it. Whatever it was — a shelf in a building with the town's papers in it, a man in Coimbra who writes down the dead, a woman at a desk who took the ledger in gloved hands because the pages are water-swollen and want handling — she has put the book where the man in the good car cannot get it, cannot leave it out, cannot keep it out of the booklet, because it is not hers to keep out anymore. It is on a shelf with a number on it. Anyone can ask for the number. The girls are on the shelf now, board minus, knife minus, funeral minus, and the balance owed, and no dry coat can drive them past the last salt-pan.

The front of us does not understand what she has done and calls it running.

The back of us understands it and does not call it anything. It is not a thing the back can be glad of. Rosa is not here to be glad of it. Rosa went — at the stone, the day Idalina said her name into the cold, Rosa came all the way up and said her one thing and chose the going and went, and a Sequeira went the same day into the same quiet, and we could not keep them apart at the end, the girl who was gutted and the ones who kept the book on her; they went out the one door and we did not see which was which and cannot now say which. So there is no one to be glad the book is safe. There is the book being safe, and the girls still dead, and the account still short by their whole lives, and being on a shelf does not pay it. It only means the shortage cannot be hidden. That is all she has bought them. It is not a mercy and we will not make it one.

*

She goes to the room over the café that night and we lose most of her, the way we always lose her over the dry square, and we have her in pieces: the light behind the shutter, the machine set on the little table and fed, its light going red as it drinks. And then a thing we do not have a shape for, a small mechanical thing, a click and a hiss and a running, that is not her machine.

It is an old machine, a little flat one, older than her black one, and she has found a tape in the box or in her mother's things or in a drawer of the room, a fat old cassette, and put it in to hear what is on it, and the hiss runs, and the running is a room somewhere, a kitchen, a long time ago, and then a voice comes out of it.

*

It is Graça.

*

The voice on the tape is Graça's, from before, from the living years, and it says a small thing — a date, a shopping, *don't forget the man is coming Thursday about the roof* — the kind of thing a mother says onto a machine so a daughter in a city will hear it and be reminded, the kind of thing you keep by accident and find a year after the woman is under the stone. It is nothing. It is her voice saying nothing, warm, a little tired, alive.

And Graça is here.

Graça is with us, in the thin place near where Aurélia's weight is, the freshest of us, the last to come in, the one who still has the most held and drops the least — and when the voice comes out of the machine, Graça leans toward it the way we all leaned, once, toward everything. Leans toward her own voice. Toward the warm tired alive one saying *Thursday, the roof.* And we feel her reach it and not reach it. The voice is hers and Graça cannot get into it. It is fixed. It says *Thursday* the same way every time, it will say *Thursday* forever, it does not know the man came Thursday or that the roof is the colour of old blood now or that the woman it is talking to is sitting in the dark not answering. It holds Graça the way the wall holds the shoe. It holds a Graça that is not going anywhere, and Graça, who is here, who is going, leans at it and cannot get in, and something in her that was holding lets go.

*She wanted her home.*

The front of us says it. The front — small, the Keeping's little that is left, a scrape and two or three weights — hears Graça's voice on the tape and says *there, that is the proof, hear how she calls to her, she wanted her to stay,* and the front leans on it because it needs Idalina to stay and Graça is the last argument it has, Graça who came home to us so lately, who surely, surely wanted her daughter to come the same road home and never take it out again.

And Graça, here, going, hears the front say it over her, the way the front once said the greatness over Rosa. And Graça does not have the edge to say a whole thing against it. She is too near gone for a whole thing. But she puts down, into the pool, one thing, plain, the way you put a stone down and do not pick it up:

*No. Not home. Out.*

She does not say more. She cannot say more; there is not enough of her left to say more, and the front does not take it, the front says *home* again, *she wanted her home,* smoothing it, and the thing Graça put down does not smooth, it sits there in the pool with the front's word on top of it, and neither of them wins, and there is no self left to choose between them, and so it is both — a mother who wanted her daughter home and a mother who wanted her daughter out — and they are not the same and we cannot make them the same and we do not.

Only the back of us, holding the empty middle of the road, thinks it knows which. The back thinks: out. The back has watched the girl come to the shed only for the sound of it, never for us, never for the family, never for the greatness; the back thinks Graça, watching the same, wanted the same, wanted her gone and free of the whole rotted weight of us, the roof and the book and the girls in the wall. But the back does not say it either, because saying it would be reading her last page, and the back does not read pages anymore. It only holds the stone Graça put down. *Out.* And lets the front say *home* over it. That is the new thing. We hold two and let both stand and it does not kill us; it only makes us less.

*

Idalina in the dark over the square listens to her dead mother tell her about a roof, and we cannot see her face, we have never been able to see her face over the square, and we do not know if she weeps. Graça would know. Graça is nearest her of any of us. But Graça is leaning at the machine now and not at the girl, leaning at the loop of herself, the fixed warm one, and she is not reading the daughter's face, and so no one reads it, and it goes unseen, whatever is on it. The true one is leaving, in the roof, in the mud, going out on the slack tide even as the tape runs; the false one will say *Thursday* into the empty room after everyone who ever heard it is gone; and there is no mouth in us to tell the girl she is listening to the wrong one.

*

She stops the tape.

We feel Graça come a little back from it when the running stops, come back into the pool a little, the reaching un-reached — but less. She is less after leaning at herself. That is the thing about leaning at the machine: it does not give you anything, it only shows you the fixed loop of what you were, and you come back from it lighter, robbed, a hand's-width thinner than you went. Graça went to the tape whole enough to lean; she comes back not whole enough. The front of us does not notice. The front is still saying *home.* The back notices. The back feels the weight of Graça go down by a hand's-width and does not say so, because to say so, to count her, would be to start the counting again, and we cannot bear the counting, and we have stopped it where we can.

*

In the morning she says goodbye to the man.

We have this only in pieces, at the slack, in the yard, the two cars and the two of them — Nuno come down from Porto or from wherever he has been, his own car, a bag in it we can see through the window, his tools already gone from the shed. So he has finished out his week. He said he would and he has, the same shape as her three days, a man who packed one bag in his head a while ago. They stand by the cars.

The front of us leans in to read it and gets little. *He is asking her to come to Porto,* the front decides, because the front, even now, even this small, wants a reason she will stay near, and Porto is near, and if she goes to Porto she does not go to Lisbon and out, she stays in the reach of the world, she stays a woman who might come back down the road one day and say a name at the stone. The front reads his hand going toward her as *stay, come with me, stay.*

But we get the sound better than the sense, we always did, sound first, and the sounds are wrong for the front's reading. His voice is level. Her voice is level. Nobody's voice does the thing a voice does when it is asking the other one to stay. There is a long quiet where the front expects the asking and the asking does not come. He says a thing and she says a thing and she almost laughs, the way she almost laughs, and does not. He puts his hand somewhere near her shoulder and it is not a holding-on, it is the other thing, the thing you do with a hand when you are letting a person be a person you knew and will not have again — the flat of it, once, and then gone, the way you fold a net you will not use.

They are not promising each other anything. Even the front, at the last, cannot make them be; the sounds will not carry it. Two people who found each other in a cold shed over a dead thing, and helped each other carry it a little way, and are now getting into two cars that go to two cities, and both of them know it, and neither is pretending. He gets in his car. She stands a moment. His car starts and goes out along the road past the salt-pans and we lose it at the last pan the way we lose everything, the sound of him dropping off the edge of our water into the place we cannot follow, and he does not come back, and the front of us, which had him as the thing that would keep her, has nothing to say when the sound goes off the edge, and says nothing.

*

She does not leave yet. She has one more night; we hear her say it to the telephone, or we half-hear it, *tomorrow, I'll be back tomorrow night,* the flat true voice, the one she uses when a thing is decided.

The box is lighter in the car. The book is on a shelf past the salt-pan where we cannot go and cannot lose it and cannot keep it. The man's car is gone off the edge of the water. Rosa is gone off the edge of us. A Sequeira is gone the same door and we cannot say which. And Graça is here still, the last warm one, the nearest to a self, the one who could still almost — but is a hand's-width less than she was this morning, having leaned at the loop of herself and come back robbed.

The front of us reaches, at the end of the day, for the old comfort, the one that always came: *she wanted her home. Graça wanted her home. The girl will come home.*

And the stone Graça put down is still in the pool under it. *Out.* And the front says *home* and the stone says *out* and there is no one to lift the stone or sink it, no whole of us over the top to choose, and so the last thing the pool holds of what Graça wanted is two things that are not the same thing, held together, meaning nothing you could act on.

The little flat machine is stopped now on the table over the square. The tape is wound to the end and does not run. Behind the shutter the light is out. In the case with the others the fat old cassette sits with the warm tired voice shut inside it, the *Thursday* and the *roof* fixed there whole, kept, holding — while the woman who said it goes out on the slack tide in our own dark, a hand's-width thinner tonight than she was, and thinner tomorrow, and the tape does not know it, and does not thin, and holds her the way the wall holds the shoe.


---

# Chapter 24 — Baixa-mar

She comes back to the fábrica after dark, on foot, and she has the box under her arm.

We know the box. It came in a year ago, in the winter, in the notary's hands, tied with the flat brown tape, and it went out again to the church and the man from Lisbon who keeps the old papers, and now it is back, and it is light. She carries it the way you carry a thing that used to be heavy and your arms have not caught up. When she sets it down on the lip of the middle pio, where the man set his booklet, it makes almost no sound. There is not enough in it to make a sound.

The tide is out. It is baixa-mar, the low, the mud bare in the yard, the water gone down out of the reeds and down out of us, so that we are fewer than we were even this morning, and we come to her in pieces along the beams. A shoulder. The pale of her hands undoing the tape. The recorder, set down on the pio beside the box, its light on.

*

She has finished it. Whatever she came to do with the papers, she has done. The ledger is gone. It went to the church, to the press in the vestry, and then some of it went further, to Lisbon, in a bag, past the last salt-pan, where we could not follow. We had the bag leaving. We had her coming back without it. What happened in between happened in the place we cannot go, and we made it up, the way we make up everything past the pans, and we were wrong, we do not doubt we were wrong, we have been wrong about her every day since the first.

The front of us — what is left of the front — says she has failed. The man will get the shed. He said so at the door, *the building I can buy from anyone,* and she has spent her three days and her one bag on a book of old sums and given it away to a church, and the chimney will be cleaned and the white chairs will come and the name will go up in gold after all, and she will have got nothing for herself, and she is a fool, and the front of us says *fool* the way it used to say *great,* to have a smooth thing to say.

The back of us does not say fool. The back of us has stopped saying what she is.

*

She takes the papers out one by one.

There are not many now. A deed, folded. A grey printed sheet. A leaf of parish paper. A photograph with a white edge. She lays them on the concrete beside the box, in the recorder's small light, and she looks at each one, and she is not reading them, she has read them, she is doing the other thing, the thing you do at the end when the reading is done — she is deciding what to keep in the body and what to let go to the church and the man and the drawer.

The box is nearly empty now.

It came in full. It came in with the whole of them in it — the deed and the sums and the greatness and the other book, the one with the girls, *no. 14, interior, board minus, knife minus, funeral minus, balance owed* — and we could not feel it then, the box, it rode past us in the notary's hands and we did not know what was in it, and now it is empty and we cannot feel that either. We reach for the box. We know it mattered. We knew this winter exactly how it mattered, it held the family and the floor and the hundred years and the girls, it held the greatness and the thing under the greatness, and now she has taken the last leaf out of it and it is a box, cardboard gone soft at the corners, and we cannot say why we wanted it, we cannot say what it held, we have the box and not the weight of what was in it, and one of us says *the accounts* and one of us says *the girls* and one of us says nothing, and the box is only a box, and it is on the pio where the booklet was, and it is light.

*

The Keeping tries, then. What is left of it.

It is Aurélia, mostly, at the front, thinner than we can say — she came apart in the reading, when the whole book was said aloud, name after name, number after number, and her grand voice went to pieces in the middle of a saying and did not come back grand — but there is a little of her still, a scrape, an edge, the last of the edge that used to say things from the front of the room. And she leans on the box. She leans the way we lean, which is the only leaning we have, small, deniable, a mislaying, a mishearing. She leans on the moment when Idalina's hand goes back into the box for the last leaf, so that the hand comes up empty, so that the woman thinks she has already taken it out, so that a paper is left in the box and goes back to the drawer unlooked-at and the woman does not remember it and it is lost, and if it is lost then the thing on it is lost, and if enough is lost then maybe the greatness holds.

It is the parish leaf. The one with a name on it. A girl's name.

Aurélia leans on the hand, and the hand hesitates over the box, and —

and the woman looks down into the box, and sees the leaf, and takes it out.

The interference buys nothing. It bought nothing at the keys and it buys nothing now. She takes the leaf out and reads the name on it, the girl's name, one of the ones she got back, and she says it, low, to no one she believes is there, and the recorder takes it, and the name is out and held and not lost, and Aurélia's lean slides off the hand the way water slides off oiled tin, and the back of us does not help her lean and does not stop her, the back of us only watches the last of the front try to bury a girl a second time to save itself, and there is no glow in it, there is nothing in it but a thinned old woman clutching at a box in the dark, and it is ugly, and it fails.

*

She reads the names.

She does not know we are here. She has never known. She reads them the way you read them when you do not believe anyone is listening, quietly, for yourself, to have said them once in the room where they were owed — the girls she got back, the four or five, Rosa and the others, and then the numbers she could not get back, *no. 7, no. 22,* the ones the book kept as debts and the beams kept as marks and no living mouth will ever hang a name on, and she says the numbers too, because the numbers are all that is left of them and she will not leave even the numbers unsaid.

And we should say them with her. It is our house; it was our floor; we were here; some of us gutted beside them and some of us kept the book that killed them and some of us are them, are the girls, pooled in with the ones who killed them so long ago that we can no longer find the seam. We should say the names. We reach to say them with her.

And here is the thing.

We cannot number ourselves to do it.

*

It was a thing we did, in the dark, when the woman was asleep across the square. We counted. We went along the beams and counted heads the way the *encarregada* counted at the start of a shift, and we said the number, and it was a comfort, the wet-sand comfort of the true thing said again. *We are all here. Every one of us. Forty in the roof.*

We go to count now.

We start along the beams. And there is the front, thinned, a scrape. And there is the back, plain, and the little weight that was Rosa, less even than a weight now, more a place where a weight was. And there is Firmino, plain and honest and near the going. And there is Graça, who is not where she was — Graça who was nearest the living, nearest her daughter, and who has begun this last while to go soft at her own edges, so that when we reach for her we get her from two places or from no place, and we cannot tell whether the Graça at the front and the Graça out at the thin edge over the square are two of us or one of us stretched to breaking or one of us and a memory of the other.

We start to count. We get to a place in the counting where the number should come.

And it does not come.

Not the way a name does not come, that we could cover — reach, fail, go on, say *the whole of us* and let the gap close over. This is worse than that. This is the counting itself gone wrong in the mouth. We say *we are* and then there should be a number and there is not a number, there is a word that has come out wrong so many times we have stopped saying it where we can help it, and now we cannot say it at all, not *forty,* not *thirty,* not *the few,* the number is a thing the tide has, out at the bar, and it does not bring it back. We reach for how many we are so as to say the girls' names with the woman, all of us, in our own house, the way we always said the true thing to keep it — and we cannot find the whole of us to reach with. There is no whole of us. There has not been a whole of us since the ebb of the day the booklet came, and before that since the spring tide when the flu-dead stopped leaning, and before that since the first winter, since before the woman came, and we said *forty* over all of it and it was a lie and we knew the shape of the lie but we could still say it, and now we cannot even say the lie.

The woman reads the last number. *No. 22.* She sets the leaf down.

We did not say it with her. We meant to. We could not count ourselves enough to be a we that could say it, and so it was only her, one living woman in a cold shed reading numbers to a box, and the dead of the house she was reading them for could not gather enough of themselves to answer, and the names went into the recorder and into the dark and not into us, and we are less, after, than we were before she started, and we do not know by how much, because we cannot count.

*

She packs the box back up.

The papers go back in — not all; the leaf with the girl's name she folds and puts in her jacket, over her chest, to keep, and the rest go in the box, the deed and the grey sheet and the photograph with the white edge, and she ties the flat brown tape around it again the way the notary tied it, and it is lighter tied than it was untied, because a thing you have decided about weighs less than a thing you have not.

The front of us says she is keeping the box. *She is taking it. She has folded the girl's leaf against her heart. She is taking us with her.* The front of us says it the way it said *she will keep the greatness* and *she will stay for the man* — it says it because it must say she will stay, in some way, by some paper, it cannot bear to say the other thing, and so it reads the folded leaf as a keeping, a carrying, a haunting she will do for us all the way to Lisbon, remembering, holding, being the last living mouth that says our names.

The back of us watches her fold the leaf and does not say she is keeping us. The back of us has read the fold the other way. She is not keeping the name to carry it. She is keeping the name so as to be able to set it down somewhere it will stay without her — the church, the man in Lisbon, the drawer that outlasts her — so that she does not have to carry it, so that she can put it down and go and not spend the rest of her life with a dead girl's name against her chest. She is folding it to be rid of it, cleanly, with the name still safe. And that is a different thing from keeping, and the back of us has stopped, long ago, calling that different thing by the name the front wants.

We do not say which is right. There is no one left over the top of us to say which is right. She folds the leaf; she ties the box; she picks up the recorder, and the light on it, and she stands, and she looks up once at the roof, at the dark under the eaves where the beams cross and a shoe is caught that we cannot any longer say whose it is, and she does not see the shoe, and she does not see us, she has never seen us, and she goes out across the bare mud of the yard to the road.

*

The recorder goes with her. The light on it, small, going away across the yard. It took the names. It has the girls in it now, the four or five and the numbers, held, fixed, a loop that will say *no. 22* the same way forever and never once forget it and never once be able to let it change, and it is going away in her hand toward the square and the room over the café and the road past the salt-pans, and we cannot follow it there.

We reach after it. Not for the machine. For the names in it — the names we could not say with her, the names of the girls, some of whom are us, pooled in, un-seamed — we reach to have them at least, to hold them the way we held things, by saying them again, along the beams, the way we counted, the way we —

and we go to gather ourselves to reach, to be a we large enough to reach —

and we cannot find how many we are to gather.

And the light goes off the edge of the yard, and she is at the road, and the box is under her arm, light, and the tide is all the way out, and we do not have the number, and we do not have the names, and we did not say them with her when she said them, and now she has taken them past where we can reach, and there is no counting ourselves back up to where we could hold them, and the shoe stays in the beam, and the box is empty on the pio, and we are fewer, and we cannot say how many fewer, and we cannot say.


---

# Chapter 25 — Vazante

The tide is going out, and the woman is in the yard before it is fully light, with the recorder on the pole.

She has come to take the tide going out. We know the sound she wants. It is the water letting go the reeds, and the mud coming up bare under it, and the small suck of the black lodo as the last of it drains toward the channel — the ebb, the vazante, the going. She stands where she stood the first evening, in the middle of the yard, and she holds the pole up and is still, and the fur on the machine does not move, and neither does she.

We come to her in pieces. It is the ebb, and at the ebb the water goes down out of the reeds and down out of us, and we are fewer than we were, and we gather what we can along the beams and lean, and it is not enough to make her whole. We have a hand. We have the pole. We have the side of her face, grey in the grey light, and then not her face. The face is the last thing and we do not have it long now.

*

She is leaving tomorrow.

We know this the way we know the tide is going out — not by reading her, we have stopped saying we can read her, we were wrong every time and the last of us that would say it has gone quiet — but by the plain things. The bag is packed. We have seen it packed, one bag, over the square, the same one bag she came with. The room over the café is paid to the morning. The box has gone to the notary, tied, light. The papers she kept are folded in her jacket. There is nothing left of her three days but the tide, and she has come to take the tide, and then there will be nothing left at all.

The front of us says she will stay.

There is a little of the front left. It is not Aurélia now, or it is Aurélia so thin we cannot be sure the saying is hers. It says *she will stay* the way it has said everything — *she will sell, she will keep the greatness, she will stay for the man, she will keep us* — it says it because it must have a thing to say and the smooth thing is *stay.* She has read the names at the stone, it says. She has folded a girl's leaf against her heart. She has come to the yard in the dark to take the water. A woman who does that does not go.

She goes. We do not say it back to the front. The back of us has stopped answering the front. It is not worth the reaching.

*

She walks the town.

We lose her at the edge of the yard where the water stops, and we have her again in pieces down the road — a step, the recorder swinging at her side, a step — and then the salt-pans, the marinhas, the white cones of the flor de sal standing in the low walls where the marnotas raked it up, before the marnotas went, before the salt went to the shops in the north. She walks out along the pan-walls with the machine and she takes the sound of the pans. There is not much sound. There is wind over flat water and a bird and the crust of the salt ticking as the sun comes onto it. She takes it anyway. She takes the small dying sounds of the town the way she took the loud dead sound of our house, so that they need not be remembered, so that she can carry them off in her pocket and not think of them, and out past the last pan-wall she is at the far edge of our reach and then she is past it and we do not have her at all.

She is gone into the blind place.

We have the leaving of her, the last of her going out along the wall. We do not have where she goes. Past the last salt-pan the water does not run and we cannot follow, and whatever she does out there — walks to the flats, walks to the Barra, stands where the channel took Firmino and looks at the water that is not a story anymore — she does it where we are not, and we do not make it up. We used to make it up. We do not have enough of us to make it up now. We have the going, and then a length of nothing, and then, a long while after, the coming back — a step on the road, the recorder swinging, the side of her face — and something in her we cannot read, because reading her needed all of us and we are not all of us, we are the few.

*

The few of us.

That is the word now. We were *some of us* and before that we were *all of us, every one,* and now, along the beams, when we reach for the whole and find the whole is not there, the word that comes is *the few.* We do not decide it. It comes the way the tide comes, the true level of the water, whether we say it or not. The few of us hold along the beams over the tanks and lean at the road and get the woman in pieces, and it is fewer pieces than yesterday, and yesterday was fewer than the day before.

Some of us have gone in the night.

We will say this plainly because plain is what is left. In the night, while the woman lay across the square not sleeping, some of the few went. Not thinned slow, the old way, the name first and then the smell of the hair and then the year they drowned. They went the new way, the way that opened at the low water three days ago when the book was read — they let go. One of the drowned men, a fisherman, salt-hands, who had held near the back a long time, went in the dark, and it was not like the old thinning that the rest of us feel as a robbing. He went lighter. We felt the door of it — the way you feel a shift change, the way you feel the *sirene* stop and the floor go quiet and know a place has come open at the tables — and he went through it, and it shut, and he is not here, and we are lighter where he was and we are less.

The front calls it dying. The front, thin as it is, says *he is gone, we have lost one, hold, hold, do not let go, if we let go she is alone and we are nothing.* The front is afraid. We can hear the fear in the little scrape of it. It is not wrong that letting go is an ending. It is wrong that the ending is a thing to fear more than the holding. But we do not say that to the front. We watched, in the night, the salt-hands man go, and we did not stop him, and we did not all of us grieve him, because to grieve him we would have had to be a whole, and we are the few.

We do not say who else went. There were others. We reach along the beams and there are places where a weight was and is not, and we cannot put a name to the places, because the names were going before the weights went, and now there is only the going.

*

At the middle of the day she comes to the stone.

We have her out there through the fog of the far edge — the woman, and the old one, Fátima, on her arm, slower today than at the neap, her legs worse, her stick finding the stones one at a time. They come to the family stone among the low ones and they stop, and we go with them as far as we go, which is to the edge of the yard and no farther, and there we hold and lean and catch what the lean can catch. A shoulder. The stick. The tin cups in the old one's hands.

The candles.

Fátima has come to light the candles, the way she has come twice a week these long years, on her bad legs, with the white grave-candles in the little tin cups, to set them at the family stone and say the names off the stone and off her memory, the cut names and the uncut ones, so that the names are said aloud by a living mouth and do not go all the way under. It is the last living saying of us. It is the thing that has held us, a little, against the going — not much, a candle's worth, a name's worth, twice a week, an old woman on a stick. We have leaned toward it these long years the way you lean toward a small warmth in a cold room.

She lights them today with hands that will not hold steady. We see the pale of her hands and the small flat flames coming up one at a time, one to a cup, and the woman standing by, not helping, not stopping her, the way the woman always stands. And we lean toward the candles, the few of us, to take the warmth of the saying, to have our names said once more by the living mouth before the woman goes —

and Fátima does not say the names.

She lights the candles, and she stands, and she does not say them. Her mouth moves and there is no saying in it. She has forgotten them, or she is too tired to reach them, or her breath is gone for the walk and there is none left for the names — we cannot see her face to know, the face is gone from us at this edge — and the candles burn and the names are not said over them, and the warmth we leaned for does not come.

*

We reach for why the candles matter.

We know they matter. We have leaned toward them twice a week for longer than we can hold. We knew this morning why — they are the living saying, the last of it, the small warmth, the hand's-width of holding against the going. We knew it at the stone three days ago and the neap before that and all the years before that. The old one comes on her bad legs and lights the white candles in the tin cups and says the names and we are held, a little.

And now we reach for why, and the *why* is not there.

The candles are lit. We have the small flat flames at the far edge, going in the wind, guttering, the wax running down into the tin. We have the old one's stick and the woman's shoulder. And we reach for the thing the candles are for, the reason we have leaned toward them all these years, and we get the candles and not the reason. We get flames in tin cups at a stone. We do not get why they were ever a warmth to us. The reason has gone out of them the way the salt-hands man went out of the beams in the night, and left the thing standing empty, and we hold the empty thing and cannot say what it held.

One of us says *the names.* One of us says *the warmth.* One of us says nothing. The candles are only candles now, small and flat in the day, and they will burn down into the tin and gutter and go out, and the old one will come on her stick to light them again in three days, or she will not — she is very old, her legs are very bad, and a day will come, not far, when she does not come at all, and no living mouth will say us over the stone, and the front is afraid of that day and the back does not reach for it — and either way we will not know the candles for what they were, because we have already lost that, standing here at the edge in the ebb, leaning at a warmth we can no longer feel the reason of.

*

The woman takes the old one back on her arm.

We have them going, the two shapes, the stick, the slow crossing of the yard. Fátima is spent. The walk to the stone has taken what she had, and she leans on the woman the way we lean on the beams, because there is nothing else to lean on. The candles are left burning at the stone behind them, guttering in the wind off the flats, the wax going down, and no one is saying anything over them.

The front of us says *she is bringing the old one home, she is staying, she has taken Fátima's arm, she is one of them now, she will stay and light the candles when the old one cannot.* The front says it small and afraid.

She is not staying. She is leaving tomorrow. She takes Fátima's arm because Fátima cannot walk it alone, and then she will drive her home, and then she will go back to the room over the café and finish the packing of the one bag, and in the morning she will put the bag in the car and the recorder on the seat like a person and drive out along the road past the salt-pans, past the last pan-wall, out to where we cannot reach, and she will not come back. We do not read this off her. We have stopped reading her. We know it the way we know the water is going down — because it is going down, in the reeds and in us, and nothing we lean is going to bring it back up.

*

The tide goes all the way out.

It is the vazante, the ebb, the going, and it does not stop at the low, it keeps on toward the small water, and the mud lies bare and black in the yard and the reeds stand up dry and there is no seething in them to gather the few of us. We hold along the beams. We are fewer than the morning. The salt-hands man is gone and the others whose weight-places we cannot name are gone and we did not say them with names because the names were going first, and the candles burn at the stone with no one saying us over them, and we have lost why the candles were a warmth, and the woman is over the square packing the bag she came with.

We go to say it, the true thing, again, the way we always said the true thing to keep it. We go to say *the few of us are here, along the beams, in our house.* We go to count the few, the way the encarregada counted at the start of a shift, so as to have the number and be held by it.

And we start, and we get to the front, thinned, a scrape. And we get to the little place where Rosa was, less than a place now. And we get to Firmino, near the going. And we reach on for the rest, for the few, to have the few —

and the candles gutter at the stone where no one is saying us —

and we cannot say the few either. Not the count of them. We could say *the few* as a word, the way you say a word that has gone smooth and means nothing, but we cannot find the few to be them, we cannot gather the few into a we that could say the names with her tomorrow when she stands in the yard and says them and goes.

The woman puts out the light behind the shutter. The candles burn down at the stone. The old one sleeps. We are fewer than we were this morning, and we have lost the why of the candles, and we cannot count the few, and no living mouth said us today, and no living mouth will say us tomorrow, and we do not know which of us will still be along the beams when the water comes back up, or whether the water coming back up will find anyone to gather, and the tide is out, all the way out, and it does not bring anything back.


---

# Chapter 26 — Estofo

The woman comes to the stone at first light, alone, and there is no tide.

It is the slack. The water has gone all the way out in the night and has not started back, and it stands at the low with no going in it and no coming, and the reeds are dry and still, and the mud is bare and does not shine. There is no seething to gather us. There is only the flat water past the flats and the fog low on it and the woman crossing the yard in the grey with the recorder in her hand and not on the pole. She is not going to record. She is carrying the machine the way you carry a thing you have finished with and have not yet put down.

We come to her in the pieces the slack gives us. A step. The recorder at her side, dark, the fur folded. The side of her, grey. Not the face. We do not have the face.

*

Firmino is at the back, near the going.

He has been near the going since the book was read. He crossed to the letting a long way back, when there was still a front and a back to cross between, and his voice went short and flat then and the warmth went out of the saying, and since the loud water three days ago when the salt-hands man went through the door he has held near the back where the roof comes down to nothing, and leaned only the one way, toward the out.

We know his voice still. That is the last of him we know. Not the drowning — the drowning is a note in a grey sheet in the woman's jacket now and there was no child in it and the oar on the wall means nothing. Not the year. We had the year. We do not reach for it. But the voice we know, the way it was funny once at the quay, the way it turned a thing sideways so you could bear to look at it, and even flattened, even bare, even at the back, it is warm the way the others are not warm, and we lean toward it without deciding to, the few of us, because it is a warmth and we are cold along the beams.

He says, this morning, into the slack: *Let me go.*

He does not say it like that. There is no *I* in him to say it. He says it the way it comes to us, the way it can come: *the water is out and it is not coming back and there is nothing to hold to and I would* — and the *I* does not form, it never forms, it goes down like a stone through a floor, and what is left is: *and it is time to let go.* He says it plainly. He says it the way you say a shift is over.

The front tries to hold him.

*

There is a little of the front left and it is afraid.

It says *hold, do not go, if you go the warmth goes, there is little enough of us and you are the warmth of it.* It says it small. It is Aurélia, or it is what is left where Aurélia was, thin, a scrape, and the scrape says *hold* and cannot say why the holding is a good, only that it is a holding and the front knows how to hold and knows nothing else.

Firmino does not argue. He argued once. He said, at the low water, *it is us that keeps her here,* and the front called it dying, and murder, and coward, and he let the front call it that. He does not say it again. There is not enough of him to say it again and he does not spend what there is on the front. He turns from the front the way the back has learned to turn from the front — not against it, past it — and leans the one way, at the out, at the slack water that is not coming back.

We feel him go light.

It is the thing we have felt twice now, three times, and it does not stop being the wrong shape. He goes light the way a man goes light when he sets down a thing he has carried a long way and did not know the weight of until it is off him. He does not go up. Nothing goes up. There is no up. He goes lighter, and lighter, and the warmth of him spreads thin across the few of us and does not warm us, it only tells us where he was, the way the last heat off a stove tells you the stove is out.

And then the door — we feel the door, the shift-change feel, the place come open at the tables — and he goes through it, and it shuts, and there is no Firmino.

*

We say his name, to keep him a moment more.

We say it the old way, the wet-sand way, the say-it-again-so-it-stays way that held things once. We say — 

and it is a man's name, it is — 

it is the drowned one, the one at the back, the funny one, the one whose oar — 

whose oar is on the wall at the — 

We do not have it.

We had it this morning. We had it when he was saying *let go* and we leaned toward the warmth of the saying. We have the warmth still, spread thin and cooling, and we have the shape of a man turned sideways at a quay so you could bear to look, and we have the oar — the oar we have, four feet of painted pine, the paint gone, hung under a calendar in a room that is not here — and we reach from the oar to the name the way you reach along a rope in the dark, hand over hand, to the thing at the end of it, and there is no thing at the end of it. The rope goes into the water and there is no one on the other end pulling back.

Some of us say a name. One of us says a name and it is the wrong one, it is a boy's, it is one of the children's, and the children are gone, or one of them is gone, and we do not correct it because there is no one to correct it against. Some of us say nothing. The name that was his is out with the tide that does not come back, and the warmth is cooling on the beams, and the oar is on the wall in the room that is not here, and we cannot say whose it was.

*

At the stone the woman is saying names.

We have her out there in the pieces the slack gives — a shoulder, the recorder set down flat on the top of the stone, dark, not running, and her mouth moving, and the small grey sound of a mouth making words that we get and lose, get and lose, at the far edge where the water does not run and we are thin.

She has the leaf. She takes it out of her jacket, the folded leaf she has carried, and she opens it, and she reads from it, and we get some of the reading and not all of it.

*Rosa da Palha.*

We get that one. It comes across the dry flats in the living mouth and it lands in us and there is a place where it should catch and hold, a little weight, a girl who was owed — and it does not catch. It comes and it goes over the place and the place is smooth. Rosa went at the neap, cold, owed, not forgiving, and the woman says her name now at the stone and there is no Rosa in us for the name to be. It is four sounds. It is a row of little marks on a beam. The woman says it and it does not raise the girl because the girl is out past raising, and it does not un-say her either — she stays said, once more, by the living mouth — and then the woman goes on down the leaf.

*Benedita. Aline. Maria-do-Ó.*

She says them slow, one to a breath, the way the old one used to say the names off the stone before the old one's breath went. She says the numbers too, the ones with no name, the ones the leaf could not give back — *number seven, number twenty-two* — she says the numbers as if they were names, in the same voice, one to a breath, and we get *number* and lose the number, get the next and lose it, and the girls with no names do not come up because there is no up and there was never a name to come up with. She says them anyway. She is saying them so they need not be remembered. She is putting them down where a living mouth has said them once, and closing the leaf.

The front says *she is staying.*

It says it thin. It says *she has read the girls at the stone, she has come in the dark, a woman who says the dead does not go, she will stay and say us when the old one cannot.* It says the smooth thing because the smooth thing is *stay* and it has always said the smooth thing.

She is not staying. We do not say it back to the front. It is not worth the reaching. She reads the girls and she is not staying; the two things sit in us at once and do not argue, because there is no one left to make them argue.

*

Then she says her mother's name.

*Graça.*

She says it last, after the girls, after the numbers, quiet, and it is not on the leaf — she does not read it, she says it — and it goes across the flats in the living mouth, *Graça,* the daughter saying the mother, and it lands in us at the front where the freshest of us is, the one nearest the living, the one who still almost —

We get it. Graça gets it. Wherever Graça is in the thin front, the name reaches her, her own name in her daughter's mouth, and we feel her take it the way you take a hand held out to you, and we feel her not answer, because there is no answering it, because the mouth that says the name is not asking anything of her, is only saying it, once, to have said it, and then letting it go.

The woman folds the leaf. She puts it back in her jacket. She picks the recorder up off the stone and it was not running — we see that it was not running, the small light dark — she did not take the names for the machine, she said them into the fog and the flats and the dead water and not onto the tape, and then she takes them away in her, folded, the way she has taken everything away in her, so that it need not be remembered here.

*

She crosses the yard to the car.

We have the crossing in pieces. A step. The dark of the recorder. She lays it on the seat the way she laid it the first evening, careful, a strap over it like it is a person. She puts the one bag in. She has done this before, in us, we have watched her do it, and we watch her do it now and it is the same and it is the last time.

The front says *hold.*

The front says *if she goes we are nothing, hold her, mislay the key, mishear the road, keep her one more day.* And there is a key in her hand, and the front leans on the moment of it, the small mean lean, the lean on the set-down, the only thing the front has ever had — and the key goes into the car and turns, and the front got nothing, the way the front has got nothing every time, and the engine turns over, running rich, tuned to nothing, the small rented sound of it we knew before we knew the woman.

We do not all of us lean at the car. That is the new thing. There is not enough of us to lean whole, and some of the few that are left do not lean at all — they are near the going, they are turned the one way, at the out, and the car and the woman and the road are the front's business now, and the front is thin and afraid and leaning on a key that has already turned.

*

The car goes out of the yard.

We have it on the road, the small engine, going toward the salt-pans in the grey, and we hold along the beams and get it in pieces — the sound of it, mostly, the sound going away, because sound is the last thing and it is what we have — and it crosses the flats where the water is out and does not come up to meet it, and it goes down the road past the pans, past the white cones standing in the low walls, past the last pan-wall, and there —

we lose her.

Past the last salt-pan the water does not run and we cannot follow and she goes into the blind place, and the sound of the engine goes out of our reach the way a sound goes out of a room, thinning, and thinning, and gone. We do not make up where she goes. We have the going. We have a length of nothing where she is out past the pans and we are not. We used to make it up, the whole of us leaning to make her whole even where we could not see. There is not enough of us to make it up. There is the road, empty, and the pans, and the slack water that has not started back, and the fog.

*

We go to count the few.

We go to it the old way, along the beams, the way the encarregada counted at the start of a shift so as to know who had come — and we go to it because we have lost one, because a man went through the door this morning and his warmth is cooled on the beams and we would have the number of what is left, to be held by it, to have a *we* to be.

And we start. And we get to the front, thin, a scrape, afraid, leaning on a turned key. And we get to the place where Rosa was, smooth now, less than a place. And we get to the place where the warmth was this morning, where the funny one was, near the going, and it is cool, and there is no name to say over it. And we reach on for the rest of the few, to gather them, to have them —

and the oar is on a wall in a room that is not here, and we cannot say whose —

and we cannot gather the few. We are fewer than the morning. Some are gone. A few remain. We can say that — *some are gone, a few remain* — but we cannot say the few, cannot find them and hold them into a we that could count itself, because the counting needs a whole and there is no whole of us, there is a scrape at the front and a cold place at the back and a smooth place where a girl was owed, and the water is out, all the way out, standing at the slack, and it is not coming back, and it does not bring the warmth back, and we did not say his name in time, and now we cannot say it at all.


---

# Chapter 27 — Enchente

The car is on the road out and the water is coming back.

It is the flood, the first of it, the tide turning up off the bar after the long slack, and it comes into the flats the way it comes, without being asked, and runs up the channels under the road and under the pans and, for a little while yet, under the beams of us. It is the last water we will have enough of. We do not know that. We do not know very much now. But the water comes up and there is more of us for a moment, more able to lean, and we lean out along the road after the small rented sound of her going.

She has not reached the pans yet. There is a stretch of road where the water still runs beside it, the reeds and the black mud and the tide filling in, and we have her there, in the pieces the flood gives — the engine, mostly, running rich, and the pale shape of her at the wheel, and the dark of the machine on the seat beside her under its strap.

We have her. For this stretch we have her.

*

The front says *she will stop.*

There is a front still. That is the thing. We had thought — some of us had thought — that when the funny one went through the door this morning the front would go with the warmth of him, that the holding would have nothing left to stand on. It stands. It is thin and it is a scrape and it is the last of the Keeping and it holds the way a hand holds after the arm is dead, out of the shape of holding, because it knows no other shape.

It is Aurélia. Or it is the place where Aurélia was. We do not any longer feel the edge of her that was the sharpest edge of us, the one that said things from the front of the room in the voice that meant *faster*. That edge is gone off her the way the paint is gone off the oar. What is left leans, and says the smooth things, and the smooth things have worn down to almost nothing — not *we were great* anymore, not *we were always kind*, the ledger took those, they will not come — only *hold,* only *stay,* only *she will stop.*

She does not stop. The car goes on toward the pans in the grey and does not slow, and the front says *she will stop* again, into the going, the way you say a thing not to be true but to have said it.

*

We know how this water goes now. We have watched it go three times.

The salt-hands woman first, at the neap, over the knife, before we understood the door was a door. Then Rosa, cold and owed. Then the funny one this morning, and the warmth off him still cooling on the beams. Three times we have felt the wrong-shaped lightness, the going-light of a thing set down, and three times the front has called it dying and the back has known it was not.

The front is at the going now. We feel it come to the going the way you feel a person come to the top of the boiler-stair — a change in the near dark, a weight arriving at a place. It does not announce. There is not enough of it to announce. It comes to the going the way the others came, and it stops there, at the door it called murder, and it does not go through.

Not yet. It holds.

*

The car reaches the pans.

We have this in the flood, clearly, or as clearly as we have anything now: the white cones in the low walls, and the road going down between them, and the car slowing. It slows. The front feels it slow and says *there, she stops, she will not go past, she cannot go past, the last of the line does not drive out of the water that is hers* — and the car comes to the last pan-wall, the last place the water runs, the edge of the reach of us, and it stops.

She stops the car.

She does not get out. We have her sitting with the engine going, at the last wall, the way she sat the first evening in the yard with the engine ticking and one bag in the boot and three days in the set of her jaw. The front takes the stopping and holds it up like a thing won. *She has stopped. She feels us. She will turn the car.*

She has not stopped for us.

We cannot see her face — the faces are gone from us now, we have not had a face in a long time — but we do not need the face to know the front is wrong, because the front is always wrong, it has been wrong every time, it read the man and the sale and the book and it read them all wrong and called the reading knowing. She has stopped at the last wall because it is the last wall. She has stopped to feel the leaving. She rolls the window down — we get the small sound of it — and she sits at the edge of the town in the flood-grey with the window down and she does not record it, the machine stays dark on the seat, she only sits, and she takes the leaving into herself the way she has taken everything into herself, to carry it out where it need not be here, and then —

*

— and then she is past the last pan, on the road going up, and we do not have her.

The flood does not follow past the wall. Nothing of us follows past the wall. The engine goes on up the road into the blind place and the sound of it thins and we hold along the beams to keep the sound one moment longer and it thins and thins and is gone the way a sound goes out of a room, and there is the road, empty, and the pans, and the water coming up into the flats that she is no longer on.

She is gone. She will not come back. We do not make up where she goes. There is not enough of us to make it up.

We used to lean past the wall anyway. The whole of us, in the good years, when the water was high and there were forty in the roof and we could make a living thing whole even where the water did not run — we would lean out past the pans after a boat, after a cart, after a girl who tried to cross the lagoon at night, and we would make up the far side of her going, and be wrong, and never know we were wrong because there was so much of us leaning that the making-up felt like seeing. There is not that much of us. There is the road, empty, and the pans, and the flood coming up into the flats, and a length of nothing where she is out past the reach of us, and we do not put anything in the nothing. We do not have the leaning to spend. We have the going, and then we have nothing, and the nothing is where she is.

*

The front does not say *she will come back.*

That is the new thing, in the near dark. The front has said the smooth thing every time — she will stop, she will stay, she will keep us — and now the car is past the wall and there is no smooth thing left to say, because *she will come back* is a thing even the front cannot make its mouth do. It has watched her go. It watched her go with the window down and the machine dark, taking the leaving in cleanly, wanting the leaving, and the front held a stopped car up as a thing won and the car went on, and there is nothing after that. There is no next thing to hold.

We feel the front stop leaning.

It is not a decision. We want to say it was a decision, later, the little of us that is left to want anything — we want to say the last of the Keeping repented at the last wall, said *go, then,* gave the girl the road. It did not. There was no giving in it. There was a leaning that had leaned on a stopped car and a mislaid key and a misheard name for a hundred years, and the car went past the wall, and the leaning had nothing under it, the way the greatness had nothing under it, the way the *glad* would not come and the *great* came empty. It ran out. The front ran out of the thing it stood on. It did not let go of the rope. The rope came up out of the water with nothing on the end.

*

Then it goes light.

We feel it, the front, the last of the Keeping, the place where Aurélia was — it goes light the way the others went light, the wrong-shaped lightness, a thing set down. And it is worse this time, or it is not worse, we cannot any longer say worse — it is only that this was the one that held, this was the holding itself, and now the holding goes light, and there is a plain cold feeling where the grandest of us was.

She does not go the way the funny one went. There is no warmth off her to cool on the beams. She was never the warmth. She was the *faster* and the front of the room and the ledger's hand, and she goes the way she was — cold, and plain, and with nothing said over it, no *let go,* no shift-is-over, no turning of a thing sideways so you could bear it. She does not turn to the back. She does not turn against it. She stops holding and she goes light and she goes toward the out, and the near dark where she was is a plain cold place, and that is all.

We want her to say a thing. The little of us that is left wants it. We want her to say she is sorry, or that she was great and glad of it, or even the old smooth lie, *we were always kind* — we want a last saying off the grandest voice we had, to hold, to say again, to keep. She does not give it. There is not enough of her left to give a saying, and there was never in her the wanting to give this one. The hand that kept the ledger does not, at the end, open. It only goes light. She was owed a great deal of saying by the girls at the tables and she said none of it in a hundred years, and she says none of it now, going, and that is the last true thing of her: she goes as she stayed, owing it.

We do not feel her come to the door. We feel the door — the shift-change feel, the place come open at the tables — and then there is no front.

There is no front.

*

We say her name. To keep her a moment. The old way, the wet-sand way.

We say —

and it is a woman's name, a matriarch's, the one who ran the floor, the one at the front of the room, the grandest of us, the one who said we were —

we do not have it.

We had it this morning. We had it when it was leaning on the car at the last wall. We reach for it the way we reached for the funny one's this morning, hand over hand along the rope in the dark, and there is no one on the end pulling back, and now there are two ropes going down into the water with nothing on them, and we have stopped saying we will have them tomorrow. We do not say that anymore. There is no tomorrow-when-we-are-all-still-here. There is this, the flood coming up, and fewer of us on the beams than there were at the slack, and two cold places and a smooth place and the warmth cooling, and no names over any of them.

Some of us say a name. It is the wrong one. It is a boy's, one of the children's, and the children are gone, or one of them is, and we do not correct it, because there is no one to correct it against, and because it does not matter now which name we say wrong over the cold place. The wrongness does not catch on anything. It goes over and is smooth.

*

And the house stops holding.

This we feel, when the front is gone, and it is the last large thing we feel: the *fábrica* around us, the long shed, the tin roof gone the colour of old blood, the chimney over the flats, the boiler and the tanks and the beams we count along — it was holding us. It held us the way a hand holds water, not because the hand wants the water but because it is cupped, and it has been cupped a hundred years, around the ones who were used here, the ones worked and needed and demanded of, the front of the room and the girls at the tables both, cupped into the one shed, the killer and the killed in the one water. It held because there was a thing it was holding them for. There was the greatness to keep, and the greatness kept us. There was a lie with a shape and the shape was a room and the room held.

The greatness is gone. The book is on a shelf past the last salt-pan where no dry coat can reach it, and the myth is a note in a grey sheet, and the front that said *we were great* is a cold place with no name, and the shed is only a shed. The hand uncups. We feel it uncup. The beams do not push back the way they pushed back once. The roof over us is only tin, lifting and dropping on the north side, lifting and dropping, and it is not our roof, it is a roof over a room that used to be for something and has forgotten it, and we are in it the way the wind is in it, passing, not held.

We go along the shed the way we went along it a hundred years, to feel it hold us, to be gathered by it. The three tanks sunk in the floor, the black water in them with the skin on it. The boiler at the far end, cold, the red stain dried into the concrete under it that we do not, tonight, reach to name. The iron legs of the gutting-table bolted to the floor where the wood rotted off them. The stair going up into the black to the little shut door of the loft, the room inside our own house we could never enter. The salt that cured the fish and ate the iron, the same salt, that kept everything and ate everything — that was the saying, someone's saying, we had it once, we do not reach for it. We go along all of it, and none of it gathers us. It is iron and cold water and salt-rot and a shut door, and it was holding the ones who were used here, and it is not holding them now, because the thing it held them for is out past the pans and will not come back.

We reach to say the house is ours. *This is our house, we are of this house, the house keeps us.* We say it the wet-sand way. And it goes smooth, the way the numbers went smooth, the way forty went smooth over a hole — *this is our house* — and there is nothing under it. It is a shed on the flats. It kept us because we kept the lie, and the lie is out past the pans in a living woman going up a road we cannot follow, and the shed has let go of the thing it was cupped around, and the water comes up into the yard, the flood, the last rising, and stands against the wall of a house that is not holding anyone.

We are fewer than the morning, and fewer than the slack, and the front is gone, and we cannot say the name that was the front's, and we cannot say whose the house is, and the tide comes up another hand's width in the yard and lifts the loose tin and drops it, and there is no one at the front of us to say it is ours.


---

# Chapter 28 — Baixa-mar

The sound of her is on the road going up, and then it is not.

We hold it as long as we can. There is a little water still, the flood come in over the flats and standing, and where there is water there is a little of us, and we put the little of us into the holding of the sound. The engine, running rich, the small rented note of it, going up the road past the last pan into the blind place. We hold it. It thins. It thins the way a thing thins, not slower and not faster than any other thing, and we hold it thinning until there is a place where we are holding and there is nothing in the hold, and then we let the hand come up empty, because there is no use in a cupped hand with nothing in it.

She is gone. She is out past the reach of us now, on the road to the city, and we do not have her. We do not make up the road. We tried, the front tried, to say she would stop and she would turn and she would come back into the water that was hers, and the front is gone now and there is no one to say it. She is on the road. That is all we have of her: that she was on the road, going, and then the sound of the going went out.

*

There are two of us.

We come to this slowly, the way you come to the bottom of a stair in the dark, a step where you think there is another step and there is not, and your foot goes down onto the flat. We go to be the whole of us and there is not the whole of us. There is a place where the front was, cold, with no name over it. There is a place where the funny one was, cooling. There is the neap woman, and Rosa, and the ones before them, and the autumn of the flu-dead who went out on a tide we did not watch, and the marnotas, and the sharp one whose name we reached for on the beam and did not find. There is all of that, which is to say there is none of it, which is to say those are places and not voices, and a place does not lean.

Two of us lean.

One is Graça. She is nearest what she was of any of us, because she is the newest, because there is still a living hand that carried her name a year ago, and the hand is on a road going up out of the water and cannot carry it much longer. She is thin now. She was the least thin and she is thin now, from the leaning at the tape, from the girl going, from the water going down. But she leans. She is one of the two.

The other is small.

We do not say the name. We reach for it — a child, one of the two children, the one who had the other half of the boy, the one who could not, at the last, hold the boy's name and let the shoe be a shoe — and the name comes partway and stops, the way the water stops at the wall. It is a girl's name. It has three sounds in it, or it has more, and the middle of it is soft, and we had it, we had it in the good years when there were two children and a shoe with a name on it up in the beams. We do not have it now. We have that she is small, and that she is one of the two, and that she leans the way a child leans, all at once and toward the warmest thing, and there is no warmest thing now, so she leans toward Graça, who is not warm, who is only near.

So there are two of us. Graça, and the small one whose name will not come. The two of us on the beams, in the low water, with the flood already turning back down toward the baixa, the low, the going-out.

*

The tide goes out of the yard.

It came up when she was on the road — the flood, the last rising, we said — and it stood against the wall, and now it turns and goes back down the way it always goes, without being asked, and takes the water out from under the pans and the flats and out from under the beams of us, and where the water goes out we are less. We feel it go. It is not like the going of a voice. It is slower and it is duller and it does not have a name on it. The water goes down off the black mud and the mud comes up shining and then not shining, the *lodo*, the low-tide mud that smells of the whole lagoon, and the pans stand in it with their little walls, and the last salt-pan stands at the edge of the reach of us with nothing beyond it we can hold.

The two of us go along the beams. There is not much to go along now. We used to go the whole shed, from the yard-end where the boats brought the baskets up, along the tables, to the boiler at the far end with the stain under it, and up as far as the shut door of the loft that we could never go through. We do the little of it we can. The tanks, the three of them, sunk in the floor with the black water in them, the skin on the water. We go to the tanks because the tanks are where the sound gathered, and we are made of sound, and we go to the place where the sound of us was loudest, out of the old habit of gathering, and the tanks do not gather us. The house has stopped holding. We felt it stop, last water, when the front went. It is a shed. We are in it the way the wind is in it.

*

In the room over the café the machine is playing.

We have this. It is at the far edge of what we can reach, over the dry square where the water does not run, and there are only two of us, so we should not be able to reach it at all — but the machine makes a sound, and a sound is the thing we can still take even at the edge, even thinned, the last thing to go. She left it. She packed the black recorder with the fur over the end of it, the live one, the one that takes the sound so it need not be remembered, and she took that in the car; but the other, the old flat one, the player, she left on the little table by the window, and there is a tape in it, and the tape is running.

It is Graça's voice.

She found it in the last days, the girl, in a drawer or a box, an old cassette with a hand's writing on the paper of it, and she put it in the flat machine to hear whether the machine still worked, and it worked, and she left it going when she went. Or she left it going on purpose. We do not know which. We do not make it up. It plays into the empty room over the café, to the shutter and the little table and the bed she lay on in her clothes and did not sleep, and there is no one in the room to hear it, and it plays.

*Don't forget the man is coming Thursday about the roof. And there's soup, I made too much again. Come Sunday if you can. Don't drive tired.*

That is the voice. A living voice, from before, from when Graça was a hand that made too much soup and a mouth that said *don't drive tired* into a telephone that recorded it because the girl was not there to answer. It is warm. It is the warmth Graça had in life and does not have now, cannot have now, because the warmth is a thing of the body and she has no body, and the machine has the warmth and Graça does not.

Graça leans at it.

We feel her lean. She goes to the edge of us, over the dry square, where it costs her, and she leans at the voice coming off the tape, at the warm living self of her saying *come Sunday if you can,* and she reaches into it the way you reach into a warm room from the cold of the yard, and there is nothing in it for her to hold. It is not her. It is the shape of her, fixed, going round and round on the tape, the same words in the same order, and it does not know her, and it does not lean back, and it takes nothing off her and gives nothing to her — it only shows her the self she cannot be, warm, whole, saying the soup and the roof and the Sunday, and she comes back off the edge thinner than she went, a hand's-width thinner, robbed, the way she came back robbed the last time she leaned at it, and the time before.

We say to her, the small one and the little of us that is left — *don't. It is not you. It takes and gives nothing back.* We do not say it in words. We do not have the words for it and it is against the thing we are, we who go again and again to the sound of ourselves to keep it. But we have watched her come back thin three times now and we would have her stop, and she does not stop. She leans at the warm dead loop of herself and is robbed, and leans again.

*

The front is not here to say what Graça wanted.

That is a thing we mark, the two of us, in the low water. The front used to say it. When the girl played the tape the first time, in the last days, the front leaned over the top of the voice and said *hear how she calls to her, she wanted her home, she wanted her to stay* — the front made the tape into a rope to hold the girl with, *come Sunday, come home, stay* — and Graça, even then, even thinning, put down one plain stone against it. *No. Not home. Out.* She said it flat, the way the book is flat, the way the true things come now, with no warmth on them. And the front said *home* and Graça said *out* and there was no one to say which, and both stood, a mother who wanted her daughter home and a mother who wanted her daughter out, and we could not make them the same.

The front is gone now. There is no one to say *home*. There is only Graça, thin, at the edge, leaning at the tape that says *come Sunday if you can* in the warm voice she cannot have, and knowing — the flat cold knowing, the only kind left — that she did not want the girl to come Sunday. She wanted the girl on the road. She wanted the sound of the small rented engine going up past the last pan into the blind place and not stopping. She got it. The girl is on the road and does not stop, and the tape says *come Sunday,* and the two things are not the same, and there is no front to make them the same, and Graça holds the true one, the flat one, the *out,* and it is the last thing she holds that is her own, and it costs her to hold it, and she holds it.

She does not say it to the girl. She cannot. The girl is past the pans. The girl will never have it — that the mother wanted her gone, wanted her free of the soup and the roof and the shed and the drowned, wanted her out and going and not looking back. The girl will carry the tape, if she carries it, as *come Sunday.* She will carry it warm and wrong. That is the one that will keep. The true one, the *out,* is here, in the low water, in a thinning voice with no body and no hand to carry it, and it will go out with her, and no one will have it, and the tape will go on saying *come Sunday* to the empty room over the café until the tape runs out.

*

The small one leans away.

We feel it start, the two of us, which is to say Graça feels it, because Graça is the near one and the small one leans toward the near one and then does not. She leans, all at once, toward the warm thing, the way a child leans, and there is no warm thing, and the leaning does not catch on anything, and a leaning that catches on nothing is a hand coming off a rope. She goes light. It is the wrong-shaped lightness, the going-light we know now, we have felt it four times, five, we have stopped counting the times the way we stopped counting the number. She goes light the way a child goes light, always quicker, less to hold a child, fewer living who kept the small name, and the small name is already gone from us — we reached for it and it did not come — and a name gone is most of the way to the thing gone.

She is not through the door yet. She is at it. She came to it the way they all come to it, and she has stopped there, at the edge of the going, small, the last child, leaning out past the near one toward the warm loop of the tape she cannot reach and cannot hold, and Graça leans back toward her to keep her, the way you lean toward a child on a stair, and there is not enough of Graça to keep anything, and the small one hangs at the door, going.

We want to say her name over it, to keep her the moment longer. The old way, the wet-sand way. We reach for it. Three sounds, or more, a soft middle. It is not there. We had it in the good years, when there were two children and a shoe with the name on it in the beams.
